<a id="paper-2606.04761"></a>
### ⭐ [Comment on "Possibility of superradiant neutrino emission by atomic condensate" by M. Blasone, L. Gastaldo and F. Romeo, Phys. Rev. D 113, 053010 (2026)](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04761v1)

**Highlighted author(s):** Wolfgang Ketterle  
**Authors:** Wolfgang Ketterle, Hanzhen Lin, Yu-Kun Lu  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum gases · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.04761v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Dicke superradiance` **3/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **3/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **1/5** · `free-space correlated emission` **1/5** · `superradiant laser` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.04761_figures/2606.04761_page2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 2</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This theoretical paper proves that superradiant neutrino emission from atomic condensates is fundamentally impossible. The authors demonstrate that fermionic anticommutators cause destructive interference, preventing the emission rate from scaling as $N^2$. This result places a strict theoretical limit on collective decay processes in quantum gases.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a rigorous theoretical constraint on collective emission processes in ultracold atomic systems, specifically ruling out superradiant enhancement in neutrino emission, which is crucial for understanding decay dynamics in BECs.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the theoretical proposal of superradiant neutrino emission from atomic Bose-Einstein condensates (BECs).

**Main result.** The authors prove that superradiant neutrino emission is fundamentally impossible due to the nature of fermionic anticommutators, which cause interference terms to cancel.

**Method.** The analysis involves deriving fundamental limits on the emission rate using quantum mechanical operators (jump operators) and analyzing the scaling of the emission rate with the number of particles, N.

**Model / system.** The system under study is neutrino emission from atomic BECs, considering various models including bosonic-bosonic, bosonic-fermionic, and fermionic-to-bosonic transitions.

**Key observables.** Neutrino emission rate, superradiant enhancement factor, and the scaling of the emission rate with particle number (N).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The rate is found to scale as $\propto N$ rather than the superradiant $\propto N^2$, and kinetic effects suppress any enhancement by factors like $10^{17}$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes the fundamental fermionic nature of the amplitudes and accounts for the rapid decay and recoil of reaction products.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically critiques the models proposed in the cited work [1] (electron capture, bosonic-bosonic, etc.), demonstrating flaws in the underlying physics or mathematical formalism for each case.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We show that the recent proposal for superradiant emission of neutrinos cannot evade our proof that superradiant neutrino emission is fundamentally impossible. Pairing two fermions in a molecule does not remove the cancellation of interference terms in neutrino emission due to fermionic anticommutators.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2606.04926"></a>
### [Adiabatic Ramp Dynamics Across the ETH--MBL Transition in Disordered XXZ Spin Chain](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04926v1)

**Authors:** Nidhi Kumari, Vinod Ashokan  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.04926v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `entanglement & information structure` **4/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **3/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **2/5** · `exotic spin models in light-matter` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5** · `scars & prethermalization` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.04926_figures/2606.04926_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Disorder-averaged adjacent-gap ratio r versus disor- der strength for system sizes L = 10, 12, and 14.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2606.04926_figures/2606.04926_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. Dependence of the diagonal entropy density, sd(tf), on the normalized ramp rate, v/Jzz(0), for the disordered XXZ spin chain. Panels in the left and right columns correspond to the ETH and MBL regimes, respectively. The rows represent linear, quadratic, and exponential ramp protocols from top to bottom. Results are shown for system sizes L = 10, 12, and 14 to illustrate finite-size dependence and the influence of different driving protocols on entropy generation during nonequilibrium dynamics.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2606.04926_figures/2606.04926_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. Behavior of the entanglement entropy density, sent(tf), with normalized ramp rate, v/Jzz(0), in the disordered XXZ chain. Left and right columns show results in the ETH and MBL regimes, respectively, while the rows correspond to linear, quadratic, and exponential driving protocols. Data for system sizes L = 10, 12, and 14 are included to examine how system size and ramp protocol affect entanglement growth generation during nonequilibrium evolution.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2606.04926_figures/2606.04926_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4. Diagonal entropy density sd(tf) and entanglement entropy density sent(tf) for ETH and MBL phase. Panel (a)-(c) for L = 10, L = 12 and L = 14.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This work examines how non-equilibrium dynamics differ between ergodic (ETH) and Many-Body Localized (MBL) quantum phases in a disordered spin chain. By adiabatically ramping the system parameters, the authors use entropy measures to show that the rate of excitation generation is strongly suppressed in the localized phase compared to the thermalizing phase. This confirms that entropy production serves as a powerful diagnostic tool for probing quantum thermalization dynamics.

**Why it may be interesting.** The use of entropy measures to diagnose non-equilibrium dynamics across a fundamental quantum phase transition (ETH-MBL) is highly relevant for understanding how quantum information spreads or is suppressed in interacting many-body systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates non-equilibrium dynamics across the transition between ergodic (ETH) and Many-Body Localized (MBL) quantum phases.

**Main result.** The entropy production (diagonal and entanglement) is significantly higher and more dependent on the driving rate in the ergodic phase compared to the MBL phase, confirming the dynamical distinction between the two regimes.

**Method.** The authors employed exact diagonalization and real-time numerical evolution to simulate the system's time evolution under time-dependent Hamiltonian ramps.

**Model / system.** The model is a disordered spin-1/2 XXZ chain, described by a Hamiltonian with disorder terms ($h_j$) and a time-dependent interaction coupling ($J_{zz}(t)$). The system is analyzed in the zero-magnetization sector.

**Key observables.** Diagonal entropy density, entanglement entropy density, and the adjacent-gap ratio (used to characterize static spectral statistics).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Disorder strength ($h_0$), system size ($L \le 14$), and the ramp speed ($v$) characterizing the adiabaticity of the time-dependent drive.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis is limited by finite-size effects, though the results show robustness across multiple system sizes. The study assumes the validity of the ETH/MBL framework for characterizing thermalization.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the disorder-averaged adjacent-gap ratio transitioning from GOE (ergodic) to Poisson (localized) statistics, and show entropy measures ($	ext{sd}, 	ext{sent}$) varying with ramp speed for different protocols.

**Paper structure.** The paper first establishes the static ETH-MBL crossover using spectral statistics. It then analyzes the non-equilibrium dynamics by applying three different time-dependent driving protocols (linear, quadratic, exponential) and comparing the resulting entropy growth in the ergodic versus localized regimes.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Many-body localization(MBL) provides a mechanism by which isolated interacting quantum systems with disorder can avoid thermalization unlike ergodic systems satisfying the eigenstate thermalization hypothesis(ETH). Many-body localized systems retain signatures of their initial conditions at long times, whereas systems obeying ETH lose such information as they approach thermal equilibrium. Studying Non-equilibrium dynamics across ETH-MBL crossover is an important problem in condensed matter physics. Adiabatic control of parameters in interacting disordered systems provides a powerful framework to investigate MBL phases and their dynamical robustness. Using exact diagonalization and time-dependent numerical methods we study the effects of adiabatically ramped interactions in a disordered spin-1/2 XXZ chain, a paradigmatic model for exploring the many-body localization transition. By monitoring diagonal entropy density and entanglement entropy density growth across various ramp speeds, and system sizes. Our study incorporates Finite-size effects of spectral observables to probe the transition between ergodic and localized phases. The numerical results show that localized dynamical behavior remains largely intact under sufficiently slow ramp evolution, while increasing the driving rate promotes stronger excitation generation and larger entropy growth. This trend highlights the strong dependence of nonequilibrium adiabatic dynamics in disordered interacting quantum many-body systems.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2606.05033"></a>
### [Crossover from Rabi oscillations to adiabatic population switching in the Faraday optical control of quantum dot spins](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.05033v1)

**Authors:** Jan M. Kaspari, Zhe Xian Koong, Dorian A. Gangloff, Michał Gawełczyk, Doris E. Reiter  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.05033v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `spintronics-quantum-optics interface` **4/5** · `exotic spin models in light-matter` **3/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **2/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **1/5** · `interference shaping light` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.05033_figures/2606.05033_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Energy level diagrams of the spin system in a) Voigt geometry and b) in Faraday geometry, including the spin states and the optical transitions indicated by arrows.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2606.05033_figures/2606.05033_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. Population dynamics of spin states for 𝑛= 1 in the balanced case 𝜂= 1 with Ω = 23.87 GHz and ℏ𝛿= 10.75 µeV (2.6 GHz).</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2606.05033_figures/2606.05033_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. Population dynamics of the spin states for Ω = 23.87 GHz for a) 𝑛= 1 with ℏ𝛿= 8.79 µeV (2.13 GHz) and b) 𝑛= 3 with ℏ𝛿= 2.93 µeV (0.71 GHz). c) Period of the oscillations (red) and the maximal achievable population inversion (blue) for orders 𝑛= 1, . . . , 5. The lines are just a guide to the eye.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2606.05033_figures/2606.05033_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4. Population dynamics of spin states (top panels) and instantaneous diabatic energy difference ℏ𝜔diff (bottom) for 𝑛= 0 at a) diabatic limit: ℏ𝛿= 17.11 µeV (4.14 GHz), b) intermediate step-like dynamics: ℏ𝛿= 0.39 µeV (0.10 GHz), and c) adiabatic limit: ℏ𝛿= 19.75 neV (4.77 MHz).</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper explores how to control spin dynamics in quantum dot qubits using optical fields in Faraday geometry. The authors demonstrate a novel mechanism where changing the laser detuning beatnote allows a controlled crossover from standard Rabi oscillations to highly efficient, step-like adiabatic population switching. This establishes the oscillating Stark shift as a powerful tool for engineering spin resonance conditions.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a detailed theoretical framework for using time-dependent, engineered couplings (the oscillating Stark shift) to precisely control quantum state transfer in solid-state spin qubits, a key area for quantum computing.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates the controlled transition between Rabi-like coherent oscillations and adiabatic population switching when controlling quantum dot spins using Faraday geometry optical fields.

**Main result.** By tuning the detuning beatnote, the system can be engineered to exhibit a controlled crossover from Rabi oscillations to step-like population inversion via Landau-Zener-Stückelberg interference.

**Method.** The dynamics are analyzed by solving the Liouville-von Neumann equation for the density matrix, comparing results from the full three-level model with effective two-level spin models.

**Model / system.** The system is a singly charged quantum dot spin coupled to negative trion states, modeled as a three-level $\Lambda$ system driven by Raman laser fields in Faraday geometry. The dynamics are governed by a time-dependent Hamiltonian.

**Key observables.** Population dynamics of spin states, coherent rotations, population inversion, and the differential AC Stark shift.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Detuning beatnote ($\delta$), imbalance ($\eta$), detuning ($\Delta$), and Zeeman splitting ($\omega_e$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on the dipole and rotating wave approximations, and the effective model assumes $\Delta \gg \Omega$.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate energy level diagrams comparing Voigt and Faraday geometries, and show population dynamics for different harmonic orders ($n=1, 3$) and the $n=0$ case, demonstrating the crossover.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from establishing the physical setup and Hamiltonian to deriving effective models, analyzing resonance conditions for different harmonic orders ($n$), and finally demonstrating the controlled crossover from Rabi to adiabatic switching in the $n=0$ case.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Stimulated Raman transitions in Faraday geometry allow for simultaneous single-shot qubit readout and qubit control. It involves driving an unbalanced $Λ$ system via an auxiliary excited state. Due to the simultaneous driving of both transitions with unequal detuning, the resulting time-dependent Stark shift gives rise to additional resonance conditions beyond the conventional picture. We identify a distinct regime in which repeated passages through avoided crossings lead to step-like population inversion arising from Landau-Zener-Stückelberg interference. By changing the detuning beatnote, we demonstrate a controlled continuous crossover from Rabi-like oscillations to adiabatic population switching. These findings establish the oscillating Stark shift as a mechanism for engineering and controlling spin dynamics in Faraday geometry.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2606.04674"></a>
### [Hydrodynamics of the Fermi-Pasta-Ulam-Tsingou chain](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04674v1)

**Authors:** Shubhadeep Chakraborty, Abhishek Dhar  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.04674v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `non-equilibrium dynamics` **4/5** · `Frenkel-Kontorova` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **3/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **2/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `scars & prethermalization` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.04674_figures/2606.04674_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 1: Current-correlators with the theoretical Drude weight values subtracted for N = 1000, β = 1, P = 2, v = 0. Left: Γ ee(t) −Γ ee(∞) (purple) with exponential fit (orange); inset shows the same on a semilog scale. Right: Γ vv(t) −Γ vv(∞).</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2606.04674_figures/2606.04674_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 2: Comparison of the Euler solution and microscopic simulations for an unequal-pressure domain wall. The initial states are uL = (rL,0,eL) and uR = (rR,0,eR), with PL ≠PR. The top panel shows the unscaled plots while the middle panel shows the same data with ballistic scaling. In the middle panel, the colored lines show ensemble averaged microscopic data plotted against the similarity variable X/t, while the solid lines show the Euler prediction obtained from the Riemann solution construction. The lower panel shows X/t2/3 scaling and we see somewhat better data collapse near the centre. The plots here are constructed after averaging 5 × 104 samples with βL = 1, βR = 2, PL = 1 and PR =...</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2606.04674_figures/2606.04674_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 2 shows the evolution of the hydrodynamic fields with time and the comparison with Euler</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2606.04674_figures/2606.04674_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 3: Evolution of an equal-pressure domain wall with βL = 1, βR = 2, P = 0 and v = 0, with 105 samples averaged. The top panel shows the unscaled plots and the middle panel shows that the best collapse for the central regions is by X/t2/3. There are moving spikes in the profiles that scale ballistically as shown in the bottom panel.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2606.04674_figures/2606.04674_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 4: Case A: Blast initial condition with Pa ≠0, ra ≠0, ca ≠0 and (k2,k3,k4) = (1,1,1). The top panel shows the time evolution of the profiles while the bottom panel shows results after a ballistic collapse.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper provides a comprehensive review of applying hydrodynamic theory to the FPUT chain, deriving both Euler and dissipative equations. It tests these theories against non-equilibrium initial conditions like domain walls and blast waves. The findings demonstrate that while hydrodynamics works well for some regimes, dissipation and noise are essential for accurately describing the system's far-from-equilibrium evolution.

**Why it may be interesting.** The study provides a rigorous test case for the crossover between microscopic Hamiltonian dynamics and continuum fluid descriptions, which is a central theme in non-equilibrium statistical mechanics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper reviews the applicability and derivation of hydrodynamic descriptions for the Fermi-Pasta-Ulam-Tsingou (FPUT) chain, especially when analyzing far-from-equilibrium phenomena.

**Main result.** While the hydrodynamic approach successfully recovers equilibrium transport coefficients like the diffusion matrix, simulations show that for certain non-equilibrium states (like equal-pressure domain walls), deviations from Euler predictions highlight the crucial role of dissipation and noise.

**Method.** The authors employ a combination of theoretical derivations (Green-Kubo type methods, characteristic analysis of Euler equations) and comparison with molecular dynamics simulations.

**Model / system.** The system is the FPUT chain, modeled by particles interacting via a nonlinear potential $V(r) = k_2/r^2 + k_3/r^3 + k_4/r^4$. The analysis transitions between microscopic Hamiltonian dynamics and macroscopic continuum fluid descriptions.

**Key observables.** Mass density ($ho$), momentum density, energy density, current-current correlators ($\Gamma_{\alphaeta}(t)$), and wave structures (shocks, rarefactions).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The degree of nonlinearity in the potential, the ambient pressure ($P_a$), and the scaling exponents (e.g., $t^{2/3}$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The primary assumption is the validity of local equilibrium for deriving the hydrodynamic equations, which is tested against non-equilibrium initial conditions.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare the theoretical three-wave structure predicted by Euler theory against microscopic simulations for domain walls, and show the collapse of central regions for blast waves, contrasting with the expected TvNS scaling.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first defining the Hamiltonian, then deriving the full set of hydrodynamic equations (Euler and NSF), followed by applying these equations to solve specific non-equilibrium problems like domain walls and blast waves.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We provide a pedagogical review of the hydrodynamics of the FPUT chain. There are three hydrodynamic fields corresponding to the conservation of mass, momentum and energy. We provide physically motivated derivations of the hydrodynamic equations at the levels of Euler and then Navier-Stokes-Fourier. Next we consider examples to test as to how successful the hydrodynamic description is in predicting the observed time evolution of nonequilibrium initial conditions such as domain walls and blasts. We find that in some cases there is good agreement of microscopic simulations with predictions from the Euler equations while, in several other cases, there is significant departure from the Euler predictions suggesting that the role of dissipation and noise is important in general.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2606.04794"></a>
### [Monitored chaotic scattering](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04794v1)

**Authors:** C. W. J. Beenakker, J. Sánchez Fernán, J. Tworzydło  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.04794v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `measurement-induced transitions` **4/5** · `quantum measurements` **3/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **1/5**

**Summary.** This paper develops a theoretical framework to study quantum transport in chaotic scattering systems when the system is continuously monitored by an external measurement. It derives a quantum master equation governing the evolution and uses Random Matrix Theory to predict the statistics of the transferred charge. The key finding is that monitoring breaks transport reciprocity, providing new diagnostics for open quantum systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant to AMO and open quantum systems as it provides a rigorous theoretical framework for analyzing non-unitary, measurement-induced dynamics in mesoscopic conductors, extending concepts from quantum chaos to open quantum systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper extends random-matrix theory to analyze quantum transport through chaotic scattering systems when the dynamics are subjected to continuous, time-resolved monitoring.

**Main result.** Monitoring breaks the reciprocity of transport measurements, and the resulting charge transfer statistics can be predicted by comparing numerical solutions of a quantum master equation against Random Matrix Theory conjectures.

**Method.** The analysis involves deriving and solving a discrete-time quantum master equation for the density matrix, followed by applying Random Matrix Theory techniques (CUE/COE ensembles) to calculate probability distributions for transferred charge.

**Model / system.** The model is a quantum dot coupled to leads, where the system's evolution is governed by a quantum channel defined by Kraus operators due to the monitoring process. The scattering is statistically modeled using the Circular Unitary Ensemble (CUE).

**Key observables.** Transferred charge ($T$), differential conductance ($G = dI/dV$), and the probability density functions $P(T)$ and $P(T')$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Weak measurement strength ($w_m$), the ratio of lead modes ($M$ vs $N$), and the fixed parameter $\gamma = Mw^2$ in the multi-mode limit.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes a weak measurement strength and relies on conjectures like the 'equipartition rule' for RMT predictions.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the setup (open/closed leads), two-terminal transport configurations, and the probability density functions $P(T)$ and $P(T')$ for both single-mode and multi-mode monitoring under CUE and COE statistics.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from defining the monitored evolution via Kraus operators to deriving the master equation. It then compares numerical solutions for charge transfer statistics ($T$) against predictions from Random Matrix Theory, culminating in generalized equipartition rules for multi-mode monitoring.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We extend the random-matrix theory of chaotic scattering to quantum dots whose dynamics is monitored by time-resolved measurements. Starting from a scattering matrix drawn from a circular ensemble, we construct the corresponding ensemble of Kraus operators for the monitored evolution of the many-body density matrix. In the single-particle sector the sum over measurement outcomes can be carried out algebraically, giving a discrete-time quantum master equation for the transferred charge. We solve this equation numerically and compare the resulting charge-transfer statistics with closed-form random-matrix predictions. The latter rely on an equipartition rule for monitored particles, which we formulate as a conjecture and test against the master equation.

</details>

**Summary.** The researchers used a Bose-Einstein condensate on an AtomChip to simulate the dynamics of an Inverted Harmonic Oscillator (IHO), which models unstable quantum evolution. They measured sub-vacuum squeezing and confirmed that the quantum state retains coherence even after undergoing rapid, amplifying expansion. This work establishes ultra-cold atoms as a powerful tool for studying fundamental quantum phenomena relevant to cosmology and quantum sensing.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a highly controlled, many-body quantum simulator for fundamental processes like cosmological inflation, offering quantitative measurements of quantum noise amplification and coherence preservation in an ultracold atomic setting.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper experimentally investigates the quantum dynamics governed by the Inverted Harmonic Oscillator (IHO), a model for unstable quantum evolution, using ultra-cold atoms.

**Main result.** They successfully demonstrated sub-vacuum squeezing and confirmed the coherent reversibility of the IHO dynamics using a Bose-Einstein condensate, establishing a clean platform for studying quantum fluctuations.

**Method.** The dynamics are probed using phase-space tomography on the Wigner function, and coherence is verified via matter-wave interference and time-reversal protocols.

**Model / system.** The system is a Bose-Einstein condensate (BEC) trapped on an AtomChip, where the transverse confinement is dynamically quenched via RF dressing to approximate an IHO potential. The dynamics are analyzed using the Gross-Pitaevskii equation (GPE) in the many-body limit.

**Key observables.** Sub-vacuum squeezing (up to 10.6 dB), Wigner function evolution, matter-wave interference contrast, and the decay/restoration of quantum purity.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Effective IHO frequency ($\omega_I = 2\pi 	imes 0.9$ kHz), initial transverse confinement ($\omega_\perp = 2\pi 	imes 2.1$ kHz), and time-of-flight measurement times ($t_E$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The single-mode IHO description is assumed accurate for short times, though the experiment probes regimes where interaction-induced mode coupling becomes significant.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the experimental setup (RF quench), the time evolution sequence including time-reversal (loop protocol), and the resulting matter-wave interference patterns and momentum distributions used for tomography.

**Paper structure.** The paper follows a structure of realizing the IHO potential via RF dressing, characterizing the resulting quantum state using Wigner tomography, demonstrating the exponential amplification and squeezing, and finally testing the coherence and reversibility via a time-reversal loop protocol.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

When a quantum system passes through an unstable fixed point the local dynamics reduces to the inverted harmonic oscillator (IHO). It exponentially amplifies along one quadrature while squeezing the other, producing macroscopically extended quantum states from microscopic zero-point fluctuations. We realize this dynamics with a Bose-Einstein condensate on an AtomChip. Radio-frequency dressing flips the transverse harmonic confinement into an IHO. Through phase-space tomography we follow the full Wigner function of the evolving quantum state, observe sub-vacuum squeezing of 10.6(1.3) dB, and test coherent reversibility by time-reversing the IHO evolution. Matter-wave interference between the two daughter clouds confirms quantum coherence over timescales far beyond the initial expansion. Our experiment establishes ultra-cold atoms as a clean, controlled, many-body platform for unstable quantum dynamics opening a route to force sensing with time-reversal-based coherence certification and to analog studies of the amplification of quantum fluctuations in inflationary field dynamics.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.05010"></a>
### [Measurement-induced state transitions in multi-qubit transmon processors](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.05010v1)

**Authors:** Baptiste Hoyau, Alexander McDonald, Boris M. Varbanov, Manuel H. Muñoz-Arias, Alexandre Blais  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.05010v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `measurement-induced transitions` **4/5** · `quantum measurements` **2/5** · `QC/QI experiment` **1/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **1/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `correlated cavity matter` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **1/5**

**Summary.** This paper develops a general theoretical framework to analyze Measurement-Induced State Transitions (MIST) in complex multi-qubit transmon processors. By comparing different adiabatic path traversals, the authors quantify how neighboring qubits and tunable couplers alter the critical photon number required for these detrimental transitions. This provides crucial guidelines for designing high-fidelity quantum readout protocols.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant to AMO physics and open quantum systems as it tackles decoherence and leakage mechanisms (MIST) in superconducting circuits, which are central to building scalable quantum hardware.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates how Measurement-Induced State Transitions (MIST) affect transmon qubits when they are embedded within complex multi-qubit quantum processors.

**Main result.** The presence of spectator qubits and tunable couplers significantly modifies the MIST threshold, sometimes leading to suppression or cancellation of these unwanted transitions.

**Method.** The authors employ advanced Floquet branch analysis, comparing 'coupling-first' and 'drive-first' labeling schemes to detect discrepancies indicative of spectator-induced avoided crossings.

**Model / system.** The system models multi-qubit transmon processors in circuit QED, involving a readout qubit, spectator qubits, and tunable couplers, described by a complex Hamiltonian.

**Key observables.** Critical photon number ($n_{	ext{spec}}^{	ext{crit}}$), average qubit and spectator populations ($\langle\hat{N}_qangle, \langle\hat{N}_sangle$), and quasienergies.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Measurement drive amplitude ($E_q$), detunings ($\Delta_{qs}$), and coupling strengths ($g_{qs}, J_{qc}$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on semi-classical approximations and the comparison between different adiabatic/diabatic path traversals (CF vs. DF labeling).

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the comparison of average populations and quasienergies between coupling-first and drive-first paths, mapping $n_{	ext{spec}}^{	ext{crit}}$ as a function of detuning and coupling strength, and showing the effect of couplers on avoided crossings.

**Paper structure.** The paper builds from single-qubit MIST analysis to a general method for multi-mode systems, then applies this method to study the specific effects of spectator qubits, and finally incorporates tunable couplers to analyze mitigation strategies.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Dispersive readout of the transmon qubit in circuit QED is known to lose its quantum non-demolition character at small to moderate measurement drive amplitudes. This phenomenon is understood to originate from Laundau-Zener transitions at accidental multi-photon resonances, where $n$ drive photons can promote the transmon by $m$ levels. This interpretation has been shown to be in agreement with experiments characterizing the dispersive readout of a single transmon. The impact of these measurement-induced state transition (MIST) of a transmon embedded in a multi-qubit chip, however, remains largely unexplored. Here, we show that the presence of other components, such as qubits and couplers, can affect the MIST threshold of a measured transmon. To arrive at these results, we present a general method to characterize measurement-induced transition when the qubit under readout is coupled to other circuit elements, a ubiquitous situation in circuit QED-based quantum processors. As an example, we consider the case of two transmon qubits, and we show that the spectator qubit can be impacted by the measurement-induced transition of the readout qubit and, conversely, that the presence of the spectator qubit can lower the MIST threshold of the readout qubit. Finally, we explore how adding a coupler mode between the two qubits further modifies these effects.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.04771"></a>
### [Pushing the Classical Frontier of 1D Fermi-Hubbard Quench Dynamics Beyond Current Quantum Simulations](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04771v1)

**Authors:** Roman Rausch, Sukhbinder Singh, Saeed S. Jahromi, Augustine Kshetrimayum, Roman Orus  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.04771v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `non-equilibrium dynamics` **4/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **3/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **1/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.04771_figures/2606.04771_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1: Main results. (a) TDVP time evolution of ⟨n46↑⟩up to t = 8 for L = 60 fermions with ϵtol = 10−4. Our U(1)×U(1) curve at χ = 4096 reproduces the Q-CTRL iTensor calculation and confirms non-convergence beyond t ≈5. Our U(1)×SU(2) TDVP with χ ≈30,000 achieves convergence in t ∈[5.2, 6] (shaded blue) and our results are reliable in t ∈[6, 7] (shaded green), extending beyond the quantum simulation window. (b) Absolute deviation of ⟨n46↑⟩from the χ ≈62,000 reference at each bond dimension. (c) RMSE of our TDVP data against Q-CTRL’s published values of ⟨n20↑⟩, ⟨n29↑⟩, and ⟨n40↑⟩. (d) Wall-clock runtimes: ITensor TDVP on CPU cluster (1) vs. our GPU implementation (same legend as (a)).</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2606.04771_figures/2606.04771_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2: Classical benchmarks for the Q-CTRL quantum simulation results of Fig. 3 in Ref. (1). (a) Expectation value ⟨ni↑⟩(up-spin density at site i) as a function of evolution time t, computed with our U(1) × SU(2) TDVP at χ ≈62,000 (float32, ϵtol = 10−4). Results already converged for t ≤6 using χ = 30, 000 (Fig S5). (b)–(d) Site-resolved values at i = 20, 29, 40 compared directly with the Q-CTRL quantum simulation results.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper pushes the classical simulation frontier for the 1D Fermi-Hubbard model by implementing a highly optimized, GPU-accelerated TDVP algorithm that exploits the full U(1)xSU(2) symmetry. The authors successfully certify the dynamics in previously inaccessible, high-entanglement regimes and extend the simulation time beyond current quantum hardware limits. This work significantly narrows the quantum-classical performance gap, establishing a new standard for benchmarking large-scale quantum simulations.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work directly addresses the computational boundary between quantum and classical simulation for strongly correlated systems, providing a crucial, highly optimized classical benchmark that forces a re-evaluation of quantum advantage claims in condensed matter physics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To rigorously establish the true quantum-classical performance gap for simulating quench dynamics in the 1D Fermi-Hubbard model, especially in highly entangled regimes.

**Main result.** The authors significantly advance classical simulation by exploiting full U(1)xSU(2) symmetry and GPU acceleration, narrowing the claimed quantum advantage from 3000x to ~36x and certifying dynamics up to t=7.

**Method.** Time-Dependent Variational Principle (TDVP) implemented using Matrix Product States (MPS) and heavily optimized with GPU-accelerated tensor contractions and symmetry exploitation.

**Model / system.** The one-dimensional Fermi-Hubbard model (FHM) is studied under quench dynamics from a half-filled Néel state. The Hamiltonian is $H = -t \sum_{\langle i,jangle,\sigma} (c^\dagger_{i\sigma}c_{j\sigma} + h.c.) + U \sum_i n_{i\uparrow}n_{i\downarrow}$.

**Key observables.** Site-resolved spin-up occupations $\langle n_{i\uparrow} angle$, Root-Mean-Square Error (RMSE) relative to quantum simulations, and the evolution of the bond dimension $\chi(t)$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** System size $L=60$, high-entanglement window $t \in[5.2, 6]$, and bond dimensions reaching $\chi \approx 62,000$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The primary approximation is the use of the MPS ansatz within the TDVP framework. The authors assume that the full U(1)xSU(2) symmetry can be fully exploited for computational efficiency.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the convergence of observables (e.g., $\langle n_{46\uparrow} angle$) across time, comparing results from insufficient symmetry ($U(1)	imes U(1)$) versus the full symmetry ($U(1)	imes SU(2)$). Runtime comparisons quantify the speedup achieved by the GPU implementation.

**Paper structure.** The paper first establishes the physical problem and the limitations of previous quantum claims. It then details the advanced classical methodology, focusing on symmetry exploitation and GPU optimization. The core results demonstrate convergence across the high-entanglement regime and extend the simulation time beyond current quantum hardware capabilities.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Establishing quantum advantage requires comparison against the best achievable classical simulation. The Q-CTRL team recently simulated quench dynamics of the one-dimensional Fermi-Hubbard model on an IBM processor, completing a $L=60$ evolution to time $t=6$ in under three minutes and claiming a $3000\times$ speedup over classical Time-Dependent Variational Principle (TDVP) simulation at bond dimension $χ=4096$. Their classical benchmark required over 160 hours on a CPU cluster, failed to converge in the high-entanglement regime $t\in[5.2,6]$, and left the most challenging window of the experiment unverified. Here, we push the boundaries of classical simulation by exploiting the full $\mathrm{U}(1)\times\mathrm{SU}(2)$ symmetry of the Fermi-Hubbard Hamiltonian combined with GPU-accelerated tensor contractions. Reaching bond dimensions up to $χ\approx62{,}000$ on four NVIDIA H200 GPUs -- among the largest ever achieved in TDVP simulations and fifteen times larger than Q-CTRL's classical baseline -- we achieve fully converged results across the entire simulation window, including rigorous certification of the previously unresolved high-entanglement regime $t\in[5.2,6]$. We further advance the classical frontier to $t=7$, which lies beyond the quantum hardware experiment and any previously verified classical evolution of the full wavefunction. At the bond dimension comparable to Q-CTRL's best classical run, our GPU implementation completes in $\sim\!100$ minutes, directly reducing the claimed $3000\times$ quantum advantage to $\sim\!36\times$. These results substantially narrow the quantum-classical performance gap and establish a new standard for tensor-network benchmarking of large-scale quantum simulations.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2606.05164"></a>
### [Floquet-Engineered Parity Anomaly Staircase in a Cold Atom Dirac Lattice](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.05164v1)

**Authors:** Binayyak Roy, Vito Scarola, Sumanta Tewari  
**Type:** both · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.05164v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<sub>Figure 4. Schematic of the chemical potential-induced Hall staircase. The gray plane denotes the chemical potential µ. As µ is swept through the shifted spectrum, the occupied Berry curvature contribution changes from C ≃0 to an intermediate response near C ≃1/2, and then to the next plateau near C ≃1.</sub>

**Summary.** This paper proposes using cold atoms in an optical lattice to engineer a system exhibiting a parity anomaly-inspired anomalous Hall staircase. By combining Floquet driving and auxiliary dressing, the authors create a model where the Hall response can be tuned stepwise by selectively populating different massive Dirac sectors. This offers a powerful, tunable platform for studying topological physics.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a concrete, experimentally accessible platform (cold atoms) to realize and tune topological phenomena like the anomalous Hall effect, which is highly relevant for quantum simulation and topological quantum computing.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper aims to engineer a system to selectively probe the Berry curvature contribution from individual massive Dirac sectors, leading to a quantized anomalous Hall response staircase.

**Main result.** The anomalous Hall response exhibits plateau-like steps near $0$, $e^2/2h$, and $e^2/h$, corresponding to the sequential activation of zero, one, or two effective massive Dirac sectors.

**Method.** The analysis involves calculating the full lattice Berry curvature integral and analyzing the Hall response ($\sigma_{xy}$) under two protocols: sweeping the chemical potential ($\mu$) or sweeping the scalar displacement term ($\Delta v$).

**Model / system.** The system is a cold atom realization in a two-dimensional $\pi$-flux optical lattice. The effective Hamiltonian incorporates time-reversal symmetry breaking (Floquet mass $m_T$), static inversion breaking ($m_I$), and a tunable momentum-dependent scalar displacement ($\Delta v$) via auxiliary AC-Stark dressing.

**Key observables.** Anomalous Hall response ($\sigma_{xy}$), which shows staircase plateaus at quantized values ($0, e^2/2h, e^2/h$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Floquet drive amplitude ($m_T$), static offset ($m_I$), scalar displacement ($\Delta v$), and chemical potential ($\mu$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The scalar displacement term ($\Delta v$) is assumed to shift energies without altering the Bloch eigenvectors, allowing for selective occupation of Berry curvature contributions.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the energy spectrum, the sector-resolved Berry curvature, and the resulting $\sigma_{xy}$ vs. $\mu$ or $\sigma_{xy}$ vs. $\Delta v$ plots, clearly showing the characteristic staircase plateaus.

**Paper structure.** The paper first derives the effective Hamiltonian using Floquet engineering techniques. It then analyzes the low-energy Dirac theory, showing how the scalar term separates the sectors. Finally, it calculates the Hall response by sweeping $\mu$ or $\Delta v$ to demonstrate the stepwise quantization.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We propose a Floquet-engineered cold atom realization of a parity anomaly inspired anomalous Hall staircase in a two dimensional $π$-flux lattice. The effective model hosts massive Dirac fermions generated by the combined action of a time reversal symmetry breaking Floquet mass and a static inversion breaking mass offset. An additional momentum dependent scalar displacement term shifts different Dirac sectors in opposite energy directions without modifying their Bloch eigenvectors. As a result, the Berry curvature contribution associated with individual massive Dirac sectors can be selectively occupied, allowing the anomalous Hall response to evolve stepwise as a function of chemical potential or scalar displacement term. Evaluating the full lattice Berry curvature integral, we find plateau-like responses near $0$, $e^2/2h$, and $e^2/h$, corresponding respectively to the activation of zero, one, and two effective massive Dirac sector contributions. We analyze the associated low energy Dirac theory, band topology, Berry curvature structure, and two parameter response maps, and discuss a possible realization using Raman-assisted tunneling, off-resonant Floquet driving, and auxiliary AC-Stark dressing in ultracold atomic optical lattices.

</details>

### [Phase-correlation-free quantum key distribution source operating at gigahertz rates](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04947v1)

**Summary.** This paper introduces a Super-Luminescent Light Emitting Diode (SLED) as a viable, low-cost source for high-speed Quantum Key Distribution (QKD). It experimentally proves that the SLED inherently randomizes the phase between successive pulses, solving a major security hurdle in QKD. Furthermore, it demonstrates the ability to engineer the necessary intra-signal coherence for time-bin encoding, establishing a scalable platform for next-generation quantum communication.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant to quantum optics and open quantum systems as it proposes a practical, solid-state alternative to complex laser systems for quantum communication, directly addressing decoherence and correlation issues in high-speed quantum protocols.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The primary challenge is developing a secure, high-speed Quantum Key Distribution (QKD) source that avoids phase correlations inherent in conventional laser sources at gigahertz repetition rates.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate that a Super-Luminescent Light Emitting Diode (SLED) can serve as a compact, cost-effective source that achieves intrinsic global phase randomization between adjacent pulses while maintaining high intra-signal coherence necessary for time-bin encoding.

**Method.** The work involves characterizing the SLED source using interferometric measurements (1DLI for phase correlation and 2DLI for intra-signal coherence) and simulating the performance using decoy-state BB84 protocols.

**Model / system.** The physical platform is a C-band SLED operating at high rates (e.g., 1.25 GHz). The source's spontaneous-emission-driven nature provides intrinsic phase randomization, which is combined with active modulation (pulse carving and basis encoding) to generate time-bin states.

**Key observables.** Visibility ($V_{1DLI}$ and $V_{2DLI}$), extinction ratio ($\approx 25$ dB), and the second-order correlation function ($g_2(0)$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Repetition rates up to 1.25 GHz, pulse widths ($\sim 100$ ps), and the effective number of temporal modes ($M$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The security analysis relies on the SLED's ability to mimic the performance of an ideal phase-randomized source, and the performance is compared against theoretical Poissonian limits.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the experimental setup for phase correlation measurements (1DLI) and time-bin signal generation (2DLI), comparing visibility results between the SLED and a reference laser source.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first establishing the need for phase randomization, then presenting the SLED as a solution, followed by detailed measurements confirming both pulse-to-pulse randomization and intra-signal coherence, and concluding with performance comparisons for QKD.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Phase randomization is essential for the security of practical decoy-state quantum key distribution (QKD) systems. Commonly, implementations rely on laser sources which are either actively phase-randomized, or gain-switched. However, at high repetition rates these show correlations, which can ultimately compromise security and performance. We present a 1.25 GHz phase-randomized QKD source based on a super-luminescent light emitting diode (SLED) operating in the C-band as a compact and cost-effective alternative. The source generates $\sim100$ ps optical pulses with $400$ ps pulse-to-pulse separation, compatible with high-speed time-bin encoding. Interferometric measurements demonstrate $>99\%$ visibility between adjacent time bins, confirming strong first-order coherence within the same quantum signals, while the spontaneous-emission-driven nature of the SLED ensures intrinsic global phase randomization between adjacent signals. This work establishes a scalable SLED-based platform for high-speed prepare-and-measure QKD systems.

</details>

**Summary.** This work demonstrates the feasibility of using radio-frequency (rf) reflectometry to rapidly probe the quantum conductance of PbTe-Pb nanowires designed to host Majorana zero modes. By measuring the reflected rf signal, the authors show a direct, fast correlation between the rf signal and the device's quantum conductance. This technique significantly accelerates characterization compared to traditional DC measurements, paving the way for topological quantum computation.

**Why it may be interesting.** The development of fast, high-bandwidth readout techniques like rf reflectometry is crucial for characterizing fragile quantum states, such as those associated with Majorana zero modes, which are central to topological quantum computation.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The primary goal is to demonstrate a fast, non-invasive readout technique for quantum states in superconducting nanowires predicted to host Majorana zero modes.

**Main result.** Radio-frequency (rf) reflectometry proves to be a viable, order-of-magnitude faster method than DC transport for probing the quantum conductance of PbTe-Pb nanowires.

**Method.** The technique involves using a Vector Network Analyzer (VNA) to measure the reflection coefficient ($S_{21}$) of the nanowire circuit as a function of gate voltage and frequency.

**Model / system.** The experiment uses selective-area-grown PbTe-Pb nanowires on a CdTe substrate, which are engineered to host Majorana zero modes. The circuit is modeled as an LCR network, allowing the rf signal to probe the device's quantum conductance.

**Key observables.** The measured observables include the reflection coefficient ($|S_{21}|$) vs. gate voltage and frequency, the device conductance ($G$), and the derived relationship between the rf signal voltage ($V_{rf}$) and $G$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Key parameters include the gate voltage ($V_{SG}$), rf frequency ($f$), and the magnetic field ($B$), which must be compatible with the zero-energy mode signatures.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis makes approximations regarding the circuit model, such as neglecting the bias-tee region for resonance analysis, and attributing small resonance dips to parasitic impedance mismatch.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the rf-readout schematic, gate-tunable reflectometry results showing $|S_{21}|$ vs $V_{SG}$ and $f$, comparisons between rf and dc probing speeds, and measurements demonstrating compatibility with magnetic fields.

**Paper structure.** The paper first introduces the rf-readout system and its compatibility with the nanowire structure. It then presents data showing the gate-tunable response, followed by comparisons to DC measurements and studies of the system's response under applied magnetic fields and capacitive coupling.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We report the implementation of radio-frequency (rf) reflectometry on selective-area-grown PbTe-Pb nanowire devices on a CdTe substrate. These nanowires were predicted to host Majorana zero modes. We demonstrate the compatibility of the rf technique, both resistive and capacitive sensing, with these nanowires. The effect of dielectric loss from the CdTe substrate is also discussed. The feasibility of rf reflectometry is also verified at finite magnetic fields where zero-energy modes occur. Our results enable the fast control of PbTe quantum devices, paving the way for its application in topological quantum computation.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.04086"></a>
### [Quantum String Interactions Revealed by Full Counting Statistics](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04086v1)

**Authors:** Chang-Yan Wang, Xue-Feng Zhang  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.04086v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `entanglement & information structure` **4/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.04086_figures/2606.04086_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Schematic of the hard-core string interaction. (a) Local plaquette flips move two fluctuating strings separated by r, while the hard-core constraint forbids their crossing. The overlap of the blue and red regions marks where the strings can meet and interact. Near contact, the blocked hopping process gives the virtual wall-hopping matrix element, with return channels A1 and A2. Each local segment has two directions, mapped to spin up/down states. (b) In the relative- string coordinate u, the constraint becomes a wall at u = −r. The virtual process connects u = −r and u = −r + 1, so the effective boundary coordinate is centered at u = −r + 1/2.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2606.04086_figures/2606.04086_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. Scaling fit of ln ∆E · ln L using ln ∆E · ln L = −a(r − 1/2)2 + b ln r + c. The dashed line is the fit. Panel (a) shows ED data for L = 10, 12, 14, 16, giving a = 4.974 ± 0.068. Panel (b) shows DMRG data for L = 32, 64, 96, 128, giving a = 4.107 ± 0.156.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2606.04086_figures/2606.04086_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. Comparison of (a,b) ED and (c,d) DMRG energies with FCS estimates. Solid blue curves are the direct finite-size mid-wall FCS integrals. Dashed blue curves for L = 64, 128 include a fitted vertical shift. Orange curves are the hybrid windowed full-wall estimates, with exact one-cut FCS factors in the central window and Gaussian conditional factors for the remaining wall constraint. The inset shows the fitted shift δ(L) ≃0.417 ln L + 0.687 for L = 32, 64, 96, 128.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2606.04086_figures/2606.04086_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4. Small-λ structure of the mid-wall FCS. (a) Exact F (λ) and the Gaussian approximation e−Gℓℓλ2/2. (b) Exact B(λ) and the dotted line marks B(0) = 2Cℓ,ℓ+1.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2606.04086_figures/2606.04086_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 5. Comparison of constrained ground state energies E+(r) from ED and DMRG for L = 12, 14, 16. Black hollow diamonds show ED values obtained from E+(r) = E0 + ∆E(r), while colored dashed lines with solid dots show DMRG energies computed directly from the projected Hamiltonian H+ = PH0P.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This work develops a theoretical framework to calculate the effective interaction between quantum strings constrained by hard-core rules. By utilizing Full Counting Statistics (FCS), the authors demonstrate that this interaction is fundamentally controlled by the entanglement entropy ($S_\ell$) of the string itself. This provides a powerful, entanglement-based method for characterizing topological interactions in quantum many-body systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** The direct connection established between entanglement entropy ($S_\ell$) and the effective interaction strength ($\Delta E(r)$) provides a powerful, non-local diagnostic tool for understanding topological defects in condensed matter systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the fundamental question of determining the effective interaction potential between quantum strings, particularly when these strings are subject to hard-core constraints that introduce nonlocal interactions.

**Main result.** The effective interaction potential is shown to be governed by the Full Counting Statistics (FCS) of the relative string operator, yielding an entanglement-controlled asymptotic form $\ln\Delta E(r) \sim -C r^2/S_\ell$.

**Method.** The authors employ the Feshbach–Schur method combined with the formalism of Full Counting Statistics (FCS) to calculate the ground-state energy shift induced by the hard-core constraint.

**Model / system.** The model involves two fluctuating hard-core quantum strings on a lattice, constrained by a hard-core rule forbidding crossing. The physics is analyzed using string Hamiltonians and projected operators.

**Key observables.** Effective Potential ($\Delta E(r)$), Full Counting Statistics generating function ($\chi(\lambda)$), Entanglement Entropy ($S_\ell$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The separation distance ($r$) between the strings, and the entanglement entropy ($S_\ell$) which sets the leading scale of the repulsion.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis often relies on the mid-wall approximation to derive analytic expressions, although the leading asymptotic behavior is shown to be preserved when considering the full-wall constraint.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare the calculated effective potential (derived from FCS) against high-precision numerical results from Exact Diagonalization (ED) and DMRG, confirming the predicted quadratic scaling dependence on the separation distance $r$.

**Paper structure.** The paper builds the theory by first relating the hard-core constraint to nonlocality, then using the Feshbach–Schur method to derive the effective Hamiltonian, and finally mapping the resulting expectation values onto the framework of Full Counting Statistics to extract the asymptotic potential.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

How quantum strings interact is a basic question for extended objects in quantum many-body physics. Even the simplest hard-core constraint (no crossing), can generate a nontrivial effective potential, whose microscopic form is difficult to determine because the relative distance between the strings is intrinsically nonlocal. Here we show that this nonlocality is naturally captured by full counting statistics (FCS). For two hard-core quantum strings, we derive an analytic FCS expression for the emergent interaction by identifying the virtual process in which the two strings touch and hop back. Using the FCS--entanglement relation, we find the effective potential has the entanglement-controlled asymptotic form $\lnΔE(r)\sim -π^2 r^2/(12 S_\ell)$ up to subleading terms, where $S_\ell$ is the entanglement entropy between the two halves of a quantum string. We confirm the theory using high-precision numerical calculations and finite-size FCS estimates. Our results reveal FCS as a direct route to effective interactions between quantum topological line-defects, which may also be extended to higher-form charge.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2606.04997"></a>
### [A thorium-229 optical nuclear clock with feedback loop](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04997v1)

**Authors:** L. Toscani De Col, T. Riebner, I. Morawetz, F. Schneider, N. Sempelmann, J. Schlachet-Lépinay, F. Schaden, M. Bartokos, G. A. Kazakov, K. Beeks, B. Gerstenecker, M. Pimon, S. Lahs, A. Hellerschmied, T. Lercher, J. Premper, A. Niessner, M. Matus, H. Denker, M. Cizek, O. Cip, V. Lal, G. Zitzer, V. Petrov, J. Tiedau, M. V. Okhapkin, E. Peik, T. Schumm  
**Type:** experiment · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.04997v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `quantum measurements` **4/5** · `measurement-induced transitions` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<sub>FIG. 1. Schematic representation of the setup. a, Thorium-229 nuclear clock located at TU Wien. **Summary.** This paper details the implementation and characterization of a highly sensitive optical nuclear clock based on the $	ext{Th-229}$ isotope. By stabilizing the laser to the nuclear transition and comparing it to an atomic clock, the researchers achieved state-of-the-art stability. This precision allows them to place world-leading constraints on variations in fundamental constants and search for signatures of ultralight dark matter.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work represents a cutting-edge application of quantum metrology, pushing the limits of precision measurement by moving from electron-based atomic transitions to nuclear transitions, which offers unique sensitivity to fundamental physics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To realize and characterize an optical nuclear clock using the Thorium-229 isotope to test fundamental physics principles and search for dark matter signals.

**Main result.** The clock achieved a shot-noise limited instability approaching $10^{-15}$ over one day, setting stringent constraints on variations in fundamental constants and dark matter coupling.

**Method.** The experiment involves stabilizing a continuous-wave laser to the $	ext{Th-229}$ nuclear transition frequency using rapid feedback based on continuous absorption spectroscopy, comparing it to a reference atomic clock ($	ext{Yb}^+$).

**Model / system.** The system utilizes $	ext{Th-229}$ nuclei embedded in a $	ext{CaF}_2$ crystal, interrogating the $148 	ext{ nm}$ nuclear transition. The measurement technique involves sophisticated laser stabilization and frequency comparison against established atomic standards.

**Key observables.** Fractional frequency instability ($\sigma_y$), frequency ratio ($	ext{f}_{	ext{Th}}/	ext{f}_{	ext{Yb}^+}$), and the amplitude of periodic fluctuations in the beat frequency spectrum.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Instability approaching $10^{-15}/\sqrt{	au}/	ext{s}$; search time scales between $20 	ext{ s}$ and $1 	ext{ day}$; constraints on fundamental constants like $\alpha$ and $\Lambda_{QCD}$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes the signal can be modeled by a Lorentzian fit for spectral analysis and relies on Monte Carlo simulations to establish detection thresholds for periodic signals.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the experimental setup, including the laser stabilization loop and frequency comparison to the $	ext{Yb}^+$ clock. Other figures show the measured frequency instability ($\sigma_y$ vs $	au$) and exclusion plots for dark matter parameters.

**Paper structure.** The paper details the experimental setup and clock stabilization methods, followed by characterization of the clock's stability, and finally applying the high precision to search for variations in fundamental constants and dark matter signals using spectral analysis techniques.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The laser-accessible nuclear transition in the thorium-229 isotope has been identified as a promising candidate for the realization of an optical nuclear clock. Such a nuclear clock might rival or outperform current optical clocks based on electron-shell transitions in atoms or ions, is expected to be more robust against external perturbations, and provides enhanced sensitivity in clock-based tests of fundamental principles of physics. Here, we implement a thorium-229 nuclear clock by stabilizing a continuous-wave laser to the 148 nm nuclear transition with rapid feedback based on continuous absorption spectroscopy. The thorium-229 nuclei are embedded into a millimeter-sized, room temperature calcium fluoride crystal. A subharmonic of the 148 nm radiation is continuously compared to a Yb+ single-ion clock. The nuclear clock shows a simple shot-noise limited scaling of the fractional frequency instability of $3\cdot 10^{-12} \sqrt{τ/\text{s}}$ where $τ$ is the averaging time, approaching $10^{-15}$ instabilities over 1 day of continuous operation. Improvements of the instability by several orders of magnitude can be projected for future solid-state nuclear clocks. We use the nuclear clock to constrain models of ultralight dark matter by searching for periodic fluctuations and slow drifts in the nuclear transition energy, on time scales between 20 s and 1 day. Drawing benefit from the enhanced sensitivity of the thorium-229 transition, these constraints compete with the best atomic clocks concerning dark matter coupling to photons and go beyond previous measurements regarding coupling to the strong force and quarks.

</details>

### [Circuit-Level Noise Estimation via Shuttling in Plaquette Circuits](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04629v1)

**Authors:** Huyen Do, Alexandru Paler  
**Type:** experiment · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.04629v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `QC/QI experiment` **4/5** · `quantum measurements` **1/5**

**Summary.** This paper presents a novel method to estimate the circuit-level noise in Quantum Error Correction (QEC) circuits using only single-shot measurements. By comparing 'fresh' versus 'recycled' syndrome qubits on trapped-ion and superconducting hardware, the authors quantify noise accumulation. The results demonstrate the effectiveness of circuit optimization and provide a vital diagnostic for assessing the feasibility of deep QEC circuits on current quantum processors.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a crucial, hardware-agnostic diagnostic tool for assessing the viability of large-scale QEC experiments by characterizing circuit-level noise directly from limited measurement data.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the critical need to estimate Quantum Error Correction (QEC) circuit-level noise rates when only single-shot measurements are available and lower-level hardware calibration is infeasible.

**Main result.** The authors successfully estimated plaquette error rates on both trapped-ion (IonQ Aria1) and superconducting (IBM Torino) processors, showing that circuit optimization significantly improves reliability, particularly on shuttling-based architectures.

**Method.** They developed an error model comparing 'FRESH' (fresh ancilla per repetition) and 'RECYCLE' (reusing ancilla) syndrome qubit configurations to quantify noise accumulation across multiple plaquette repetitions.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on surface-code plaquette circuits, testing the methodology on physical quantum hardware platforms: the IonQ Aria1 trapped-ion device and the IBM Torino superconducting processor.

**Key observables.** Effective plaquette error rates ($	ext{plaq}$), time-like correlated error rates ($	ext{corre}$), and the probability of ancilla measurement outcomes being '1' as a function of circuit depth.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Plaquette depth (number of repetitions $n$), circuit optimization level (transpiler settings), and the specific syndrome qubit configuration (FRESH vs. RECYCLE).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The primary assumption is that the derived error model accurately captures the noise scaling, which is shown to fail on fixed-layout QPUs due to routing overhead.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the surface code plaquette structure, the FRESH and RECYCLE strategies, and show polynomial fits of the error probability versus circuit depth for different plaquette designs on the IonQ Aria1 QPU.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of single-shot noise estimation, details the error model comparing FRESH and RECYCLE strategies, validates the approach by optimizing circuits for native gate sets (IonQ), and compares results across different hardware architectures (IonQ vs. IBM Torino).

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We present a method for estimating QEC circuit-level noise levels assuming that only single-shot measurements are available (e.g. measurements are slow and performed in a zoned/parallel fashion), and that lower level quantum hardware calibration is not possible (e.g. cloud access) or not feasible (e.g. large scale computing). We develop and run surface code plaquette experiments using two syndrome qubit configurations: FRESH, involving fresh qubits for each plaquette repetition, and RECYCLE, reusing qubits. To validate our approach, we compile plaquettes to ion-trap (IonQ Aria1) native gate set and apply hardware-aware rewrite templates to reduce circuit depth and execution time. We also run the experiments on a non-shuttling, superconducting processor (IBM Torino). We estimate circuit-level noise rates from the resulting single-shot plaquette measurement statistics, and conclude numerically about the viability of low-depth QEC experiments.

</details>

**Authors:** Evan Munaro-Langloÿs, Axel Stenquist, Jan Marcus Dahlström

**Summary.** This paper explores controlling quantum dynamics in two-level atoms using resonant dichromatic laser pulses. By exploiting the slowly varying beating envelope, the authors demonstrate coherent control over the geometric phase accumulated during Rabi oscillations. This control manifests in unique, predictable features in the photoelectron spectrum, offering a new route for engineering light-matter interactions.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a detailed, analytical understanding of how the temporal structure of the driving field (the beating envelope) can be used to engineer specific interference patterns in ultrafast light-matter interactions, which is central to quantum optics and AMO physics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates how geometric phase control can be achieved in two-level atoms driven by near-degenerate dichromatic laser pulses.

**Main result.** The beating envelope dynamics lead to spectral structures, such as re-emergent central peaks and higher-order sidebands, which differ qualitatively from standard Autler-Townes splitting.

**Method.** The authors analytically derive the essential-state dynamics using an exactly solvable model for flat-top pulses and analyze the resulting photoionization spectra via Fourier analysis.

**Model / system.** The system is a two-level atom undergoing Rabi dynamics, driven by a dichromatic field composed of two closely spaced frequencies. The dynamics are tracked using a pseudo-spin vector on the Bloch sphere.

**Key observables.** Photoelectron spectra, geometric phase accumulation, and the appearance/disappearance of Autler-Townes doublets and central peaks.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The frequency detuning ($\Delta\omega$), the Rabi frequency ($\Omega$), and the beating area (e.g., $\pi$-beating area vs. $2\pi$-beating area).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis utilizes an exactly solvable model for flat-top pulse envelopes and relies on time-dependent perturbation theory for ionization amplitudes.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate Rabi dynamics showing the effect of $\pi$-beating vs. $2\pi$-beating areas, and show photoelectron spectra comparing results from dichromatic pulses versus ordinary Rabi oscillations.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by establishing the theoretical framework of geometric phase accumulation in the presence of a beating envelope, deriving the time-dependent amplitudes, and finally analyzing the resulting photoionization spectra across different beating areas.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We investigate geometric-phase control in resonantly driven two-level atoms exposed to near-degenerate dichromatic laser pulses. In contrast to conventional two-color schemes based on widely separated frequencies, the closely spaced frequency components generate a slowly varying beating envelope that repeatedly reverses the pseudo-spin rotation on the Bloch sphere. This enables coherent control of the geometric phase accumulated during Rabi dynamics and strongly modifies the resulting photoelectron spectra. Using an exactly solvable model for flat-top pulse envelopes, we derive the essential-state dynamics analytically and analyze photoionization induced both by an auxiliary field and by the dichromatic driving field itself. We show that the formation of Autler--Townes doublets can be interpreted in terms of destructive interference associated with geometric phases acquired during completed pseudo-spin rotations. Beyond the canonical Autler--Townes regime, beat-induced reversal dynamics lead to qualitatively different spectral structures, including re-emergent central peaks and higher-order sidebands. These effects originate from the nonuniform temporal evolution induced by the beating envelope, which modifies the balance between positive and negative excitation amplitudes. Our results establish beating-field control as a route toward engineering geometric-phase interference in ultrafast light--matter interactions and suggest broader applications in coherent control of atoms, molecules, and x-ray-driven systems.

</details>

### [An Entanglement Measure from Quantum Optimal Transport](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04969v1)

**Authors:** Enmin Shao, Lin Chen, Huixia He  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.04969v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `entanglement & information structure` **4/5**
