<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2606.04969_figures/2606.04969_page3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 3</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2606.04969_figures/2606.04969_page4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 4</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2606.04969_figures/2606.04969_page5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 5</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2606.04969_figures/2606.04969_page6.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 6</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This work proposes a novel entanglement measure, $E(ho)$, defined using the quantum Wasserstein distance between a state and the set of separable states. By leveraging the metric properties of the Wasserstein distance, the authors establish that $E(ho)$ satisfies all necessary axioms for an entanglement measure. This provides a versatile, geometrically grounded tool for quantifying quantum correlations.

**Why it may be interesting.** The geometric approach using optimal transport provides a powerful, mathematically rigorous framework for entanglement quantification, connecting it directly to measurable quantities like entanglement witnesses.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper aims to develop a rigorous, axiom-satisfying measure for quantifying quantum entanglement in bipartite systems.

**Main result.** It introduces the entanglement measure $E(ho)$ based on the quantum Wasserstein distance, which satisfies fundamental axioms and provides quantitative lower bounds related to entanglement witnesses.

**Method.** The core method involves defining $E(ho)$ as the minimal order-1 quantum Wasserstein distance from a state $ho$ to the set of separable states ($	ext{SEP}$), and utilizing its dual variational formulation.

**Model / system.** The analysis is focused on bipartite quantum states $ho$ acting on a tensor product Hilbert space $\mathcal{H}_A \otimes \mathcal{H}_B$. The measure quantifies the distance from $ho$ to the set of separable states.

**Key observables.** Entanglement measure $E(ho)$, quantum Wasserstein distance $W_1(ho, \sigma)$, and entanglement witnesses $W$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The Lipschitz seminorm ($\lVert H Vert_{	ext{Lip}}$) and the dimension of the subsystems ($d_A, d_B$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The derivation relies on the data-processing inequality of the Wasserstein metric and the assumption that the infimum/maximum operations can be interchanged.

**Paper structure.** The paper establishes the measure via the Wasserstein distance, derives its dual variational form, proves it satisfies key axioms (LOCC monotonicity, etc.), and provides explicit lower bounds for pure and mixed states.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We propose a bipartite entanglement measure $E(ρ)$ defined as the minimal order-1 quantum Wasserstein distance from a state to the set of separable states. Owing to the universal data-processing inequality of the Wasserstein metric, the measure satisfies all fundamental axioms within a single geometric framework. A Lipschitz dual formulation yields explicit lower bounds for pure and mixed states, a sharp constant for two-qubit systems, and an expected value for Haar-random pure states. We further establish a quantitative connection to entanglement witnesses: any negative witness expectation value certifies a lower bound on $E$, and the dual variational bound is exactly the maximal violation achievable by a Lipschitz-1 witness. The approach naturally provides subadditivity, trace-distance estimates, and bounds on local observables, while pointing toward large-deviation conjectures. This work furnishes a versatile paradigm at the interface of entanglement theory, optimal transport, and experimental entanglement detection.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.04470"></a>
### [Multi-entropy in random tensor networks](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04470v1)

**Authors:** Miao Hu, Simon Lin, Ion Nechita  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.04470v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `entanglement & information structure` **4/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.04470_figures/2606.04470_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1: We tile the hyperbolic disk isometrically using a graph. Each dark blue node represents a random tensor and the connecting edges indicate tensor contractions. The random tensor network defines a state on the Hilbert space supported on the boundary, represented here by dangling legs. (a) The geometric dual of the entanglement entropy: The entropy of region A is proportional to the area of the minimal surface (black dashed) that divides A and its complement ¯A. (b) The proposed geometric dual for the multi-entropy. Here we take q = 3 as an example. The tripartite multi-entropy between region A : B : C is proportional to the area of the minimal multiway cut (also black dashed) that...</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2606.04470_figures/2606.04470_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2: The diagrammatic notation for the reduced density matrix ρj1···jq i1···iq , and the con-</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2606.04470_figures/2606.04470_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 4: Examples for the minimal energy configuration of (a) R´enyi negativity, and (b) R´enyi reflected entropy in a hyperbolic tensor network. Different domains are represented by different colors.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2606.04470_figures/2606.04470_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 6: The connected three-terminal tree used in the tree example, followed by its five minimal multiway cut labelings. In panels (b)–(f), the pair records the labels (λ(u), λ(v)) of the two bulk vertices. Note that the three terminals t1, t2, t3 have fixed labels in panels (b)–(f).</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2606.04470_figures/2606.04470_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 7: The symmetric triangle network, followed by representative minimum multiway cut labelings. Panel (b) shows the constant labeling C1, while panel (c) shows the aligned labeling I. The three terminals t1, t2, t3 have fixed labels in the last two panels.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This work analyzes multi-partite entanglement in Random Tensor Networks by comparing generalized multi-entropies to minimal multiway cuts. While the conjecture holds for the simplest case (n=2), the authors prove it fails for higher Rényi indices (n>2) by identifying replica-symmetry-breaking saddle points that yield lower energy values. This has significant implications for applying geometric intuition to complex quantum entanglement structures.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a deep, mathematically rigorous understanding of how entanglement measures (multi-entropies) relate to geometric structures (minimal cuts) in quantum many-body systems, which is crucial for understanding holographic duality.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates the relationship between Rényi multi-entropies in Random Tensor Network (RTN) states and the area of minimal multiway cuts in the large bond-dimension limit.

**Main result.** The multi-entropy equals the minimal multiway cut area only for the specific case of the Rényi index n=2; for n>2, the conjecture fails due to the existence of replica-symmetry-breaking saddle points.

**Method.** The analysis uses saddle-point approximations derived from minimizing a free energy functional defined over permutations, relating it to geometric minimal cuts.

**Model / system.** The model is Random Tensor Networks (RTNs) defined on graphs, which are used to simulate quantum states exhibiting multipartite entanglement, drawing analogies to the Ryu–Takayanagi formula in holography.

**Key observables.** Rényi multi-entropies $S^{(q)}_n$, the free energy $F(g)$, and the area of minimal multiway cuts $A(\{\Gamma_k\})$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The Rényi index $n$, the number of parties $q$, and the bond dimension $\chi$ (taken to infinity).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis heavily relies on the replica trick and saddle-point approximations, which are shown to break down for $n>2$ due to replica symmetry breaking.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the geometric dualities, comparing entanglement entropy to minimal surfaces and proposing a dual relationship between multi-entropy and minimal multiway cuts.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first establishing the conjecture for $n=2$ (where it holds), then providing explicit counterexamples for $n>2$ by analyzing the free energy minimization, and finally characterizing the structure of the true minimizers using RSB elements.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study the evaluation of Rényi multi-entropies $S^{(q)}_n$ in Random Tensor Network (RTN) states in the large bond-dimension limit. For the case of Rényi index $n=2$ and arbitrary number of parties $q$, we prove that that multi-entropies are determined by minimal multiway cuts through the network. When the minimal multiway cut is degenerate, we characterize the full minimizer set via compatible families of minimal cuts and give a criterion for all minimizers to come from ordinary cut partitions. For $n=2$, this gives a natural generalization of the minimal cut description of bipartite entanglement to multipartite systems with arbitrarily many parties. For the case of integer $n>2$, we show that the minimal multiway cut conjecture is in general \emph{not true} by providing explicit counter examples for both the single random tensor and for the network built from isometric tilings. We discuss the implication for our results on the multipartite entanglement structures in RTN and holography.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2606.04721"></a>
### [Quantification of Entanglement in Three-Qubit Systems](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04721v1)

**Authors:** Zikra Aram  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.04721v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `entanglement & information structure` **4/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

**Summary.** This paper introduces a global concurrence measure to quantify entanglement in three-qubit pure states by summing bipartite entanglement contributions across all possible partitions. Using the Acin canonical form, the authors demonstrate that this measure provides a broader view of total quantum correlations compared to measures sensitive only to genuine tripartite entanglement. It is a theoretical advancement for characterizing multipartite quantum states.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a robust, comprehensive tool ($E$) for quantifying total quantum correlations in small quantum systems, which is crucial for understanding resource theories in quantum information processing.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study addresses the complex quantification of multipartite entanglement in three-qubit pure states, aiming to overcome the limitations of existing entanglement measures.

**Main result.** The proposed global concurrence measure provides complementary insights into multipartite correlations, successfully distinguishing between different entanglement classes (e.g., GHZ vs. W states) where established measures might fail or only detect genuine entanglement.

**Method.** The authors employ an analytical approach using the Acin canonical representation and define a global concurrence measure as the sum of generalized concurrences derived from the determinants of reduced density matrices of all three possible bipartitions.

**Model / system.** The system under investigation is a three-qubit quantum system, whose pure states are parameterized using the Acin canonical representation, which generalizes the Schmidt decomposition.

**Key observables.** Global concurrence ($E$), three-tangle ($	au$), and tripartite negativity ($\mathcal{N}_3$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The state is parameterized by five non-negative amplitudes ($\lambda_i$) and one phase ($\phi$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes that the global concurrence, constructed from bipartite correlations, is a suitable and comprehensive measure for total entanglement in three-qubit systems.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the variation of the global concurrence ($E$) across different entanglement classes (Biseparable, GHZ, W) and compare $E$ against $	au$ and $\mathcal{N}_3$ for both GHZ and W states.

**Paper structure.** The paper proceeds by first establishing the mathematical framework using the Acin canonical form, deriving the global concurrence measure $E$ from reduced density matrix determinants, and finally comparing $E$'s behavior against established measures like the three-tangle and tripartite negativity across various entanglement classes.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

This study presents an analytical investigation of entanglement quantification in three-qubit pure states through the Acín canonical representation, which serves as a generalization of the two-qubit Schmidt decomposition. Driven by the intricacies of multipartite entanglement and the shortcomings of current measures, we employ a global concurrence measure derived from the generalized concurrence of various bipartitions within the system. The characteristics of this measure are explored both analytically and numerically across a range of SLOCC entanglement classes, such as product, biseparable, GHZ-type, and W-type states. When compared to the three-tangle and tripartite negativity, our findings indicate that this measure yields complementary insights into multipartite correlations. The outcomes underscore the significance of this measure in elucidating the structure and distribution of entanglement within three-qubit quantum systems.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper develops a rigorous theoretical framework using the spin density matrix formalism to describe the dynamics of spinor polariton condensates coupled to an excitonic reservoir. It corrects flaws in existing theories concerning polarization invariance and analyzes how the condensate's polarization state evolves under external pumping, showing the critical role of reservoir relaxation times.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant to AMO physics and quantum optics as it tackles the complex, non-equilibrium dynamics of interacting quantum matter waves (polaritons) in the presence of dissipation and polarization effects.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the theoretical description of dynamics in driven-dissipative spinor polariton condensates, specifically focusing on the coupling between the condensate and an incoherent excitonic reservoir.

**Main result.** The standard theory fails when the reservoir has non-zero in-plane linear polarization; the correct description requires using the spin density matrix formalism to maintain polarization invariance.

**Method.** The authors derive and analyze coupled equations for the condensate and reservoir density matrices using the spin density matrix formalism, transforming between circular and linear polarization bases.

**Model / system.** The system involves cavity polaritons, which are modeled as spinor condensates coupled to an incoherent excitonic reservoir. The dynamics are governed by coupled equations derived from a mean-field approach.

**Key observables.** Condensate wavefunction ($\Psi$), condensate density matrix ($\hat{ho}$), reservoir density matrix ($\hat{ho}_R$), and the Stokes parameters ($S_x, S_y, S_z$) characterizing the condensate polarization.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Relaxation time $	au_{\parallel}$, pump polarization components ($S_x, S_y, S_z$), and coupling constants ($g, 	ilde{g}, \gamma, \Gamma$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis often neglects spatial effects ($
abla^2\phi$ term) and assumes the coupling is limited to polaritons and incoherent excitons.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the stationary state polarization dependence on pump ellipticity ($S_P z$), showing polarization plane rotation above a threshold, and the time evolution of Stokes parameters, which are non-sinusoidal near threshold but approach sinusoidal behavior for large $S_P z$.

**Paper structure.** The paper first establishes the theoretical necessity of the spin density matrix formalism due to polarization invariance issues. It then applies this formalism to analyze the polarization dynamics of the condensate under varying pump ellipticity and reservoir relaxation times.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We review the problem of description of the dynamics of driven-disspipative spinor polariton condensates, focusing on the terms corresponding to the coupling between a macroscopic wavefunction of the condensdate and incoherent excitonic reservoir created by a non-resonant pump. We demonstrate that the existing version of the theory breaks down in case, when reservoir has non-zero components of the Stokes vector corresponding to in-plane linear polarization. The polarization invariant theory of reservoir to condensate coupling is formulated with use of the spin density matrix formalism.

</details>

### [Efficient Description of Parametric Amplification of Quantum Pulses](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04759v1)

**Summary.** This paper introduces an efficient analytical method to calculate the output quantum state of parametric amplification when the input is a complex quantum state. By decomposing the input operator relative to the output modes, the authors simplify the calculation significantly. The method is demonstrated using various input states and highlights the utility of quantum state purification through mode heralding.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a powerful, computationally efficient analytical tool for analyzing quantum state evolution in parametric processes, which is central to quantum optics and quantum information processing.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper develops an efficient, analytical method to determine the quantum state of output modes following parametric amplification when the input is an arbitrary non-vacuum quantum state.

**Main result.** The method successfully calculates output states for various inputs (coherent, cat, single-photon) and demonstrates that vacuum detection in one output mode can significantly purify the state in the other.

**Method.** The core method involves applying the amplification transformation to the vacuum state first, and then applying a transformed version of the operator that creates the input state from vacuum, avoiding complex ancillary mode calculations.

**Model / system.** The system modeled is bosonic quantum fields (like light in waveguides) undergoing parametric amplification, described by a Hamiltonian quadratic in creation and annihilation operators.

**Key observables.** Purity ($\gamma$), Wigner function, covariance matrix ($\sigma$), and the quantum state itself ($\hat{ho}_{	ext{out}}$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Input state parameters (e.g., $\alpha$ for coherent states), pump strength ($\xi$), and cavity loss rate ($\gamma$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The formalism relies heavily on the quadratic form of the Hamiltonian and the effective treatment of Gaussian states, although it claims generalizability.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the schematic transformation process, the Wigner functions of input/output states, and the effect of vacuum detection on state purity.

**Paper structure.** The paper builds from the general mathematical formalism for state transformation through parametric amplification, details the single-mode and two-mode operator decompositions, and concludes with specific examples demonstrating state purification via heralding.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

A single quantum pulse undergoing parametric amplification feeds into at most two pulses in the output. In this work, we present an efficient, analytical method for finding the quantum state of these output modes. Our method applies the amplification to the vacuum rather than to the input state, and subsequently applies a transformed version of the operator that creates the input state from vacuum. Given the input and output pulse mode functions, the method is analytical, and therefore computationally very efficient, and it can be readily generalized to multiple non-vacuum input modes. We exemplify the method by computing the output quantum states resulting from the input of a coherent, a Schrödinger cat, and a single photon input quantum state. We further employ the method to obtain the quantum state in one of the two output modes heralded upon detection of vacuum in the other, least populated, mode.

</details>

### [Theory of frozen flux in a narrow uniform superconducting strip after cooling in a small magnetic field](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04961v1)

**Authors:** Alexei E. Koshelev  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.04961v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `driven-dissipative phase transition` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **3/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **2/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5** · `Frenkel-Kontorova` **1/5** · `Kondo & dissipative impurity` **1/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **1/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `scars & prethermalization` **1/5**

**Summary.** This theoretical work models how magnetic flux gets trapped (frozen) in narrow superconducting strips during cooling. By solving the dynamic-balance equation for vortex movement, the authors predict the freezing temperature and the resulting trapped flux density. This provides a quantitative understanding of flux pinning relevant to superconducting electronics.

**Why it may be interesting.** The study of flux freezing and dynamic equilibrium in superconductors involves non-equilibrium statistical mechanics, critical slowing down, and barrier crossing phenomena, which share mathematical structures with open quantum systems dynamics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper analyzes the phenomenon of residual frozen magnetic flux trapped in a narrow superconducting strip when cooled through its critical temperature in a small external magnetic field.

**Main result.** The freezing process is governed by a dynamic balance equation, yielding a freezing temperature ($T_{	ext{fr}}$) and a frozen flux density ($n_{	ext{fr}}$) that exhibit strong, predictable dependencies on the magnetic field and cooling rate.

**Method.** The authors derive and solve the dynamic-balance equation describing the thermally-activated escape and entrance of vortices over geometric energy barriers.

**Model / system.** The system is a long, narrow superconducting strip ($W \ll \Lambda$). The dynamics are modeled using vortex energy profiles and the master equation for flux density evolution.

**Key observables.** Frozen flux density ($n_{	ext{fr}}$), freezing temperature ($T_{	ext{fr}}$), and the effective flux-expulsion magnetic field ($H_{	ext{eff}}$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The relative freezing temperature ($\epsilon_{	ext{fr}} = 1 - T_{	ext{fr}}/T_c$), the cooling rate ($R$), and characteristic energy scales like the Pearl energy ($E_P$) and the minimum flux-expulsion field ($H_e$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on the narrow-strip approximation ($W \ll \Lambda$) and assumes the system freezes in regimes where thermal activation dominates the dynamics.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate vortex energy profiles, the reduced function determining the escape barrier, and the time evolution of the flux density during cooling.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from establishing the physical problem (flux trapping) to deriving the dynamic-balance equation, solving it asymptotically, and finally calculating the quantitative dependence of the freezing temperature and density on external parameters.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We analyze residual frozen flux in a long narrow superconducting strip cooled through its transition temperature $T_{c}$ in a small perpendicular magnetic field. This problem is relevant for the issue of trapped magnetic flux in superconducting electronic devices. During cooling, the low-temperature vortex configuration is formed at temperatures very close to $T_{c}$, where the flux density is determined by dynamic balance between the thermally-activated exits and entries of vortices over the geometrical energy barrier formed by the interaction with the strip edges and the Meissner screening current. In the field range between the minimum flux-expulsion field and the penetration field, the equilibrium flux density is finite due to thermal activation and rapidly decreases with decreasing temperature. During cooling, however, the escape rate decreases exponentially, and the vortex density falls out of equilibrium at a field-dependent freezing temperature $T_{\mathrm{fr}}$. We derive and solve the dynamic-balance equation for this process, which yields definite quantitative results for $T_{\mathrm{fr}}$ and the frozen vortex density. The relative freezing temperature $1\!-\!T_{\mathrm{fr}}/T_{c}$ exceeds the fluctuation width of the transition by a large logarithmic factor, rapidly increases when the magnetic field approaches the minimum flux-expulsion field, and logarithmically increases with decreasing cooling rate. The resulting frozen flux density has a very strong magnetic-field dependence which can be used to define the effective flux-expulsion magnetic field.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [Squeezed Phonon Lasing via Floquet-Controlled Solid-State Defects](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.05083v1)

**Summary.** This paper presents a theoretical framework for achieving squeezed phonon lasing in solid-state defects within hBN membranes using Floquet engineering. By carefully controlling the system's driving fields and coupling mechanisms, the authors demonstrate a pathway to stabilize the phase and generate robust, non-classical mechanical states. This offers a promising route for quantum metrology applications.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant as it combines several advanced topics: Floquet engineering for quantum control, solid-state quantum emitters, and the generation of non-classical states (squeezing) in mechanical oscillators, which are core areas of AMO physics and quantum optics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper proposes a general Floquet-engineered scheme to achieve a continuous transition from conventional phonon lasing to phase-locked squeezed phonon lasing.

**Main result.** The scheme successfully demonstrates the realization of stable, steady-state squeezed phonon lasing in a solid-state platform by controlling the system's phase and quadrature fluctuations.

**Method.** The analysis relies on deriving effective Hamiltonians using Floquet theory and the Magnus expansion, followed by solving the resulting Master Equation to characterize steady-state dynamics.

**Model / system.** The physical system is a solid-state platform using color centers embedded in a hexagonal boron nitride (hBN) membrane, coupling a mechanical oscillator (phonons) to two principal and ancillary spins. The dynamics are governed by a complex Hamiltonian incorporating time-periodic driving terms.

**Key observables.** Lasing threshold, mechanical occupation ($\langle\hat{b}^\dagger\hat{b}angle_\infty$), second-order correlation function ($g^{(2)}(0)$), and the variance of generalized quadrature operators ($\langle(\Delta \hat{X}_	heta)^2angle$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Resonance conditions ($\Omega = \sum \Delta_j + \omega_m$), local driving frequencies ($
u$), and coupling strengths ($\lambda_1, J, \epsilon$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis utilizes approximations such as the Rotating-Wave Approximation (RWA), adiabatic elimination of fast-decaying ancilla spins, and specific parameter hierarchies to simplify the effective Hamiltonian.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the time evolution of $g^{(2)}(0)$ and quadrature variance, showing convergence to values characteristic of lasing and squeezing, respectively. Wigner functions are used to visualize the transition to squeezed states.

**Paper structure.** The paper builds from a general theoretical proposal (Section II) to the specific application on the hBN platform (Section III), detailing the derivation of effective Hamiltonians for both squeezed amplification and phase-locked regimes.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We propose a general Floquet-engineered scheme for phonon lasing that enables a continuous transition from conventional lasing to phase-locked squeezed phonon lasing. Focusing on a solid-state platform based on color centers embedded in a circular hexagonal boron nitride (hBN) membrane, we demonstrate that a mechanical oscillator coupled to principal and ancilla spins, and controlled via effective Floquet driving simultaneously exhibits squeezed-state amplification and cooling dynamics, leading to the emergence of a stable squeezed phonon laser. We analyse the steady-state properties of the system, including the lasing threshold, mechanical occupation, emission spectrum and second-order correlations. Furthermore, we show that Floquet engineering can realize phase-locked lasing while enabling controlled quadrature squeezing, thereby providing a simple yet effective route toward squeezed lasing in quantum mechanical systems. Our results offer new insights into the generation of squeezed phonon lasers in solid-state platforms, with potential applications in quantum metrology.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.04608"></a>
### [Correlated States in Quantum Dot Clusters Coupled to a Common Superconductor](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04608v1)

**Authors:** Martin Žonda, Jakub Rękas, Tobiáš Poláček, Jana Kodrlová, Vladislav Pokorný, Martin Friák  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.04608v1>

**Summary.** This work theoretically analyzes correlated electron behavior in quantum dot arrays coupled to a superconductor. By mapping the complex Hamiltonian into a particle-number-conserving form, the authors use advanced NQS methods to map out distinct quantum phases. The findings reveal rich physics, including gap closing transitions and the emergence of effective Heisenberg models in different dimensions.

**Why it may be interesting.** The combination of superconducting proximity effects with strong electronic correlations in nanoscale arrays is a major frontier in condensed matter physics, directly relevant to superconducting quantum circuits and quantum computing hardware.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the correlated electronic states within quantum dot clusters coupled to a common superconductor.

**Main result.** The research identifies three distinct interacting regimes—trivial singlet, Heisenberg-like, and critical—and finds robust triplet ground states in 2D clusters.

**Method.** The authors employ a combination of exact diagonalization, DMRG, and advanced Neural Quantum State Variational Monte Carlo (NQS-VMC) methods.

**Model / system.** The system is an effective model of regular quantum dot clusters coupled to a superconductor, described by a complex Hamiltonian involving local repulsion, inter-dot hopping, and superconducting pairing terms.

**Key observables.** Superconducting gap ($\Delta$), local moment ($\langle S_z angle$), mean electron occupation ($\langle N angle$), and entanglement entropy.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Superconducting gap ($\Delta$), Coulomb repulsion ($U$), inter-dot coupling ($W$), and coupling strength ($\zeta$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on canonical transformations to restore particle-number conservation for NQS methods, and approximations like the Superconducting Atomic Limit ($\Delta 	o \infty$).

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the evolution of the Bogoliubov-de Gennes spectrum showing gap closing at critical points, and phase diagrams mapping out ground state changes based on coupling strengths.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from defining the full Hamiltonian and applying canonical transformations to mapping the system onto a particle-number-conserving form. It then analyzes the non-interacting limit (gap closing) before tackling the interacting regimes using advanced numerical techniques.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study an effective model of regular quantum dot clusters coupled to a common superconductor. By applying a canonical transformation, we map the system onto a particle-number-conserving representation, making it directly accessible to standard fermionic neural-network quantum-state variational Monte Carlo methods. We show that the superconducting gap closes at a particular high-symmetry point, which, in finite non-interacting systems, corresponds to crossings between singlet ground states of different character. Combining exact methods, density matrix renormalization group, and neural quantum-state variational Monte Carlo calculations, we identify three distinct interacting regimes: a trivial superconducting singlet phase, a strongly correlated regime connected to an effective Heisenberg model, and a critical intermediate regime with qualitatively different behavior in one and two dimensions. In one-dimensional systems, the intermediate regime exhibits a sequence of singlet-doublet transitions and becomes gapless in the thermodynamic limit even for finite Coulomb interaction. In two-dimensional clusters, we find robust triplet ground states. Furthermore, our results demonstrate that relatively standard fermionic neural quantum states provide an efficient approach for correlated superconducting nanostructures.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.04187"></a>
### [Emergence of Macroscopic Quantum Order via Translational Zero Modes](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04187v1)

**Authors:** Kenan Gundogdu  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum gases · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.04187v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `analog quantum simulation` **3/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **3/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **2/5** · `correlated cavity matter` **2/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5** · `Frenkel-Kontorova` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.04187_figures/2606.04187_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 1 From fixed-spectrum condensation to zero-mode-stabilized collective order. (a) In conventional condensation, particles occupy the lowest level of a fixed excitation spectrum only after thermal occupation of higher-energy states is reduced below a critical temperature. (b) In a solitonic confining potential, fluctuations of the translational zero mode shift the potential relative to the laboratory frame. In the soliton frame, this motion coherently mixes the defect-bound excitonic states. (c) In the many-body regime, all confined excitons couple to the same shared zero-mode coordinate. This collective structural channel drives a single many-body state |ΨN⟩to lower energy, with...</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2606.04187_figures/2606.04187_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 2 Density-induced lattice instability and formation of a self-generated solitonic potential. (a) Effective Landau free energy Feff as a function of the lattice deformation coordinate φ for increasing excitation density. At low density, the uniform configuration φ = 0 is the stable minimum. As the density increases, the quadratic exciton–lattice coupling g2 renormalizes the effec- tive quartic coefficient ueff, causing the free-energy landscape to develop finite-deformation minima. At sufficiently high density, a finite-φ configuration becomes energetically favored, indicating a ther- modynamic instability of the uniform lattice. (b) Real-space representation of the transition. In the...</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2606.04187_figures/2606.04187_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 3 Impact of the solitonic transition on excitonic spatial coherence and potential landscapes. (a) In the homogeneous regime, the uniform crystal lattice defines an unconfined con- tinuous spectrum, supporting extended excitonic eigenstates approximated here as plane waves. (b) Extended excitons interact with a gapless continuum of traveling thermal phonons. These low-energy fluctuations generate random lattice distortions that modulate the potential landscape, dephase the excitonic wavefunctions, and distribute the population over many spatially incoherent configurations. (c) The solitonic transition changes the electronic geometry. A localized structural deformation gen- erates a...</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2606.04187_figures/2606.04187_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 4 Thermal-capacity suppression by a density-dependent collective gap. a, A finite ∆gap reduces the Bose phase-space factor from the gapless value g3/2(1) = ζ(3/2) to</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** The paper proposes that macroscopic quantum order can be sustained at high temperatures not by cooling, but by the self-generation of a mobile confining potential (soliton) coupled to the lattice. This coupling creates a 'translational zero mode' that opens a protective energy gap, stabilizing the collective quantum state and enhancing the critical temperature beyond conventional predictions.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a powerful, general theoretical framework for achieving high-temperature quantum coherence by engineering structural degrees of freedom (the zero mode) to modify the many-body spectrum, which is highly relevant to AMO physics and quantum optics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To find a mechanism for achieving macroscopic quantum order at elevated temperatures that bypasses the limitations of fixed excitation spectra or conventional pairing instabilities.

**Main result.** The coupling of electronic excitations to a deformable lattice via a translational zero mode generates a self-confining potential, opening a density-dependent gap that stabilizes coherence at higher temperatures.

**Method.** The analysis uses effective Hamiltonian descriptions, mean-field theory, and variational principles to model the collective energy lowering driven by the zero mode.

**Model / system.** The system involves excitons strongly coupled to a deformable lattice, leading to a density-induced lattice instability that creates a mobile, self-generated solitonic potential. This potential gives rise to a translational zero mode.

**Key observables.** Density-dependent gap ($\Delta_{gap}$), critical temperature ($T_c$), and the occupation of the single collective many-body orbital ($\lambda_0 = O(N)$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Critical density, coupling constants ($g_1, g_2$), and the zero-mode stiffness ($\kappa$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The zero mode acts as a shared collective coordinate, and the system can be effectively described by a two-level subspace in the macroscopic limit.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the transition from uniform to solitonic phases, the modification of the Bose phase-space factor due to the gap, and the enhancement of $T_c$ beyond standard scaling laws.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from identifying the general problem to modeling the lattice instability, deriving the zero-mode coupling, calculating the resulting energy lowering, and finally demonstrating the stabilization of coherence via the gap and ODLRO.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Macroscopic quantum coherence in solids, such as in superfluids, superconductors, and condensates, is generally limited to low temperatures because order forms within a fixed excitation spectrum whose competing states become thermally populated as temperature rises. Here, we show that strong coupling between electronic excitations and a deformable lattice enables a different route. Above a critical density, this coupling nucleates self generated confining potentials that trap the very excitations generating them. Unlike rigid external traps, these potentials can translate through the host lattice without changing their internal structure, defining a translational zero mode. Coupling to this zero mode provides a shared dynamical coordinate that lowers and isolates a single collective many body configuration, opening a density dependent gap that suppresses thermal occupation of competing states and supports off diagonal long range order at elevated temperatures. As a concrete realization, we identify high-temperature superfluorescence in lead halide perovskites as the radiative instability of this zero mode dressed ordered excitonic state. More broadly, this establishes a general route to macroscopic quantum order: not cooling within a fixed spectrum, nor pairing instabilities, but a self generated mobile confining structure whose translational zero mode reconstructs the many-body spectrum to protect coherence.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2606.04558"></a>
### [Extremely slow scaling of minimal Hamming distance in quantum sampling data](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04558v1)

**Authors:** P. S. Golubev, I. A. Iakovlev, V. V. Mazurenko  
**Type:** both · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.04558v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `analog quantum simulation` **3/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **3/5** · `QC/QI experiment` **2/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **2/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `exotic spin models in light-matter` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **1/5** · `quantum measurements` **1/5**

Minimal Hamming distance functions calculated for a few notable quantum states (from top to bottom): the classically-sampled 512-qubit Dicke state with 256 excita- tions; 324-qubit spin-glass states simulated on the D-Wave quantum annealer [20] with the square lattice topology and quench times of 7 (the upper yellow curve) and 20 (the lower yellow curve) ns; ground state of the frustrated antiferromag- netic J1 −J2 model optimized with transformer-based neural network quantum state approach [21] on 10 × 10 square lat- tice at J2 = 0.5; 53-qubit Haar-random state imitated on Sycamore quantum processor [22].</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** The paper introduces the average minimal Hamming distance as a universal metric to characterize quantum states from sparse measurement data. By fitting this distance to a power law, the authors demonstrate that the resulting parameters can reliably quantify state complexity and detect quantum phase transitions across diverse physical models, including those studied on quantum annealers.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a powerful, data-driven diagnostic tool that bypasses the need to calculate explicit physical observables, making it highly relevant for analyzing noisy, limited-data measurements from NISQ quantum hardware.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The core challenge is characterizing complex quantum states using only sparse measurement data, which is limited by the exponentially large Hilbert space.

**Main result.** The average minimal Hamming distance ($ar{r}_1(N_b)$) exhibits a universal power-law scaling, allowing the reliable extraction of quantum state complexity and the identification of phase transitions.

**Method.** The authors propose using the power-law fitting of $ar{r}_1(N_b) \propto N_b^{-\alpha}$ as a robust metric, comparing results from real quantum hardware, classical simulations, and advanced neural network representations.

**Model / system.** The analysis is applied to various quantum systems, including Dicke states, the $J_1-J_2$ model on a square lattice, and spin-glass systems, often simulated using Variational Neural-Network Quantum States (ViT).

**Key observables.** Average minimal Hamming distance ($ar{r}_1(N_b)$), power-law parameters ($A$ and $\alpha$), and Intrinsic Dimension (ID).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The exponent $\alpha$ is shown to be extremely slow, and the analysis is sensitive to critical points in the $J_1-J_2$ phase diagram.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The method assumes that the underlying quantum state structure dictates a universal power-law scaling in the minimal Hamming distance, even with limited sampling.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the power-law scaling of $ar{r}_1(N_b)$ for various states (Dicke, $J_1-J_2$), showing the dependence on system size and phase, and comparing results from different computational methods.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the metric, applies it to diverse physical models (Dicke, $J_1-J_2$), compares results across different computational techniques (ED, ViT, QMC), and concludes by establishing the universality of the power-law scaling for state characterization.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum data can be obtained from a diverse range of sources, including direct measurements from noisy quantum processors, cold-atom simulators, and classical approximations such as variational neural-network states. However, our ability to characterize these systems is fundamentally limited, as the available measurement data is often sparse compared to the exponentially large Hilbert space of the system. To address this, we propose using the average minimal Hamming distance calculated for a set of unique bitstrings as a robust metric revealing a universal power-law behaviour. Through various examples of real experiments and simulations, we show that the power-law parameters reliably capture the complexity of quantum states and identify quantum phase transitions from limited quantum information, without the need for accumulating extensive statistics or explicitly calculating physical observables. This enables the analysis of completely different quantum experiments within a single framework.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2606.04666"></a>
### [Nonreciprocal Dispersive Coupling for Quantum Sensing](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04666v1)

**Authors:** Dong Xie, Chunling Xu  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.04666v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `quantum measurements` **3/5** · `Tavis-Cummings & cavity-many-emitter` **2/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **2/5** · `correlated cavity matter` **2/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5** · `measurement-induced transitions` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.04666_figures/2606.04666_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1. Schematic diagram of nonreciprocal dispersive cou- pling. (A) The cavity mode a is coupled to the bosonic mode b with the coupling strength denoted by g. The qubit couples to the cavity mode a and bosonic b, with dispersive coupling strengths λ and χ, respectively. The dissipation rate suﬀered by the bosonic mode b is much higher than that of the cavity mode a, κb ≫(κ, g). (B) Schematic diagram of the equivalent nonreciprocal coupling between the cavity and the qubit. Af- ter fully eliminating bosonic mode b adiabatically, the cavity mode a couples to the qubit σz via nonreciprocal dispersive coupling with strength Γ.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2606.04666_figures/2606.04666_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2. Ratio of measurement uncertainty δnr/δnnr versus cavity photon number n and angle θ under reciprocal and nonreciprocal dispersive coupling. Here, we consider that λ ≫ κ and Γ ≫κ.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper theoretically investigates how nonreciprocal dispersive coupling can enhance quantum sensing beyond what is achievable with standard reciprocal coupling. By analyzing the measurement uncertainty using the Quantum Fisher Information, the authors demonstrate that nonreciprocity provides an exponential advantage in precision for measuring both photon number and driving strength. This work proposes a novel method to build ultra-precise quantum sensors using engineered nonreciprocal interactions.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant to AMO physics and quantum optics as it provides a theoretical framework for enhancing quantum measurement precision by engineering nonreciprocal interactions in cavity QED systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper compares the quantum sensing capabilities of nonreciprocal versus reciprocal dispersive coupling in a qubit-cavity system.

**Main result.** Nonreciprocal coupling provides superior measurement precision for both cavity photon number and driving strength compared to its reciprocal counterpart, especially at high photon numbers or driving strengths.

**Method.** The authors derived measurement uncertainties using the Quantum Fisher Information and the Quantum Cramér-Rao bound for both coupling types.

**Model / system.** The system involves a qubit coupled dispersively to a cavity mode, which is modeled using a Hamiltonian and analyzed via the master equation and quantum Langevin equations.

**Key observables.** Measurement uncertainty ($\delta^2 n$ for photon number; $\delta^2 \epsilon$ for driving strength $\epsilon$), and Quantum Fisher Information ($F_Q$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Dispersive coupling strengths ($\lambda$, $\Gamma$), cavity dissipation rate ($\kappa$), initial photon number ($n$), and driving strength ($\epsilon$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on adiabatic elimination of an intermediate bosonic mode and assumes specific dissipation rate hierarchies.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 schematically illustrates the nonreciprocal coupling setup and its equivalent form. Figure 2 (mentioned) likely compares the ratio of measurement uncertainties ($\delta_{nr}/\delta_{nnr}$) versus photon number $n$.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by establishing the Hamiltonian for both coupling types, deriving the evolution equations, calculating the Quantum Fisher Information for both, and finally comparing the resulting measurement uncertainties for photon number and driving strength using novel measurement strategies.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Dispersive coupling is widely utilized for quantum information readout. Most prior studies have concentrated on reciprocal dispersive coupling. Here, we further construct nonreciprocal dispersive coupling and apply it to quantum sensing. For cavity photon number measurement, nonreciprocal dispersive coupling delivers higher precision than its reciprocal counterpart, and this advantage grows more pronounced with an increase in photon number. When directly measuring the single-photon driving strength, however, nonreciprocal dispersive coupling shows no superiority over reciprocal dispersive coupling. By converting the information of driving strength into cavity photon numbers via our proposed strategy, nonreciprocal dispersive coupling again outperforms reciprocal dispersive coupling in precision, with the advantage becoming increasingly significant at larger driving strength. This work presents a novel method to boost quantum sensing and enable the fabrication of ultra-precise quantum sensors.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper tackles the fundamental trade-off between extreme sensitivity and noise immunity in non-Hermitian quantum sensors. By proposing an 'Off-ED' operating regime, the authors show how to move away from a mathematically singular point to achieve a vastly superior, linearly scaling Signal-to-Noise Ratio (SNR). This establishes a robust framework for practical, high-performance topological sensing devices.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a concrete, actionable paradigm for designing next-generation topological sensors by offering a method to bypass fundamental noise limitations inherent at idealized singular points.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The central challenge addressed is reconciling the extreme sensitivity of non-Hermitian sensors with the simultaneous amplification of environmental noise (the sensitivity-noise trade-off).

**Main result.** The authors propose an 'Off-ED' operating regime that eliminates pathological geometric singularities, restoring a linear scaling law for the Signal-to-Noise Ratio (SNR) while preserving exponential sensitivity scaling.

**Method.** The analysis involves comparing the performance at the strict Exceptional Deficiency (ED) limit versus operating slightly away from it (Off-ED), using noise matrix analysis and scaling law derivations.

**Model / system.** The system is a double-chain Hatano-Nelson model featuring unidirectional interlayer coupling, analyzed using a non-Hermitian Hamiltonian with an off-diagonally asymmetric block structure.

**Key observables.** Signal-to-Noise Ratio (SNR), spectral splitting ($\Delta E_n$), Eigenspace Cosine Similarity ($C$), and the background bias ($\kappa_2$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Exceptional Deficiency (ED) limit, Off-ED regime, disorder strength ($\alpha$), and noise magnitude ($\delta$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on the highly asymmetric interlayer coupling condition and the ability to model noise via a global noise matrix.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare SNR scaling ($	ext{SNR} \propto \delta^{-1/2}$ vs. $	ext{SNR} \propto \delta^{-1}$) and show the structural robustness of the system under varying levels of spatial disorder.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically analyzes the trade-off by first establishing the ED limit's limitations, then introducing the Off-ED framework to restore linear noise scaling, and finally demonstrating this improvement across various noise channels and disorder levels.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

A central challenge in non-Hermitian sensing is that spectral singularities simultaneously amplify both the signal and environmental noise. We address this predicament in a double-chain Hatano-Nelson model featuring unidirectional interlayer coupling. At the exceptional deficiency (ED) limit, the system exhibits a macroscopically degenerate complex spectrum and a pronounced non-Hermitian skin effect (NHSE), yielding a sensitivity that scales exponentially with lattice size $N$ while remaining robust across a six-order-of-magnitude detuning range. By introducing diagonal spatial disorder, we demonstrate that the NHSE is progressively suppressed, whith eigenspace cosine similarity analysis quantifying a well-defined fault-tolerance threshold. To reconcile the sensitivity-noise trade-off, we delineate "At-ED" and "Off-ED" operating regimes. While the At-ED configuration imposes fractional-order noise amplification (SNR $\propto δ^{-1/2}$) that saturates at a suboptimal plateau, migrating to the Off-ED regime eliminates this geometric singularity and restores a linear scaling law (SNR $\propto δ^{-1}$), achieving an SNR enhancement of several orders of magnitude. Crucially, this improvement is achieved while fully preserving the exponential sensitivity scaling, albeit at a slightly reduced absolute sensitivity compared to the strict At-ED limit. Our findings establish the Off-ED framework as a concrete paradigm for next-generation topological sensors that reconcile extreme sensitivity with robust noise immunity.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2606.05092"></a>
### [$η$-pairing in metallic and particle-hole asymmetric systems](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.05092v1)

**Authors:** Philipp Werner, Aaram J. Kim, Lei Geng

**Summary.** This theoretical work explores $\eta$-pairing superconductivity in correlated materials driven out of equilibrium by light. Using nonequilibrium DMFT, the authors find that while $\eta$-pairing is robust against charge imbalance in strongly correlated regimes, any asymmetry in the effective doublon and holon temperatures significantly suppresses the superconducting order parameter.

**Why it may be interesting.** The use of nonequilibrium DMFT to study photo-induced pairing mechanisms provides a powerful theoretical tool for understanding non-equilibrium quantum phenomena in correlated materials, bridging concepts from quantum optics and condensed matter theory.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the existence and robustness of $\eta$-pairing superconductivity in non-particle-hole symmetric and photo-excited metallic systems, extending beyond previous studies limited to symmetric Mott insulators.

**Main result.** $\eta$-pairing is robust against changes in total filling and doublon-holon imbalance in the strongly correlated regime, but the order parameter is strongly suppressed by asymmetry in effective doublon and holon temperatures.

**Method.** The authors employ nonequilibrium Dynamical Mean Field Theory (DMFT) combined with strong-coupling impurity solvers up to third order to model the non-equilibrium dynamics.

**Model / system.** The system is modeled using the single-band Hubbard model on the Bethe lattice, focusing on photo-doped Mott insulators and correlated metallic states.

**Key observables.** $\eta$-pairing order parameter, effective critical temperature ($T_{c, 	ext{eff}}$), doublon and holon densities, and effective doublon/holon temperatures.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Strongly correlated regime, large Mott gap, low effective doublon/holon temperatures, and the degree of particle-hole asymmetry.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on imposing nonequilibrium distribution functions corresponding to effective doublon and holon temperatures, and the use of specific strong-coupling solvers (NCA, OCA, TOA).

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate nonequilibrium distribution functions for gapped vs. metallic states, phase diagrams showing $T_{c, 	ext{eff}}$ dependence on filling and temperature, and spectral functions comparing different theoretical approximations.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first establishing the theoretical framework in the particle-hole symmetric limit, then extending the analysis to non-symmetric, photo-doped metallic states, and finally detailing the robustness and suppression mechanisms related to charge imbalance and temperature asymmetry.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Light-induced superconducting-like states have been reported in several classes of correlated materials. From a theoretical point of view, the induction of $η$-pairing is a promising route to nonthermal superconductivity. Numerical studies of photo-doped Mott systems revealed $η$-pairing states with very high effective critical temperatures. These investigations were however restricted to particle-hole symmetric states in large-gap Mott insulators, while the experiments were performed on strongly correlated metallic systems. It is thus relevant to explore if $η$-pairing also exists in non-particle-hole symmetric setups and in photo-excited metallic states. Here we use steady-state nonequilibrium dynamical mean field theory combined with a strong-coupling impurity solver up to third order to investigate this issue. We find that in the strongly correlated regime with large Mott gap, and for low effective doublon and holon temperatures, $η$-pairing is robust against changes in the total filling and an imbalance in the doublon and holon density. An asymmetry in the effective doublon and holon temperatures can however strongly suppress the order parameter. In photo-doped metallic systems with a three-peak structure in the local density of states, $η$-pairing can be realized in set-ups with positive doublon and holon temperatures and a population inversion in the low-energy quasi-particle band.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.05056"></a>
### [Measuring Entanglement in Qubit System](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.05056v1)

**Authors:** Vignesh S, Tabish Qureshi  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.05056v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **3/5** · `interference shaping light` **3/5** · `quantum measurements` **2/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **1/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `quantum optics experiment` **1/5** · `spintronics-quantum-optics interface` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.05056_figures/2606.05056_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Modified Stern-Gerlach interferometer for using the spin states as path markers. The spin, in addition, is en- tangled with an ancilla qubit. Another Stern-Gerlach setup is employed after this stage (no shown) with field in the x- direction, to implement quantum erasure before final detec- tion.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2606.05056_figures/2606.05056_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. Mach–Zehnder interferometer geometry. PBS1 cre- ates two path amplitudes |ψ1⟩, |ψ2⟩, BS2 recombines them, and output polarizers enable quantum-eraser post-selection before detection at D1, D2.</sub>

**Summary.** This paper presents a novel, operational method to measure entanglement in qubit systems by analyzing interference experiments. It demonstrates that the entanglement content can be extracted directly from the measurable asymmetry observed in the output channels of both spin and photon interferometers. This unifies the understanding of decoherence, quantum erasure, and entanglement estimation into a single, compact framework.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a powerful, unified theoretical tool linking fundamental concepts like entanglement, decoherence, and quantum erasure into a single, experimentally testable diagnostic based on interference asymmetries.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper aims to develop an operational and experimentally accessible method to quantify entanglement in qubit systems using interferometric measurements.

**Main result.** It establishes a unified framework showing that entanglement, coherence loss, and quantum erasure are all governed by the same underlying correlation structure, allowing concurrence to be calculated directly from measurable output channel asymmetries.

**Method.** The analysis uses a tripartite description involving paths, an internal qubit degree of freedom, and ancillary states, deriving the entanglement measure (concurrence) from the measured asymmetry of the output channels after quantum erasure.

**Model / system.** The theory is applied to two distinct physical platforms: a modified Stern-Gerlach interferometer for spin-1/2 particles and a Mach-Zehnder interferometer for polarization-carrying photons.

**Key observables.** The measurable asymmetry of the two output channels, which is directly related to the overlap of ancilla states ($	ext{Re}\langle\alpha|etaangle$) and determines the concurrence.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The overlap parameter $s = \langle\alpha|etaangle$ and the resulting concurrence $C_2 = 1 - |\langle\alpha|etaangle|^2$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes the ability to perform quantum erasure via measurement in a complementary basis and that the ancillary degree of freedom, while potentially inaccessible, dictates the measurable correlation structure.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates the modified Stern-Gerlach setup using spin states, while Figure 2 details the Mach-Zehnder interferometer geometry incorporating polarization post-selection for quantum erasure.

**Paper structure.** The paper first introduces the tripartite model, applies it to the spin and photon interferometers, derives the relationship between the measured asymmetry and the overlap parameter, and finally establishes the universal operational entanglement law $C_2 = 1 - A^2$.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

An operational way of measuring entanglement in a balanced two-path interferometers is presented, where path information is carried by some internal degree of freedom which, in turn, gets entangled with an ancilla system. The analysis is based on a tripartite description involving paths, an internal qubit degree of freedom, and some ancillary states entangled with the internal degree of freedom. It is then applied to two physically distinct experimental situations: a modified Stern-Gerlach interferometer with spin-1/2 particles and a Mach-Zehnder interferometer with photons carrying polarization. The ancilla degree of freedom may not be experimentally accessible. Tracing out the ancillary system, and employing a quantum erasing procedure based on the internal degree of freedom, it is demonstrated that a concurrence-based measure of the entanglement, between the internal degree of freedom and the ancilla, can be extracted directly from measurable asymmetry of the two output channels. These results show that loss of coherence, quantum erasure, and entanglement estimation in interferometric experiments arise from the same underlying correlation structure and provide a compact experimentally accessible framework for quantifying entanglement in qubit systems.

</details>

### [Quantum simulations of ultrafast optical spectroscopy of semiconductors on digital quantum computers in the semi-classical approximation](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04295v1)

**Authors:** Mykhailo Klymenko, Bahar Goldozian, Thong Hoang, Jared H. Cole, Muhammad Usman

**Summary.** This work establishes a quantum simulation framework to model how semiconductors interact with ultrafast light pulses. By mapping the complex fermionic dynamics onto qubits, it allows for benchmarking quantum computers against established classical methods. The results demonstrate the framework's capability while critically analyzing the impact of realistic hardware noise on spectroscopic measurements.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a concrete, physically motivated application for quantum computation—simulating complex, non-equilibrium quantum dynamics—and directly addresses the challenges of noise in near-term quantum hardware.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Developing a digital quantum simulation framework to model ultrafast optical spectroscopy in semiconductor materials.

**Main result.** The framework successfully simulates key spectroscopic features (absorption/gain) and shows quantitative agreement with classical methods in the noiseless limit, while also characterizing the impact of realistic NISQ noise.

**Method.** The simulation maps the fermionic Hamiltonian onto qubits using the Jordan-Wigner transformation and evolves the system using the Suzuki-Trotter formula for time evolution.

**Model / system.** The system is semiconductor materials (e.g., GaAs) modeled by a Hamiltonian including kinetic, Coulomb, and time-dependent light-matter interaction terms. The dynamics are treated as an open quantum system.

**Key observables.** Induced dipole moment/polarization, electric susceptibility $\chi(\omega)$, and the linear absorption/optical gain spectra $\alpha(\omega)$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Ultrafast time scales, finite temperatures ($T=3$ K to $T=30$ K), and the presence of realistic hardware noise.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The semiclassical approximation is used (classical field, quantum matter), and the simulation often relies on the quasi-equilibrium approximation for temperature effects.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare microscopic polarization and linear absorption spectra across classical, noiseless quantum, and noisy quantum simulations for GaAs, illustrating the effect of noise and temperature.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from defining the physical problem and Hamiltonian, detailing the theoretical mapping to qubits (JWT), outlining the time evolution via Trotterization, and finally presenting comparative results across different noise and temperature regimes.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We present a digital quantum simulation framework for ultrafast optical spectroscopy of semiconductor materials. The framework is based on Brillouin-zone discretization and the second-quantization formalism, and is designed as a quantum alternative to classical simulations based on the semiconductor Bloch equations. Its current capabilities include quantum simulations of linear absorption and optical gain spectra, incorporating Lorentzian broadening, finite-temperature band-filling effects, and reduced-dimensionality effects. Benchmark comparisons with classical simulations for GaAs demonstrate quantitative agreement in the noiseless limit. The inclusion of realistic hardware noise of NISQ-era quantum computers effectively manifests itself as an additional source of scattering processes, resulting in increased spectral broadening. While no exponential quantum advantage is expected in the single-particle approximation, the framework naturally extends to many-body regimes where classical simulations face the hierarchy problem and exponential scaling and provable quantum advantage will be possible. The quantum simulations considered in this work capture central elements of semiconductor spectroscopy, the aspects such as open quantum systems, light-matter interactions, statistical mechanics, non-equilibrium quantum dynamics, and many-body physics. As such, it provides a physically motivated and scalable model for benchmarking quantum computers in applications to complex, real-world problems.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.04958"></a>
### [Cooper quartets and fractional vortices in frustrated Josephson junction dice arrays](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04958v1)

**Authors:** Erik Lennart Weerda, Olav F. Syljuåsen, Matteo Rizzi, Michele Burrello  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.04958v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `analog quantum simulation` **3/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `exotic spin models in light-matter` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.04958_figures/2606.04958_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1. Illustration of a JJA in dice lattice geometry. The red arrows show the unit-cell vectors. By applying a perpendicular magnetic field to the array, a magnetic flux Φ can be threaded through every plaquette.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2606.04958_figures/2606.04958_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2. Critical currents as a function of frustration f for a 50 × 50 dice lattice calculated using the phase-bias relaxation method. The inset shows corresponding results for a 100×100 square lattice. The critical currents are normalized by Ic(0). Each purple dot shows the critical current obtained from a single phase configuration. A lower bound on the true critical current can be estimated by taking the maximum of these values (black line). The fringes at low values of f are finite- size effects.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2606.04958_figures/2606.04958_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 3. Energy per island (colored curve) and its derivative (grey) vs. f for a 50×50 dice lattice without source and drain islands, i.e. free boundary conditions, obtained by simulated annealing and a subsequent relaxation step, see App. A.</sub>

<sub>Figure 4. Typical current-phase relation for f = 1/3 for a phase-biased supercurrent between source (purple) and drain (pink) islands situated in the middle of a 50 × 50 dice lattice. I is expressed in units of 2e</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2606.04958_figures/2606.04958_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 5. Harmonics of the supercurrent across a single rhom- bus as a function of f. </details>

**Summary.** This paper analyzes superconducting behavior in frustrated Josephson junction dice arrays, predicting that the system enters a $4e$ superconducting phase when frustrated at $f=1/3$. By using advanced numerical and theoretical methods, the authors confirm this $4e$ state through the dominance of the second supercurrent harmonic and the algebraic decay of the corresponding correlation function.

**Why it may be interesting.** The work provides a concrete, tunable platform (JJ arrays) to study fractional charge carriers and topological order, bridging concepts from superconductivity, frustrated magnetism, and quantum phase transitions.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the emergence of exotic superconducting phases mediated by Cooper quartets ($4e$) in frustrated Josephson junction (JJ) dice arrays.

**Main result.** The critical current peaks at a frustration of $f=1/3$ are linked to a superconductor-insulator transition compatible with half-vortex deconfinement, confirming the onset of a $4e$ superconducting phase.

**Method.** The research employs a combination of numerical simulations (relaxation dynamics), Monte Carlo techniques, and advanced theoretical tools like infinite tensor networks and Renormalization Group (RG) analysis.

**Model / system.** The system is a Josephson junction array (JJA) on a dice lattice, modeled by an XY Hamiltonian. The analysis focuses on the effect of magnetic frustration ($f$) and the competition between $2e$ (Cooper pair) and $4e$ (Cooper quartet) superconducting correlations.

**Key observables.** Critical current ($I_c$), critical temperature ($T_c$), supercurrent harmonics ($I_1$ vs $I_2$), and correlation functions ($	ilde{C}_{2e}(r)$ vs $	ilde{C}_{4e}(r)$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Magnetic frustration ($f$), charging energy ($E_C$), Josephson energy ($E_J$), and temperature ($T$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis often assumes negligible charging energy for the classical XY model description, though later sections incorporate $E_C$ effects.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the dice lattice geometry, the dominance of the $I_2$ (quartet) component at $f=1/3$, and the algebraic decay of the $4e$ correlation function ($	ilde{C}_{4e}(r)$).

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from establishing the physical model and analyzing the critical current dependence on frustration ($f$), to characterizing the supercurrent harmonics ($I_1$ vs $I_2$), and finally delving into the quantum nature using correlation functions and RG analysis to confirm the $4e$ phase.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Superconductivity mediated by Cooper quartets of charge 4e is a phenomenon of key importance for both the understanding of certain exotic superconductors and the engineering of quantum memories protected at the hardware level by topological order. Josephson junction arrays in the shape of the dice lattice constitute one of the main candidates for the realization of this phase of matter. Here, we analyze numerical signatures of the emergence of this exotic phase when superconducting dice arrays are frustrated by inserting one third of a flux quantum per rhombic plaquette. We adopt simulations of relaxation dynamics to study the critical current of such devices and analyse the Fourier decomposition of their bulk supercurrents. A further characterization of these systems at finite temperature is obtained through Monte Carlo techniques and two-dimensional infinite tensor networks. Our results indicate that the observed peaks of the critical current and temperature at frustration 1/3 correspond to a superconductor-insulator phase transition compatible with the deconfinement of half-vortices, while the low-temperature correlation functions of the model confirm the onset of a 4e superconducting phase mediated by Cooper quartets. We finally address the effects of Josephson energy and flux disorder typical of experimental arrays, and comment on the role of charging energies in the corresponding two-dimensional quantum model.

</details>
