<a id="paper-2606.05066"></a>
### [Fermionic non-Gaussianity via Bell sampling: monotones and efficient quantum algorithms](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.05066v1)

**Authors:** Poetri Sonya Tarabunga  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.05066v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **3/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **2/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **1/5** · `quantum measurements` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.05066_figures/2606.05066_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. A t-doped Gaussian protocol. Blue boxes E0, E1, . . . , Et denote arbitrary Gaussian protocols, possibly with post-selection; orange boxes W1, W2, . . . , Wt are non-Gaussian gates, each acting on two qubits (four Majorana modes) in arbitrary locations.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This work develops a resource theory for fermionic non-Gaussianity using the 'bridge degree' monotone, derived from the eigenvalue structure of a specific operator accessible via Bell sampling. It proves this degree is irreversible under Gaussian operations, yielding strong lower bounds for tasks like state preparation. The paper culminates in efficient, sample-efficient quantum algorithms to test for Gaussianity and certify the 2-design property using this measure.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a concrete, measurable, and theoretically robust resource measure (the bridge degree) for fermionic quantum computation, directly linking structural properties to computational resource costs.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper aims to quantify fermionic non-Gaussianity, which is a crucial resource for achieving quantum computational advantage beyond classical simulation capabilities.

**Main result.** They introduce the 'bridge degree' as a novel, computable, and irreversible non-Gaussianity monotone, establishing strong lower bounds on the non-Gaussian gate cost for state preparation and 2-design certification.

**Method.** The analysis centers on the eigenvalue structure of the 'bridge operator' ($\Lambda$) acting on two copies of the fermionic state, which is accessible via Bell sampling measurements.

**Model / system.** The system is based on fermionic quantum computation, utilizing fermionic Gaussian unitaries (matchgates) acting on $n$-mode fermionic states, often restricted to 'even' states.

**Key observables.** Bridge degree ($\Lambda_d(\psi)$), approximate bridge degree ($\Lambda_{d,\epsilon}(\psi)$), and the bridge spectral distribution ($p_\psi(\lambda)$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The complexity scales are related to $n$ (number of modes), $\epsilon$ (approximation error), and $\delta$ (failure probability).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The primary assumption is that the resource theory framework can be successfully mapped onto the eigenvalue structure of the bridge operator, and that Bell sampling provides an efficient measurement tool.

**Figures summary.** Not specified, but the notes reference TABLE I summarizing various non-Gaussianity monotones and their properties.

**Paper structure.** The paper develops the bridge degree monotone, proves its monotonicity under Gaussian protocols (establishing irreversibility), derives lower bounds for state preparation and 2-design costs, and finally presents efficient Bell-sampling algorithms for testing Gaussianity and 2-design properties.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Fermionic non-Gaussianity is an essential resource for unlocking the full computational power of fermionic quantum platforms. In this work we develop monotones and efficient quantum algorithms for fermionic non-Gaussianity, all built on the eigenvalue structure of the operator $Λ= \sum_{j=1}^{2n}γ_j\otimesγ_j$ defined on two copies of an $n$-mode fermionic state, accessible via Bell sampling. In particular, we introduce the \emph{bridge degree} of even pure states, a novel non-Gaussianity monotone defined as the largest eigenvalue sector of $Λ$ populated by two copies of the state. Our key technical result is that the bridge degree is non-increasing under post-selected Gaussian protocols, which yields no-go theorems for Gaussian conversion stronger than those obtainable from previously known monotones and shows that the resource theory of fermionic non-Gaussianity is irreversible in the exact-conversion setting. Beyond this, the bridge degree exhibits several further features: it (i) is easy to compute, (ii) is efficiently witnessed through Bell sampling, (iii) lower-bounds the non-Gaussian gate complexity of state preparation, (iv) controls the non-Gaussian gate complexity of producing quantum state designs, and (v) naturally extends to mixed states via the Choi--Jamiołkowski isomorphism. We further develop an approximate variant together with an efficiently measurable lower bound, yielding an experimentally certifiable lower bound on the non-Gaussian cost of approximately preparing any state, based directly on Bell-sampling data. Finally, the same eigenvalue structure underlies two Bell-sampling-based algorithmic primitives, both with polynomial sample complexity: a two-copy Gaussianity test with perfect completeness, optimal among two-copy tests sharing this property, and a test for the state $2$-design property of matchgate-invariant ensembles.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates a high-dimensional Quantum Key Distribution (HD-QKD) protocol by directly exploiting all core modes of a multicore fiber network. By achieving a record-high key rate over a deployed campus link, the authors prove that core-mode encoding is a practical and efficient method for building next-generation quantum-secure communication networks.

**Why it may be interesting.** The work directly addresses the practical implementation of quantum communication protocols in existing infrastructure, providing concrete rate benchmarks that are highly relevant to quantum network engineering.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To demonstrate high-dimensional quantum key distribution (HD-QKD) with high rates and robustness over deployed, real-world communication infrastructure.

**Main result.** The authors achieved a record finite-key rate of $6.19 	imes 10^{-3}$ bits/pulse at 10 dB loss, establishing core-mode encoding as a viable architecture for high-rate quantum-secure communications.

**Method.** The protocol uses a two-decoy finite-key method, employing inverse Chernoff bounds to estimate the secret key length, which is then applied to experimentally measured detection counts.

**Model / system.** The system utilizes Multicore Fibers (MCFs) as the physical platform, encoding information across the full set of available core modes ($d=4$). The setup involves complex phase and spatial modulation across these multiple cores.

**Key observables.** Composable finite-key rate ($R = 6.19 	imes 10^{-3}$ bits/pulse), Quantum Bit Error Rate (QBER), and state fidelity ($ar{F} = 0.96 \pm 0.01$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Dimension ($d=4$), Channel Loss (tested at 10 dB), and security parameters ($\epsilon_{sec}, \epsilon_{cor}$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis uses advanced finite-key bounding techniques (Zhang et al. [27]) that avoid assumptions of Gaussian fluctuation profiles, which is crucial for accuracy.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate polarization stability over time, QBER distributions fitted with Beta distributions, and comparisons of achieved key rates against previous state-of-the-art demonstrations.

**Paper structure.** The paper details the physical setup (MCF deployment), the theoretical framework for finite-key rate estimation using decoy states and Chernoff bounds, the experimental measurements across various link lengths, and the final comparison of the achieved rate against benchmarks.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum key distribution (QKD) provides information-theoretic security rooted in quantum physics, while high-dimensional (HD) encoding increases both noise tolerance and secret-key yield. Multicore fibers (MCFs), a leading platform for next-generation telecom networks, are a natural substrate for HD-QKD. Field demonstrations over deployed MCFs have so far relied on a hybrid qudit encoding strategy that combines two path (core modes) with the time-bin photonic degree of freedom, rather than exploiting the full set of available core modes. Although practical, this approach incurs intrinsic efficiency penalties that grow with dimension. Here we implement a four-dimensional ($d=4$) QKD protocol that directly exploits the full set of core modes of a four-core MCF, operating over an installed MCF network across the Universidad de Concepción campus under continuous environmental perturbations. We further benchmark the scheme using superconducting nanowire detectors at $10\,$dB channel loss, achieving a composable finite-key rate of $R = 6.19\times 10^{-3}\,$bits/pulse, the highest per-pulse rate reported to date for HD-QKD at comparable loss. This result establishes core-mode encoding as a viable architecture for realistic, high-rate quantum-secure communications.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper reports on the experimental realization of a highly sensitive electric-field sensor using Rydberg-atom Electromagnetically Induced Transparency (EIT) in a rubidium vapor cell. By meticulously engineering the atomic coherence and employing superheterodyne detection, the authors successfully suppressed technical noise sources. They achieved an electric-field sensitivity matching the fundamental photon shot-noise limit, marking a significant step toward practical quantum-limited sensing.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant as it pushes the boundaries of quantum sensing by experimentally verifying the theoretical limit imposed by quantum noise (shot noise) in a complex atomic system, providing a blueprint for quantum-limited measurements.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The primary goal is to develop and experimentally demonstrate a highly sensitive quantum electric-field sensor using Rydberg-atom electrometry that approaches the fundamental photon shot-noise limit.

**Main result.** The authors achieved an electric-field sensitivity of 12.5(8) nV cm^-1 Hz^-1/2, which is in close agreement with the calculated photon shot-noise limit, establishing a practical route for quantum-noise-limited sensing.

**Method.** The experiment utilizes a superheterodyne detection scheme applied to Rydberg Electromagnetically Induced Transparency (EIT) in an atomic vapor cell, requiring active compensation of external magnetic and laser noise.

**Model / system.** The system is based on a cascade-type EIT scheme in an 85Rb atomic vapor cell, leveraging the strong, controllable interactions in Rydberg states to enhance the electric-field coupling.

**Key observables.** Electric-field sensitivity (nV cm^-1 Hz^-1/2), EIT linewidth (achieved 1.6 MHz), and the spectral slope ($\kappa_0$) of the EIT response.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Photon Shot-Noise (PSN) limit, Rydberg-EIT linewidth, and the optimal probe power for maximizing signal-to-noise ratio.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes that the dominant noise source limiting sensitivity is the probe-photon shot noise, and that the system can be engineered to minimize technical noise sources like magnetic field fluctuations.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the energy-level diagram and experimental setup, compare EIT spectra with and without magnetic field compensation (showing linewidth narrowing), and detail the superheterodyne time-domain response and the dependence of sensitivity on probe power.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first establishing the basic EIT sensing mechanism, then detailing the necessity and implementation of magnetic field compensation to narrow the linewidth, and finally optimizing the superheterodyne detection scheme to quantify the sensitivity relative to the theoretical PSN limit.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Rydberg-atom electrometry is a core technique in the development of highly sensitive quantum electric-field sensors. Its sensitivity based on atom-photon interaction is typically limited by photon shot-noise (PSN) and spectral broadenings. Here, we experimentally demonstrate a near PSN-limited Rydberg electrometry from a 85Rb atomic vapor cell. By engineering atomic coherence through control of residual magnetic fields and laser frequency noise, we achieve the Rydberg electromagnetically induced transparency (EIT) with the narrow linewidth of 1.6 MHz, yielding an enhanced spectral slope for high-sensitivity Rydberg-EIT electrometry. Under optimized superheterodyne detection conditions, we obtain an electric-field sensitivity of 12.5(8) nV cm^-1 Hz^-1/2 at 37 GHz, in close agreement with the calculated PSN limit. These results provide direct experimental evidence of the high-sensitive quantum electrometry and establish a practical route toward quantum-noise-limited Rydberg electrometry.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper proposes a detailed quantum control protocol for manipulating nuclear spins in boron vacancy centers within hBN. By leveraging the electron spin as a mediator, the authors engineer controlled interactions to implement high-fidelity single- and multi-qubit gates. The results demonstrate that the scheme is fast enough to evade electron spin decoherence, paving the way for quantum computation in this solid-state platform.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work directly addresses the challenge of scalable quantum memory and gate operations in solid-state systems, which is a major focus area for quantum information scientists.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper aims to develop a robust protocol for implementing arbitrary single- and multi-qubit quantum gates on the nuclear spins within a hexagonal boron nitride (hBN) platform.

**Main result.** A protocol was established that achieves high gate fidelities (up to 99% for single-qubit gates) with short gate execution times (< 300 ns), enabling practical quantum computation using boron vacancy centers.

**Method.** The protocol utilizes the electron spin as an auxiliary qubit, employing multi-tone continuous drives and Hahn echo pulse sequences to engineer and control the electron-nuclear coupling.

**Model / system.** The system is based on negatively charged boron vacancy centers ($	ext{V}_	ext{B}^-$) in hBN, modeled by a total Hamiltonian involving electron ($\hat{H}_e$), nuclear ($\hat{H}_n$), and interaction ($\hat{H}_i$) terms. The dynamics are analyzed by reducing the system to an effective two-level electron subspace.

**Key observables.** Gate fidelities (up to 99% single-qubit, 95% multi-qubit), gate execution duration (< 300 ns), and the coherence function $C_{WT}(\omega)$ for spin distinction.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Moderate background magnetic field ($B \sim 0.1-1 	ext{ T}$), and the decoherence rate ($\Gamma$) which must be overcome by fast gate operations.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes the ability to suppress undesired electron-nuclear interactions using Hahn echo sequences and relies on the electron spin being fast enough to execute gates before decoherence.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the spin cluster geometry, the dependence of gate infidelity on time and decoherence rate, and the coherence function showing distinct peaks for individual spin addressing.

**Paper structure.** The work progresses by first defining the Hamiltonian and the physical system, then detailing the gate implementation protocol using RF drives and Hahn echo sequences, and finally presenting numerical results demonstrating high fidelity and fast operation times.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Due to its localized nature and controllability, the negatively charged boron vacancy centers (V$_\text{B}^-$) in hexagonal boron nitride (hBN) are a promising spin platform for accessing its neighboring nuclei with potential for performing quantum computational tasks. However, the methods of utilizing and manipulating the nuclear spins are still lacking. In this work, we propose a protocol for the preparation of single- and multi-qubit gates on the nuclear spins, utilizing the electron spin as an auxiliary qubit. By applying a background magnetic field and a multi-tone continuous drive, we show that the electron spin coupling to the nuclei can be efficiently engineered. This allows for suppressing the undesired electron-nuclear interactions through the Hahn echo pulse sequence. The target gates are then implemented by employing proper RF drives. Our numerical results for realistic parameters show gate fidelities as high as $99\%$ for single-qubit and $95\%$ for multi-qubit gates. With the gate execution durations being less than $300$ ns, our protocol evades electron spin decoherence effects. Therefore, our scheme sets the stage for the practical application of V$_\text{B}^-$ in hBN for quantum computation purposes.

</details>

### [Experimental observation of three-dimensional Anderson localization of electromagnetic waves](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04897v1)

**Summary.** This paper experimentally demonstrates three-dimensional Anderson localization of microwaves in a disordered metal aggregate. By measuring the time-dependent transverse spreading of the wave beam, the authors distinguish between normal diffusive transport and localization, which is marked by the saturation of the beam width. This provides a robust, measurable confirmation of a key concept in condensed matter physics.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a direct, measurable realization of wave localization in a solid-state system, offering an experimental platform to test theories of wave interference and transport in highly disordered media, which has parallels to quantum wave function localization.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the experimental observation of three-dimensional (3D) Anderson localization of electromagnetic waves, a phenomenon predicted to occur in disordered media.

**Main result.** The authors report an unambiguous experimental proof of 3D Anderson localization by observing the temporal arrest of transverse wave spreading in a highly disordered microwave medium.

**Method.** The study combines microwave measurements using narrowband filters with theoretical analysis based on the Self-Consistent Theory (SCT) of localization.

**Model / system.** The system is a disordered medium composed of randomly packed aluminum particles (metallic scatterers) through which microwaves propagate. The disorder is controlled by the metal volume fraction ($\phi$).

**Key observables.** Transverse spreading width squared, $w^2(t)$, and the total transmission spectrum $T(f)$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Metal filling fractions ($\phi=15\%$ for diffusive, $\phi=60\%$ for localized); localization length ($\xi$); sample thickness ($L$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The measurements assume the ability to exclude broadband inelastic and nonlinear signals, and the analysis relies on the SCT framework.

**Figures summary.** Figures show comparisons of transmission spectra and the time evolution of the beam width ($w^2(t)$) for both diffusive and localized regimes, confirming saturation in the localized case.

**Paper structure.** The paper establishes the theoretical background (Anderson localization in 3D), details the experimental setup using microwave transmission, presents diagnostics by comparing time-dependent beam spreading in different disorder regimes, and validates the results using SCT and numerical simulations.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

A prominent phenomenon in contemporary condensed matter physics is Anderson localization -- suppression of wave propagation in disordered systems as a result of interference effects. Despite being observed with various types of waves over the years, all prior attempts to reach Anderson localization of light in three-dimensional systems have been hampered by experimental artifacts. Here, we report an unambiguous experimental proof of three-dimensional Anderson localization of microwaves in disordered metal aggregates. By studying samples with different metal volume fractions, we show a clear difference between diffusive and localized behaviors, and the latter is confirmed by a scaling analysis of transmitted beam width in excellent agreement with theoretical and numerical results. Our demonstration opens avenues for both fundamental studies and practical applications of this extraordinary phenomenon.

</details>

**Summary.** The authors present a superconducting flux-tunable resonator (tRes) magnetometer designed for ultra-sensitive magnetic measurements at cryogenic temperatures. By coupling a SQUID to a microwave resonator, the device achieves a superior combination of fast readout speed and high flux sensitivity. This versatile tool allows for quantitative characterization of magnetic phenomena, including superconducting flux memory dynamics.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a solid-state, high-bandwidth platform for quantum sensing, which is highly relevant to AMO physics research involving magnetic characterization of quantum materials or superconducting qubits.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The goal is to develop a highly sensitive, fast, and non-invasive magnetometer capable of operating at milli-Kelvin temperatures, surpassing the limitations of conventional superconducting quantum interference devices (SQUIDs).

**Main result.** The superconducting flux-tunable resonator (tRes) was demonstrated as a versatile magnetometer, achieving high Signal-to-Background Ratios (SBR) and successfully characterizing superconducting flux memory devices.

**Method.** The technique involves coupling the SQUID's flux-dependent inductance to a microwave resonator, causing a measurable shift in the resonator's resonant frequency ($	ext{f}_{	ext{resonance}}$) when exposed to an external magnetic flux.

**Model / system.** The physical platform is a superconducting flux-tunable resonator (tRes) integrated circuit, which couples the non-linear inductance of an RF SQUID to an LC resonator. This allows the system to combine the speed of an LC circuit with the high flux sensitivity of a SQUID.

**Key observables.** Resonant frequency shift ($\Delta f$), Signal-to-Background Ratio (SBR), and the extracted mutual inductance ($M$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Operation at milli-Kelvin temperatures ($	ext{mK}$), $	ext{MHz}$ magnetic sampling rates, and the magnetic flux quantum ($\Phi_0$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis often relies on the SQUID operating in the non-hysteretic regime ($eta 	ext{L} < 1$) and uses the SBR metric due to the spectroscopic nature of the measurement.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the $	ext{|S21|}$ dip at resonance, the periodic modulation curve relating load current to resonant frequency, and advanced measurements like 2D iso-frequency scans and flux memory hysteresis loops.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from introducing the fundamental $	ext{tRes}$ concept and its frequency modulation readout (Fig. 2), to demonstrating advanced measurement modalities like flux superposition and characterizing superconducting flux memory cells (Figs. 3 & 4).

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The quantum-technology revolution is reshaping computing, sensing, and communication. In magnetometry, recent advances leverage precise control of spin qubits and color centers in solid-state crystals for mesoscopic-scale sensing. Yet at very low temperatures, superconducting sensing technology remains unrivaled because of its non-invasiveness and higher sensitivity. Here we describe a class of superconducting sensors that offers low loss and quantum non-demolition measurement characteristics. We designed and fabricated a superconducting flux-tunable resonator (tRes) in a superconducting chip foundry and matured it to a level that combines the speed of an inductor-capacitor circuit with the flux sensitivity of a superconducting quantum interference device (SQUID) to perform magnetometry at milli-kelvin temperature to investigate targets. We introduce its fundamental functionality readily at MHz magnetic sampling rate, showcase two measurement modalities, and investigate three circuits with gradually increasing complexity to extract target-specific information. The combination of high sensitivity and fast readout characteristics make tRes an attractive and versatile magnetometer.

</details>

### [Continuous-Variable Quantum State Tomography Enabled by Quantum Mirrors](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04277v1)

**Authors:** Mariano Uria, Amaru Moya, Carla Hermann-Avigliano, Pablo Solano, Aldo Delgado  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.04277v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `quantum measurements` **3/5** · `interference shaping light` **2/5**

(b) The control atom in state |g⟩ generates complete transmission of the modes going through the mirror. **Summary.** This paper proposes using quantum mirrors to overcome the exponential complexity of continuous-variable quantum state tomography. By coupling the photonic state to a control atom, the entire state information can be read out solely by measuring the atomic system. This offers a robust, experimentally feasible framework for characterizing complex quantum states of light.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a highly practical and theoretically rigorous method for quantum state verification in continuous variables, directly leveraging atomic coherence to overcome measurement limitations in quantum optics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Continuous-Variable Quantum State Tomography (CV-QST) suffers from exponentially growing sample complexity using conventional methods.

**Main result.** The use of quantum mirrors allows for full photonic state characterization (including Wigner and P functions) by measuring only the coupled control atomic system.

**Method.** The protocol maps the unknown photonic state information onto measurable interference patterns and probabilities derived from the control atom's state.

**Model / system.** The system involves an ancillary two-level control atom coupled to photonic modes via a Quantum Mirror (QM) interaction, which is modeled using unitary operators derived from beam splitter relations.

**Key observables.** Husimi Q function, Glauber–Sudarshan P function, Wavefunction $\psi_r(s)$, and Wigner function $W(\alpha)$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Relative phase shifts ($\phi$), displacement parameters ($\alpha, \gamma$), and the atomic state basis ($|eangle, |gangle$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes the ability to implement independent phase shifts and relies on the QM action transferring complete state information onto the atom.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the quantum mirror action, the quantum circuit implementing the unitary operation, and schematics for wavefunction and Wigner function reconstruction protocols.

**Paper structure.** The paper develops the formalism for the QM operator, details measurement protocols for various state representations (Q, P, Wigner), and mathematically links these measurements back to the unknown photonic state $ho$.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

In quantum technologies, continuous-variable systems offer advantages over their discrete counterparts. However, continuous-variable tomography suffers from exponentially growing sample complexity. We propose protocols using quantum mirrors to transfer the complete information of incident photonic states onto a control atomic system. This enables full photonic state characterization through measurements on the control atom alone, realized via kernel functions, direct wavefunction reconstruction, and pointwise Wigner function measurements. Our approach overcomes the limitations of conventional photon counting, statistical inference, and inverse transformation, providing a robust framework for benchmarking and verifying non-Gaussian states in continuous-variable quantum optics.

</details>

### [Minimal superfluid vortices in chiral perturbation theory](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04556v1)

**Authors:** Fabrizio Canfora, Martın Garrido, Massimo Mannarelli, Anibal Neira  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum gases · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.04556v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

**Summary.** This theoretical paper studies superfluid vortices in a pion condensate using Chiral Perturbation Theory. By applying the BPS bound, the authors derive the energy cost and quantized angular momentum associated with these vortices. The results confirm superfluidity and estimate the critical rotation frequency required for vortex formation.

**Why it may be interesting.** The mathematical framework used (BPS bounds, vortex solutions) is highly relevant to understanding topological defects and superfluid dynamics in ultracold atomic systems, providing a theoretical analogue to BEC vortex physics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates the properties of superfluid vortices that can form in a pion condensed phase, which is analogous to a Bose-Einstein condensate (BEC). It aims to determine the minimal energy required for these vortices to nucleate.

**Main result.** The theory confirms that the pion condensate supports quantized vortices, and the energy cost scales linearly with the winding number, suggesting superfluid behavior. A critical rotation frequency for nucleation is estimated.

**Method.** The analysis employs leading order Chiral Perturbation Theory (ChPT) and utilizes the Bogomol'nyi-Prasad-Sommerfield (BPS) bound to simplify the free-energy minimization problem for vortex solutions.

**Model / system.** The system is a pion gas in the pion condensed phase, modeled using an effective Lagrangian derived from ChPT. The analysis focuses on the field configurations ($\alpha, \Phi, \Theta$) that describe the superfluid order parameter.

**Key observables.** Quantized angular momentum ($L_z$), free energy ($F$), critical rotation frequency ($\Omega_c$), and the isospin number density ($n_I$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Pion mass ($m_\pi$), isospin chemical potential ($\mu_I$), and the winding number ($n$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes vanishing temperature, uses the leading order $\chi$PT action, and often simplifies the geometry by assuming cylindrical symmetry and $\varphi_3=0$.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the radial dependence of energy density, pressure, and number density for a central vortex, and show the estimated critical frequency dependence on radius.

**Paper structure.** The paper follows a logical progression: establishing the $\chi$PT setup, deriving the BPS bound, constructing the vortex solutions by solving simplified differential equations, and finally calculating the total free energy and the critical nucleation frequency.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We derive some properties of rotational vortices in the pion condensed phase. Employing leading order chiral perturbation theory we determine the minimal energy condition for vortex nucleation. Vortices have quantized angular momentum along the rotation axis, an hallmark of superfluidity, and self-confine pions. The critical rotation frequency for vortex nucleation is estimated.

</details>

### [Soliton-antisoliton pairs in the supersymmetric gapped phase of an interacting Majorana chain](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.05095v1)

**Authors:** Alberto Nocera, Mobin Shakeri, Armin Rahmani, Ian Affleck  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.05095v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `analog quantum simulation` **3/5** · `Frenkel-Kontorova` **1/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

The ground-state energy exhibits a linear dependence on ∆in the or- dered phase for small ∆. A positive (negative) ∆lowers the energy in the even (odd) fermion parity sector, leading to a unique ground state with even (odd) parity. These results were obtained with the energy scale t set to one.</sub>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2606.05095_figures/2606.05095_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 5: Density of Dirac fermions for positive ∆(panel a) and negative ∆(panel b) in each parity sector. The value of |∆| has been taken smaller than the level crossing shown in Fig. 4(a). Regardless of the sign of ∆, the even and odd parity sectors have 〈nc〉≃0, 〈nd〉≃1/2 and 〈nc〉≃1/2, 〈nd〉≃0, respectively.</sub>

</details>

<sub>Figure 7: (a–b) Ground-state and first excited state energies in the two fermion parity sectors as a function of the soliton location m. All data are for L = 120 Dirac fermions and SA pinning field ∆SA = 0.001. **Summary.** This work analyzes an interacting Majorana chain near a supersymmetric tricritical point, demonstrating that supersymmetry persists into the gapped phase. The key finding is that the low-energy excitations are soliton-antisoliton pairs, which bind localized Majorana modes and collectively form a nonlocal Dirac fermion. The study uses advanced numerical diagnostics to map out the phase boundaries and characterize these emergent quasiparticles.

**Why it may be interesting.** The connection between topological order, emergent supersymmetry, and localized excitations (solitons) in a 1D strongly correlated system provides a rich theoretical playground relevant to understanding quantum materials.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates how supersymmetry (SUSY) manifests and what the nature of excitations are in the gapped phase adjacent to the supersymmetric tricritical Ising (TCI) point of an interacting Majorana chain.

**Main result.** SUSY is shown to persist in the gapped phase, evidenced by the continuous decay of a SUSY diagnostic ratio, and the low-energy excitations are identified as soliton-antisoliton pairs forming nonlocal Dirac fermions.

**Method.** The study employs numerical techniques like DMRG, analyzing lattice supercharge operators and introducing symmetry-breaking perturbations to probe the system's ground and excited states.

**Model / system.** The system is a strongly interacting chain of Majorana fermions, modeled by the O’Brien–Fendley (OF) Hamiltonian, which exhibits a transition between a critical Ising phase and a gapped, ordered phase.

**Key observables.** The SUSY diagnostic ratio ($R$), the energy splitting ($\Delta E_{0,1}$) of the first excited state, and the difference in Majorana Green's functions between even and odd fermion-parity sectors.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Interaction strength ($g/t$), the SA-pinning perturbation strength ($\Delta_{SA}$), and the separation distance ($m$) of the soliton-antisoliton pair.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes the applicability of lattice supercharge operators flowing to field-theoretic operators and focuses on the lowest excited states.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the phase diagram, the behavior of the SUSY diagnostic ratio $R$ near the TCI point, and the energy dependence of the first excited state on the SA-pinning field.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first establishing the physical model and the SUSY diagnostic ($R$), then characterizing the TCI transition, and finally using SA-pinning perturbations to confirm the existence and nature of the soliton-antisoliton excitations in the gapped phase.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

A strongly interacting chain of Majorana fermions realizes the supersymmetric tricritical Ising phase, with supersymmetry (SUSY) extending into a symmetry-broken ordered phase adjacent to the tricritical point. Although the signatures of SUSY at the tricritical point are well understood, their behavior in the gapped phase remains less clear. Here, we address two key questions: how SUSY manifests in the gapped phase and what is the nature of the excitations in this phase. We show that, in the thermodynamic limit, a conventional SUSY diagnostic that remains finite at the tricritical point diverges immediately on the Ising side, yet decays continuously to zero deeper in the gapped phase, signaling the persistence of SUSY. Focusing on the lowest excited states in the supersymmetric gapped regime, we find that the excitations consist of soliton-antisoliton pairs separating distinct ordered regions. Each soliton binds an emergent localized Majorana mode, and together the pair forms a nonlocal Dirac fermion. The occupation of this Dirac mode distinguishes eigenstates with even and odd fermion parity.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2606.04430"></a>
### [Atom Interferometry with Transverse Optical Modes](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04430v1)

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates atom interferometry using the transverse phase structure of optical beams, specifically utilizing the helical phase windings of HyGG beams. The authors successfully measured rotations and provided a detailed theoretical and experimental characterization of how thermal motion causes spatially-dependent decoherence in the interferometer contrast. This advances the field by establishing a robust method for high-precision sensing using structured light.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant as it pushes the metrological capabilities of atom interferometry by systematically incorporating and quantifying the effects of transverse optical structure (OAM) and thermal decoherence, providing a roadmap for next-generation quantum sensors.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To develop and experimentally demonstrate atom interferometry utilizing the transverse phase profile (orbital angular momentum) of structured optical modes, moving beyond traditional longitudinal phase measurements.

**Main result.** The interferometer successfully measures rotations with a sensitivity scaling linearly with the orbital angular momentum quantum number ($\ell$) and interrogation time ($t_{	ext{int}}$). Furthermore, the study derived and confirmed an analytical expression for the spatially-varying thermal decoherence of the fringe visibility.

**Method.** The authors employed Ramsey interferometry with cold $^{87}	ext{Rb}$ atoms, using Hypergeometric Gaussian (HyGG) beams to imprint helical phase windings. They measured rotation by inducing physical rotations of the optical fields and characterized decoherence by measuring fringe contrast reduction.

**Model / system.** The physical system is atom interferometry using cold $^{87}	ext{Rb}$ atoms and structured light fields (HyGG beams). The theoretical analysis models the phase accumulation ($\Delta\phi$) and contrast ($C$) by considering the dependence on atomic velocities ($\dot{r}, \dot{	heta}$) in the presence of a helical phase front.

**Key observables.** Rotational phase shift ($\phi$), interferometer fringe contrast, and the measured dephasing radius ($R$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Orbital Angular Momentum quantum number ($\ell$), interrogation time ($t_{	ext{int}}$), and the atomic temperature (which dictates decoherence).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on approximations for the change in angle ($\Delta	heta$) during ballistic expansion and assumes that the primary limitation for sensitivity is thermal phase diffusion.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate measured Ramsey fringes showing dependence on $\ell$ and $\Omega$, and plots confirm the linear scaling of the measured dephasing radius ($R$) with the predicted analytical scaling law derived from the thermal motion.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from demonstrating the basic rotation measurement using transverse modes to quantitatively characterizing the thermal decoherence. It develops the theoretical framework by modeling the phase accumulation in polar coordinates, leading to the final analytical expression for the contrast reduction due to velocity spread.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We experimentally demonstrate atom interferometry using the transverse phase profile of an optical mode. As proof-of-principle, we use the helical phase windings of Hypergeometric Gaussian beams for Ramsey interferometry with ensembles of ballistically-expanding cold Rb87 atoms, and we show that the interferometer can measure rotations induced by a motor with a sensitivity that scales linearly with orbital angular momentum and interferometer time. We characterize the thermal decoherence of the interferometer, deriving and experimentally confirming a closed-form expression for the spatially-varying interferometer visibility arising near the singularity of the helical phase winding, motivating the use of condensed atoms in ring-shaped traps.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

**Summary.** This paper introduces a novel, efficient calibration technique for quantum gates by exploiting the low-rank structure of the gate fidelity Hessian. By restricting optimization to the principal subspace defined by this Hessian, the authors successfully demonstrate high-fidelity controlled-Z gates on neutral atoms. This method offers a general, robust framework for improving quantum gate performance in complex atomic platforms.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a powerful, physically motivated mathematical tool (Hessian analysis) to simplify the notoriously difficult problem of optimizing complex quantum control pulses, directly applicable to state-of-the-art neutral atom quantum computing.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the challenge of calibrating high-dimensional waveforms required for optimal quantum control to achieve high-fidelity multi-qubit gates.

**Main result.** A low-rank Hessian optimization method is established as an efficient calibration strategy, achieving high gate fidelities (e.g., 0.99902) with demonstrated robustness to environmental variations.

**Method.** The core method involves analyzing the fidelity Hessian of the gate error, exploiting its low-rank structure to restrict optimization to a small, physically relevant principal subspace.

**Model / system.** The work uses neutral atom qubits, specifically $^{171}	ext{Yb}$ nuclear-spin qubits, to implement an amplitude-robust controlled-Z (CZ) gate. The dynamics are modeled using optimal control theory applied to a multi-level atomic system.

**Key observables.** Gate fidelity ($F$), raw and post-selected error rates ($\epsilon$), and the eigenvalues/eigenvectors of the fidelity Hessian.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The rank ($r$) of the Hessian, which is determined by the number of accessible leakage and coherent error channels; laser power variation (up to 20%).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on a second-order expansion of the infidelity in the control parameter error ($\delta\vec{\Omega}^T H \delta\vec{\Omega}$), and the low-rank structure is assumed to capture the dominant error channels.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the CZ gate setup, the eigenvalue spectrum showing the few nonzero eigenvalues, the decomposition of the error into parallel and perpendicular components, and comparisons of optimization strategies.

**Paper structure.** The paper develops the theory by analyzing the fidelity Hessian, applies it to the CZ gate, demonstrates the optimization protocol using experimental feedback, and verifies the robustness and error correction capabilities.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum optimal control can produce fast and robust multi-qubit gates, but experimentally calibrating the resulting high-dimensional waveforms remains challenging because direct searches over large parameter spaces converge slowly. Building on the low-rank structure of quantum-control landscapes, we develop and benchmark a Hessian-based calibration method for optimal-control gates. The method identifies the few waveform directions that affect fidelity to leading order, with the number of directions set by the accessible leakage and coherent error channels, and optimizes only within this principal space using closed-loop experimental feedback. We apply this approach to an amplitude-robust controlled-Z gate on metastable-state 171Yb nuclear-spin qubits. Experimentally, we verify the predicted Hessian-sensitive directions and demonstrate rapid convergence of the optimization protocol. The optimized gate reaches a raw fidelity of 0.9959(2), increasing to 0.99902(7) after postselection on no detected loss, and the performance is essentially unchanged under laser-power variations of up to 20%. We further show that the same fidelity Hessian directions can correct certain Hamiltonian parameter errors. These results establish low-rank Hessian optimization as an efficient and physically motivated calibration strategy for high-dimensional optimal-control gates, which is broadly applicable to many qubit types.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that the simple antiferromagnet $	ext{Cr}_2	ext{O}_3$ exhibits room-temperature multiferroic behavior localized at its domain walls. This effect is driven by a specific coupling mechanism called the flexo-Zeeman interaction. The findings establish a pathway for developing high-temperature, spin-driven magnetoelectric memory and logic devices.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on condensed matter magnetism, the study of emergent electric polarization driven by spin textures (domain walls) provides a model for coupling magnetic order parameters to electric polarization, which is a key concept in advanced quantum materials and spintronics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The research aims to demonstrate and understand room-temperature multiferroic ordering in simple, collinear magnetoelectric antiferromagnets.

**Main result.** The study experimentally and theoretically confirms that domain walls in $	ext{Cr}_2	ext{O}_3$ exhibit multiferroic behavior driven by the flexo-Zeeman interaction, leading to measurable electric polarization and magnetization.

**Method.** The work combines experimental characterization using SNVM and KPFM with theoretical modeling involving the flexo-Zeeman Hamiltonian and *ab initio* calculations.

**Model / system.** The physical system is the simple oxide $	ext{Cr}_2	ext{O}_3$, an antiferromagnet. The key theoretical element is the flexo-Zeeman coupling term, which links crystal symmetry, Néel vector gradients, and magnetization, leading to non-collinear magnetoelectric effects.

**Key observables.** Electric polarization ($P$), uncompensated magnetization ($M$), stray field patterns (from SNVM), and the ratio of magnetic transition fields ($	ext{H}_{	ext{DW}}/	ext{H}_{	ext{SF}}$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Room temperature, flexo-Zeeman coupling coefficient ($b$), and domain wall periodicity ($\approx 300-390 	ext{ nm}$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The multiferroicity is localized to specific domain wall orientations in the basal plane, and the effect can be extended to global reconfigurable channels.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the symmetry classification of the flexo-Zeeman interaction, energy dependence of polarization, and comparative SNVM/KPFM scans showing stripe stray fields in domain walls under different magnetic field cooling conditions.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from establishing the theoretical framework of the flexo-Zeeman coupling, to presenting experimental measurements on domain walls in $	ext{Cr}_2	ext{O}_3$ using multiple techniques, and finally confirming the predicted magnetoelectric response and its implications for device applications.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Spin-driven multiferroicity attracts significant interest due to its tunability and inherently strong magnetoelectric coupling. While this mechanism induces sizeable electric polarization, it typically occurs at low temperatures and in complex materials. In the simple oxide, the magnetoelectric antiferromagnet Cr$_2$O$_3$, we experimentally demonstrate the existence of specific domain walls that act as room-temperature multiferroic regions. This behavior stems from an anisotropic crystal-symmetry-dependent mechanism of exchange origin, which is applicable to a broad class of bipartite antiferromagnets. The key signature is the magnetization occurring at antiferromagnetic textures, driven by the flexo-Zeeman interaction. These findings establish a foundation for exploring high-temperature spin-driven multiferroicity for magnetoelectric spin-orbit memory and logic applications.

</details>

### [Magnetic structure of diamond EuTi2Al20](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04802v1)

**Authors:** S. W. Lovesey, D. D. Khalyavin  
**Type:** both · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.04802v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `spintronics-quantum-optics interface` **3/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.04802_figures/2606.04802_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Primary (σ, π) and secondary (σ', π') states of x-ray polarization; σ = σ' = (0, 0, 1), π = (cos(θ), sin(θ), 0), π' = (cos(θ), − sin(θ), 0). Corresponding wavevectors q and q' subtend an angle 2θ. The Bragg condition for diffraction is met when q − q' coincides with a vector τ(h, k, l) of the reciprocal lattice. The depicted Cartesian (x, y, z) coincide with local axes (ξ, η, ζ) in the nominal setting of the crystal.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This work uses resonant X-ray diffraction theory to distinguish between two possible low-temperature magnetic symmetries of EuTi2Al20. By calculating how magnetic multipoles affect the diffraction pattern based on symmetry constraints, the authors provide a detailed diagnostic tool to resolve the ambiguity left by current experimental data.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on solid-state magnetism, the use of advanced X-ray scattering techniques to probe subtle symmetry breaking via multipole interactions is relevant to understanding complex electronic correlations in materials.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper aims to definitively determine the magnetic symmetry of the compound EuTi2Al20 that emerges below 3.3 K, distinguishing between two competing orthorhombic candidates.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate that the predicted resonant X-ray diffraction patterns, particularly those involving parity-odd multipoles, are distinct for the two candidate symmetries, offering a path for experimental discrimination.

**Method.** The study combines theoretical calculations of electronic structure factors with the analysis of selection rules derived from parity mixing in the parent cubic structure.

**Model / system.** The physical system is EuTi2Al20, which undergoes magnetic ordering below 3.3 K. The analysis focuses on the magnetic symmetries and the resulting magnetic multipoles formed by Eu ions.

**Key observables.** Diffraction intensities measured via resonant X-ray diffraction, specifically analyzing polarization ($\sigma$ vs. $\pi$) and azimuthal angle ($\psi$) dependence.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The critical temperature (3.3 K), the specific magnetic space groups ($	ext{PInna}$ and $	ext{PInn2}$), and the parity-odd multipole contributions.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes that the selection rule from parity mixing in the parent cubic structure is preserved, and that the local axes align with the crystal axes in the nominal setting.

**Figures summary.** The notes mention the use of X-ray polarization states ($\sigma, \pi, \sigma', \pi'$) and the fulfillment of the Bragg condition, suggesting figures illustrate these polarization dependencies.

**Paper structure.** The paper proceeds by establishing the context of magnetic symmetry determination, detailing the theoretical framework involving parity mixing and multipole calculations for the two candidate symmetries, and concluding with a comparison of the predicted, symmetry-dependent diffraction patterns.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study magnetic symmetries of EuTi2Al20 that appear in the ordering of Eu ions below a temperature at 3.3 K, according to an analysis of substantial experimental evidence [M. Kawamata et al., J. Phys. Soc. Japan. 95, 024701 (2026)]. They descend from the cubic diamond structure that depends explicitly on mixing of orbitals with different spatial parities, e.g., diamond s- and p-atomic states. The lattice (2, 2, 2) Bragg spot, for example, is lattice forbidden in the absence of parity mixing. Our work is motivated by a first proposal that low-temperature magnetic symmetries of diamond EuTi2Al20 are orthorhombic and belong to magnetic crystal classes that include time reversal. Body-centre anti-translation in diffraction patterns is a corollary. Current experimental data does not distinguish between two orthorhombic candidates, even though one is centrosymmetric and the other one is polar. We demonstrate that magnetic parity-odd entities (multipoles) formed by mixing d-and f-atomic states at europium positions, with discreet symmetries that match those of a Dirac monopole, are different for the two candidates. Moreover, the multipoles are visible in resonant x-ray diffraction patterns. The selection rule from parity mixing in the parent cubic diamond structure is preserved, and permitted Bragg spots are different for the two candidate magnetic symmetries. Our symmetry informed diffraction patterns include rotation of the crystal about the reflection vector (an azimuthal angle scan) and a full account of x-ray polarization to spur more revealing experiments.

</details>

### [Leggett-Garg test inequality with spin and flavour neutrino oscillations in a constant magnetic field](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04412v1)

**Summary.** This theoretical work tests the limits of quantum mechanics by examining the violation of the Leggett-Garg inequality in neutrino oscillations. The study models both flavour and spin transitions under the influence of a strong magnetic field. The main finding is that the LGI is violated in specific spatial regions, providing a quantum signature potentially observable in extreme astrophysical environments like magnetars.

**Why it may be interesting.** While the core topic is particle astrophysics, the methodology—testing fundamental quantum limits (LGI) using time/space correlations derived from quantum evolution equations—is relevant to open quantum systems theory and quantum foundations.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates the violation of the Leggett-Garg inequality (LGI) when applied to neutrino flavour and spin oscillations in the presence of a constant magnetic field.

**Main result.** The LGI is shown to be violated for both flavour and spin neutrino oscillations in regions defined by the oscillation length $\Delta L$, particularly when strong magnetic fields are present.

**Method.** The analysis involves calculating specific correlation functions, $K_3$ and $K_4$, which serve as diagnostics for LGI violation, using joint probability distributions derived from the neutrino evolution equations.

**Model / system.** The system models neutrino flavour ($
u_L^e \leftrightarrow 
u_L^\mu$) and spin ($
u_L^e \leftrightarrow 
u_R^e$) oscillations governed by the Dirac equation, incorporating the effects of a constant transverse magnetic field ($B_\perp$).

**Key observables.** The LGI violation is quantified by the correlator functions $K_3$ and $K_4$, and the primary observables are the flavour and spin oscillation probabilities $P(t)$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Key parameters include the mass squared difference ($\Delta m^2$), mixing angle ($	heta$), neutrino energy ($p$), and the magnetic field strength ($B \sim 10^{12}$ T).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis utilizes the ultra-relativistic approximation ($p \gg m$) and treats the system dynamics using the effective Hamiltonian derived from the Dirac equation.

**Figures summary.** The figures plot the LGI test functions ($K_3$ and $K_4$) as a function of distance ($L$ or $\Delta L$) for spin oscillation, comparing results for zero and non-zero $\Delta m^2$ in a transverse magnetic field.

**Paper structure.** The paper establishes the LGI framework, derives the oscillation probabilities for flavour and spin transitions in a magnetic field, and then calculates the specific correlation functions $K_3$ and $K_4$ to demonstrate the violation of the inequality.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The Leggett-Garg inequality (LGI), an analogue of Bellś inequality involving correlations of measurements of one observable on a system at different times, stands as one of the hallmark tests of quantum mechanics against classical predictions. In this work, we investigate its implications in the context of neutrino flavour ($ν_{e}^{L}\leftrightarrowν_μ^{L}$) and spin ($ν_{e}^{L}\leftrightarrowν_{e}^{R}$) oscillations in the presence of a constant transverse magnetic field. For systems with strong magnetic fields, we show that, for both cases, there are length regions $ΔL$ where the LGI is violated, as quantified by the correlator functions $K_{3}$ and $K_{4}$.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

Nonlinearities in neural networks produce high-frequency power laws. a Four example images from a natural image subset of ImageNet [4] imprinted to the network. b Four low-frequency modes of the images that dominate the spectrum (top), and four high-frequency modes at the end of the spectrum (bottom). c Temporal envelope of the neural data used in [4]. The readout time is indicated by a dashed line. d Kernel function (orange) as obtained through Eq. (10) for g/gV = 1. Gray curve is the affine-linear kernel Eq. </details>

**Summary.** This theoretical work develops a framework showing that the inherent chaos in recurrent neural networks does not preclude stable computation. By analyzing the network's kernel structure using advanced mean-field techniques, the authors prove that this chaos acts as a beneficial intrinsic regularizer, enabling smooth, generalizable coding despite microscopic unpredictability.

**Why it may be interesting.** The mathematical machinery developed (DMFT, replica method) for analyzing correlations in complex, non-equilibrium systems like RNNs shares conceptual overlap with techniques used in analyzing disordered quantum systems, such as spin glasses or spin-boson models.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the fundamental conflict between the intrinsic chaotic nature of recurrent neural networks and the necessity for their population codes to exhibit smooth, stable representations for functional computation.

**Main result.** Chaotic dynamics induce a structural property—local roughness while maintaining global smoothness—that acts as an intrinsic regularizer, allowing chaotic systems to sustain coherent, differentiable coding.

**Method.** The authors combine kernel methods with Dynamical Mean-Field Theory (DMFT) and the replica method to derive the effective kernel describing the network's representation geometry.

**Model / system.** The study analyzes Recurrent Neural Networks (RNNs) operating in regimes of intrinsic chaos, comparing the theoretical predictions derived from continuous-rate models against those from discrete, spiking (Glauber) dynamics.

**Key observables.** Representation geometry (smoothness/roughness), power-law spectral signatures, and the two-replica kernel function $k_{ts}(x_1 \cdot x_2)$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Synaptic strength ($g$), network size ($N$), and the ratio of recurrent to feedforward coupling ($g/g_V$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies heavily on the large-$N$ limit (mean-field theory) and the assumption that the system's statistics can be captured by the two-replica correlation function.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the comparison of classification accuracy and kernel functions between chaotic discrete networks and regular/feedforward models, demonstrating the regularization effect of local chaos.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first establishing the theoretical link between chaos and representation geometry, then deriving the mean-field equations for the two-replica kernel using DMFT, and finally contrasting the resulting kernel structure for continuous versus discrete signaling dynamics.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Cortical circuits operate in a regime of intrinsic chaos, where even tiny changes in input can lead to divergent neural responses. Yet, remarkably, population codes in the brain vary smoothly with sensory stimuli, forming coherent representational manifolds. How can chaotic networks sustain such stable coding? Here, we develop a theoretical framework that links the microscopic chaos of recurrent networks to the macroscopic geometry of neural representations. Combining kernel methods with dynamical mean-field theory, we show that chaotic dynamics induce local roughness (introducing sharp distortions at small scales) while preserving global smoothness across larger stimulus variations. This structural property acts as an intrinsic regularizer, enhancing generalization while maintaining expressivity. Moreover, we show how chaotic networks naturally produce power-law spectral signatures, closely matching experimental observations in cortical recordings. These results explain how chaotic spiking networks can sustain smooth, differentiable population codes and establish a theoretical framework linking network dynamics, computational structure, and recorded neural activity.

</details>

### [Maximal Minimal Spacing for Random Points](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04837v1)

**Authors:** Fabio Deelan Cunden, Noemi Cuppone, Giovanni Gramegna, Pierpaolo Vivo  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.04837v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `analog quantum simulation` **2/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.04837_figures/2606.04837_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1. Choose 𝑀+1 points out of 𝑁+1 so as to maximize their minimal spacing. Here 𝑁= 7, and 𝑀= 4. The top line contains the initial configuration (black circles). The  6 3  = 20 possible selections of points are in the lines below. <details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2606.04837_figures/2606.04837_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2. Sketch of the threshold-resetting random walk. In this example, there are 𝐾𝑁(𝑠) = 3 complete cycles of length 𝐿1(𝑠) = 4, 𝐿2(𝑠) = 3, 𝐿3(𝑠) = 2 in 𝑁= 12 steps.</sub>

</details>

<img src="2606.04837_figures/2606.04837_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>

**Summary.** This paper provides a rigorous statistical mechanics treatment of maximizing the minimum spacing among randomly placed points. It reformulates the problem using a threshold-resetting random walk, yielding exact formulas for the tail probability $P(S^* \ge s)$. The results are crucial for understanding optimal resource dispersion in various physical models.

**Why it may be interesting.** The mathematical techniques developed—relating optimal resource allocation (spacing maximization) to random walk statistics and large deviation theory—are highly relevant for analyzing resource constraints in quantum information or many-body systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the problem of maximizing the minimal spacing ($S^*$) when selecting $M+1$ points from an initial set of $N+1$ random points on a line.

**Main result.** It derives exact distributional identities and asymptotic formulas for $P(S^* \ge s)$ for general i.i.d. gap distributions, relating it to first-passage functionals of a threshold-resetting random walk.

**Method.** The core methodology involves mapping the optimization problem to a random walk process, utilizing generating functions, and employing saddle-point approximation techniques for large $N$ and $M$.

**Model / system.** The model consists of $N+1$ points on a line with successive spacings ($T_j$) assumed to be independent and identically distributed (i.i.d.). The analysis covers general, exponential, and geometric gap distributions.

**Key observables.** The maximal minimal spacing ($S^*$), the tail probability $P(S^* \ge s)$, and the relative spacing $eS$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The ratio $\alpha = M/N$, the total number of gaps $N$, and the number of selected blocks $M$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The initial spacings $T_j$ are assumed to be i.i.d., and the analysis often focuses on the large $N$ limit.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the max-min spacing problem for small $N, M$, sketch the threshold-resetting random walk cycles, and show numerical comparisons of $S^*$ for uniform and exponential gaps.

**Paper structure.** The paper builds from defining the max-min spacing problem, reformulating it via a threshold-resetting random walk, deriving exact distributional identities using generating functions, and finally establishing asymptotic results using saddle-point methods for different gap types.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

From $N+1$ random points on a line we wish to select $M+1$ points so as to maximize the minimal spacing between them. We consider an initial configuration with independent and identically distributed spacings. The problem is equivalent to optimally grouping consecutive gaps into $M$ blocks and maximizing the smallest block sum. For general gap distributions, and for all $M\leq N$, we derive exact distributional identities for the optimal spacing and obtain its asymptotic behavior. The problem admits a reformulation in terms of a threshold-resetting random walk. The walk advances by successive random increments and is reset to the origin upon exceeding a fixed threshold. The probability that the optimal spacing exceeds a given value coincides with the probability that the walk completes at least $M$ reset cycles within $N$ steps. This yields an exact representation in terms of first-passage functionals of the walk. The same mapping suggests a numerical scheme for the max-min spacing problem in the regime of large $N$ and $M$, whose accuracy is tested against the exact results obtained here.

</details>

**Summary.** This theoretical paper calculates the asymptotic ratio of storage capacities for complex neural networks when real constraints are imposed on the pre-activations. It employs sophisticated techniques from statistical physics, including the replica method and the Harish-Chandra-Itzykson-Zuber formula. The work provides a rigorous, albeit highly technical, analysis of model expressivity in complex spaces.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on neural networks, the mathematical machinery—replica methods, integration over compact groups, and free energy calculations—is highly relevant to understanding phase transitions and complexity in many-body quantum systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates the asymptotic ratio of storage capacities for complex-valued neural networks when the pre-activations are constrained to be real versus unconstrained complex values.

**Main result.** The authors derive the asymptotic ratio $\Gamma$ using advanced mathematical tools, suggesting a non-trivial dependence on the network structure and constraints.

**Method.** The analysis heavily employs the replica method, saddle-point evaluations, and advanced mathematical identities like the Harish-Chandra-Itzykson-Zuber (HCIZ) formula for integration over compact manifolds.

**Model / system.** The model is a complex-valued neural network analyzed in the context of its storage capacity and expressivity, utilizing concepts from statistical physics like the Gardner volume.

**Key observables.** Storage capacities ($\alpha_r, \alpha_c$), the asymptotic ratio ($\Gamma$), and the quenched free energy ($f$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Sparsity constraint parameter ($ho$), generalization margin ($\kappa$), and the network size ($N$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The derivation relies on the replica symmetric ansatz, the exchange of limit orders, and the applicability of advanced asymptotic approximations like Laplace's method.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 plots the ratio $\Gamma$ across varying parameters $ho$ and $G_{r,c}, B$, illustrating the theoretically valid regime $[0, 1]$.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first establishing the core problem (ratio of capacities), then developing the mathematical machinery using the replica trick and complex integration over unitary/orthogonal groups, culminating in the final asymptotic expressions for the capacity.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We find the asymptotic ratio between the storage capacities when enforcing real pre-activations in a complex hypothesis class as opposed to complex ones in the same class. Our methods depend on Gardner volume comparisons at critical capacity. Our proof relies on an application of the Harish-Chandra-Itzykson-Zuber (HCIZ) formula, nonstandard in literature. With the HCIZ formula, we may obtain a more robust approximation for the final asymptotic ratio. This strategy is applicable to our work specifically since we integrate over the unitary and orthogonal compact manifolds, facilitated via the Weyl integration formula and the Haar measure.

</details>

### [Spectral Fusion for Identifying Early State Exclusion in Symmetric Quantum Spin Chains](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04353v1)

**Authors:** Mia Escobar, Valentin Garcia, Anastasiia Minenkova  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.04353v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `analog quantum simulation` **2/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5** · `exotic spin models in light-matter` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **1/5**

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2606.04353_figures/2606.04353_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 7 Example of no ESE occur- rences for J with the reduced spectrum {0, ±1, ±14, ±15, ±16, ±19}.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper analyzes the spectral conditions governing quantum state transfer in 1D spin chains, focusing on the phenomenon of Early State Exclusion (ESE). By introducing the spectral fusion (SF) process, the authors construct infinite families of Hamiltonians that exhibit both perfect state transfer and ESE. This deepens the understanding of how the spectral structure dictates the time evolution and information transport properties of quantum many-body systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides deep spectral insights into quantum transport phenomena in 1D systems, connecting the algebraic structure of the Hamiltonian (Jacobi matrix) directly to observable quantum dynamics like state transfer and its early failure modes.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates the phenomenon of Early State Exclusion (ESE) in quantum spin chains, which is the vanishing of the time-evolved state overlap with the initial state at a time earlier than the first Perfect State Transfer (PST).

**Main result.** The authors provide explicit constructions, using a process called spectral fusion (SF), to build infinite families of Jacobi matrices exhibiting both PST and ESE, thereby broadening the known class of such spin chains.

**Method.** The analysis heavily relies on the spectral properties of the governing Jacobi matrix, utilizing polynomial sequences defined by three-term recurrence relations and analyzing the time evolution amplitude function $A(t)$ in terms of the matrix eigenvalues.

**Model / system.** The system is a one-dimensional quantum spin chain with nearest-neighbor interactions, whose dynamics in the single-excitation subspace are modeled by a real symmetric tridiagonal Jacobi matrix $J$.

**Key observables.** Perfect State Transfer (PST) time $T_0$, the amplitude function $A(t) = \langle e^{-iJt} e_0, e_0 angle$, and the occurrence of ESE at time $	au < T_0$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The length of the chain (parity of $N+1$), the structure of the spectrum $\sigma(J)$, and the convergence parameter $m$ used in spectral fusion.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis focuses on the single-excitation subspace, and several results assume the spectrum $\sigma(J)$ is symmetric or 'reduced' for simplification.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the quantum spin-chain model, examples of ESE occurrences relative to PST times, and comparisons between different spectral structures (e.g., T-Rex vs. Krawtchouk chains) regarding ESE.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by defining the problem (ESE vs. PST), deriving amplitude formulas based on spectral properties, introducing the spectral fusion (SF) process as an inverse operation to spectral surgery, and finally proving convergence theorems to construct infinite families of matrices exhibiting these behaviors.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Perfect state transfer (PST) in one-dimensional quantum spin chains provides a natural setting in which quantum information transport can be analyzed using spectral methods. In the single-excitation subspace, the dynamics of a chain with nearest-neighbor interactions are governed by a Jacobi matrix, allowing questions of state transfer to be formulated in terms of eigenvalue distributions and symmetry properties of eigenvectors. In this work, we investigate the phenomenon of \emph{early state exclusion} (ESE), whereby the overlap of the time-evolved state with the initial state vanishes at a time strictly earlier than the first occurrence of perfect state transfer. Building on earlier constructions of Hamiltonians exhibiting PST with and without ESE, we provide explicit Jacobi matrix realizations for arbitrary odd-length chains and establish general conditions under which ESE occurs or does not occur. We propose the process of \emph{spectral fusion} (SF) to build infinite families of such Hamiltonians. These results broaden the known class of spin chains displaying early state exclusion and further clarify the role of spectral structure of the Hamiltonians.

</details>

### [Stabilizing the parquet problem](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04936v1)
