**Summary.** This work addresses the numerical instability of the parquet equations used to calculate properties of strongly correlated electron systems like the Hubbard model. By analyzing the Jacobian spectrum, the authors provide a stabilization strategy that allows the iterative solution to converge to the correct physical fixed point even in highly non-perturbative regimes where standard methods fail.

**Why it may be interesting.** The rigorous mathematical treatment of convergence failure in diagrammatic methods provides crucial insight into the non-perturbative physics of strongly correlated materials, which is central to modern condensed matter theory.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper systematically analyzes the stability of the iterative solution of the parquet equations, which are used to solve for interacting many-body systems.

**Main result.** The instability of the parquet iteration is not solely caused by divergences in the two-particle irreducible vertex, and a controlled stabilization strategy was introduced that successfully converges to the physical solution in deep non-perturbative regimes.

**Method.** The analysis involves studying the spectrum of the Jacobian associated with the damped fixed-point iteration of the parquet equations, leading to a stabilization procedure.

**Model / system.** The study applies to the Zero-point (ZP) model and the Hubbard atom (HA), which represents the strong-coupling limit of the Hubbard model. The formalism relies on diagrammatic techniques like the parquet equations.

**Key observables.** Convergence issues, Jacobian eigenvalues, physical fixed point stability, and the two-particle irreducible vertex.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Interaction strength (U), chemical potential deviation ($\delta\mu$), and the damping parameter ($p$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes the validity of the iterative fixed-point approach, though it shows this approach can fail due to local instabilities.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the stability phase space for the ZP model, comparing conventional iteration failure versus success using the stabilized method, and show convergence trajectories for coupled equations.

**Paper structure.** The paper proceeds by analyzing the stability of the iterative solution, deriving stability criteria from the Jacobian spectrum, introducing a stabilization strategy, and finally applying this procedure to the ZP and HA models.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We systematically analyze the stability of the iterative solution of the parquet equations by studying the spectrum of the Jacobian associated with the commonly used damped fixed-point iteration procedure. In this context, we provide an explicit criterion that determines when the physical fixed point of the parquet iteration becomes unstable. Importantly, we demonstrate that misleading convergence issues, observed in parquet calculation at intermediate-to-high interaction values, are not restricted to parameter regions where the two-particle irreducible vertex diverges, but can also arise in absence of vertex divergences. Hence, the misleading convergence issues of parquet-based algorithms are not directly caused by the crossings of two solutions of the (multivalued) Luttinger-Ward functional, that are associated with vertex divergences. Building on these insights, we introduce a controlled stabilization strategy that allows the convergence to the physical solution in the instability regimes. We apply this procedure to the zero-point model and the Hubbard model in the atomic limit, where we successfully stabilize the physical solution deep in the non-perturbative regime, even across multiple divergence lines.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2606.05099"></a>
### [Quantum Time Lower Bounds by Permutation Invariance](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.05099v1)

**Authors:** Qisheng Wang  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.05099v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **2/5** · `quantum measurements` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.05099_figures/2606.05099_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1: Diagram of relationships amongst our results.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2606.05099_figures/2606.05099_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2: Quantum circuit for tester T .</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper introduces a systematic theoretical framework to derive tight lower bounds on the quantum time complexity required for quantum property testing. By reducing the problem to sample complexity bounds via permutation invariance, the authors prove time-optimality for several fundamental quantum estimation protocols, including purity and inner product estimation.

**Why it may be interesting.** For a theoretical physicist, this work provides rigorous, systematic tools to quantify the fundamental computational limits (time vs. sample) for characterizing quantum states, which is crucial for designing efficient quantum measurements.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper aims to establish systematic and tight lower bounds on the quantum time complexity (circuit size) required to test specific quantum properties of quantum states.

**Main result.** A novel framework is introduced that relates the time complexity of testing permutation-invariant properties to the sample complexity of related, simpler problems, yielding time-optimality for several key quantum estimation protocols.

**Method.** The core method involves reducing the problem of estimating a complex property $P_n$ to a known sample complexity lower bound $S(Q_m)$ via theorems based on permutation invariance and embeddability.

**Model / system.** The analysis focuses on $n$-qubit quantum states ($ho$) and their properties, such as purity ($	ext{tr}(ho^2)$), inner products ($	ext{tr}(ho\sigma)$), and productness. The framework is generalizable to qudits.

**Key observables.** Quantum time complexity $T(P_n)$, quantum sample complexity $S(P_n)$, purity, inner product, and high-order power traces.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The error parameter ($\epsilon$), the number of qubits ($n$), and the ratio $R$ derived from the structure of the property are crucial parameters.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The results heavily rely on the assumption that the properties being tested exhibit permutation invariance, allowing the use of the derived reduction theorems.

**Figures summary.** Figures summarize the relationships between the derived time-optimality results for various quantum algorithmic tools.

**Paper structure.** The paper develops a theoretical framework using theorems (like Theorem 1.1 and 1.2) to relate time and sample complexity. It then applies this framework to derive specific, time-optimal lower bounds for protocols like the SWAP test, Shift test, and samplizer.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Tight bounds on quantum sample complexity and quantum query complexity have been known for various computational problems in the literature, whereas tight bounds on quantum time complexity (i.e., the size of quantum circuits) remain unresolved. In this paper, we provide a framework to establish lower bounds on the quantum time complexity for testing permutation-invariant properties of quantum states, via a reduction from quantum sample complexity. As an application, we obtain a series of matching lower bounds when given sample access to the input quantum states, including:   1. The SWAP test due to Buhrman, Cleve, Watrous, and de Wolf (Phys. Rev. Lett. 2001) is time-optimal to estimate the purity $\operatorname{tr}(ρ^2)$ and the inner product $\operatorname{tr}(ρσ)$.   2. The Shift test due to Ekert, Alves, Oi, Horodecki, Horodecki, and Kwek (Phys. Rev. Lett. 2002) is time-optimal to estimate the high-order functionals $\operatorname{tr}(ρ^k)$.   3. The productness tester for multipartite pure states due to Harrow and Montanaro (J. ACM 2013) is time-optimal.   4. The LMR protocol due to Lloyd, Mohseni, and Rebentrost (Nat. Phys. 2014) is time-optimal to implement the reflection operator about a pure state.   5. The samplizer due to Wang and Zhang (IEEE Trans. Inf. Theory 2025) is time-optimal for pure states.   6. The estimator for pure-state trace distance and fidelity due to Wang and Zhang (ICALP 2026) is time-optimal.   To the best of our knowledge, this is the first method that allows us to systematically establish tight lower bounds on quantum time complexity.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [Imaginarity witness](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04763v1)

**Authors:** Zhiqi Liang, Yufan Lin, Yu Guo, Yuqi Sun  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.04763v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **2/5**

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 5</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2606.04763_figures/2606.04763_page6.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 6</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper introduces 'imaginarity witnesses' as a new tool to quantify the imaginary component of quantum states. It establishes a theoretical framework showing that the resulting 'witnessed imaginarity' measure is mathematically equivalent to both the trace norm and the robustness of imaginarity. This advances the understanding of quantum resource theory by providing a concrete diagnostic tool for imaginary quantum features.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a rigorous, operational framework for quantifying a specific non-classical feature (imaginarity) of quantum states, drawing direct parallels to well-established concepts like entanglement and coherence witnesses.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper develops and analyzes a new class of 'imaginarity witnesses' to characterize and detect imaginary quantum states, extending resource theories like entanglement and coherence.

**Main result.** The authors prove that a newly defined 'witnessed imaginarity' measure coincides with both the trace norm of imaginarity and the robustness of imaginarity.

**Method.** The methodology involves defining witnesses based on the maximum and minimum eigenvalues of the real part of the witness operator, and then analyzing the geometric relationships between the sets of detectable states.

**Model / system.** The study concerns quantum states (density matrices $ho$) acting on a Hilbert space of dimension $d \ge 2$. The analysis uses specific examples like qubits and qutrits.

**Key observables.** Imaginarity witness ($W$), Imaginarity measure ($	ext{IW}(ho)$), Trace norm of imaginarity ($	ext{Itr}(ho)$), and Robustness of imaginarity ($	ext{IR}(ho)$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The minimum ($m$) and maximum ($M$) eigenvalues of $	ext{Re}(W)$ define the detection interval $[m, M]$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis builds upon the established framework of quantum resource theories and assumes the existence of a reference basis for defining real states.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the concept of imaginarity witnesses, derives new classes of witnesses ($W_{m,M}$), analyzes the relationships between different witnesses (equality and inclusion of detection sets), and culminates in proving the equivalence of the defined imaginarity measure with known quantities.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Imaginarity has shown to be an important resource in quantum information. The witness theory of quantum resource, such as entanglement witness, coherence witness, and imaginarity witness, has been established, in particular entanglement witness and coherence witness have been extensively explored. We present here another class of imaginarity witnesses beyond the one in [{Phys. Lett. A} \textbf{530}, 130135 (2025)]. Within our framework, any nonreal Hermitian operator under the reference basis is an imaginarity witness and only finite of them can detect all the imaginarity states. As the common approaches that were explored in the witness theories of entanglement and coherence, we then explore the relations between these imaginarity witnesses in different cases: (i) when they can detect common imaginary states, (ii) when they can detect the same sets of imaginary states, and (ii) when they obey the finer relation. Finally, we define an imaginarity measure in terms of witnesses, termed witnessed imaginarity, and prove that it coincides with both the trace norm of imaginarity and the robustness of imaginarity.

</details>

**Summary.** This work uses molecular dynamics and percolation theory to study how structural connectivity changes in compressed amorphous glasses like $	ext{SiO}_2$. By comparing bonded and non-bonded structural analyses, the authors map out sequential percolation transitions corresponding to the formation of different structural motifs ($	ext{SiO}_4, 	ext{SiO}_5, 	ext{SiO}_6$). The findings suggest that these transitions are governed by a mechanism distinct from simple random percolation, hinting at topological or rigidity effects.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on condensed matter physics, the rigorous application of percolation theory and scaling laws to characterize structural phase transitions in disordered systems provides a powerful, generalizable framework for understanding connectivity and rigidity in complex materials, which has parallels in analyzing network dynamics in quantum many-body systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates the percolation criticality associated with amorphous-amorphous structural transitions in compressed glasses, specifically $	ext{SiO}_2$ and $	ext{H}_2	ext{O}$. It aims to understand the underlying physical mechanism governing these structural changes.

**Main result.** The study reveals critical percolation transitions for various structural motifs ($	ext{SiO}_2$-$	ext{SiO}_2$ clusters, $	ext{SiO}_6$-$	ext{SiO}_6$ clusters) whose critical exponents suggest a possible rigidity percolation mechanism, deviating from standard random correlation models.

**Method.** The research employs large-scale molecular dynamics simulations combined with percolation theory, utilizing cluster analysis (e.g., Union-Find algorithm) and finite-size scaling methods to determine critical exponents and transition pressures.

**Model / system.** The primary systems are compressed amorphous silica ($	ext{a-SiO}_2$) and amorphous water ($	ext{a-H}_2	ext{O}$). The analysis compares 'bonded' models (based on polyhedral connectivity like $	ext{SiO}_4$ tetrahedra) with 'non-bonded' models (based on coordination numbers like $	ext{OOZ}$ or $	ext{SiSiZ}$ clusters).

**Key observables.** Percolation order parameter ($P_\infty$), correlation length ($\xi$), average cluster size ($\langle S angle$), critical exponents ($
u, eta, \gamma, 	au$), and fractal dimension ($D_f^*$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Pressure (ranging from 0 to 35 GPa), coordination number ($Z$), and system size ($10^6$ atoms).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on the applicability of scaling laws near the critical point and assumes that structural transitions can be mapped onto percolation phenomena.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the pressure dependence of correlation length and cluster size, showing power-law scaling near critical points, and demonstrate data collapse using Finite Size Scaling (FSS) ansatz for various cluster types.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically compares results from bonded and non-bonded approaches, mapping out sequential percolation transitions ($	ext{SiO}_4 	o 	ext{SiO}_5 	o 	ext{SiO}_6$) as pressure increases, and applying rigorous scaling analysis to quantify the critical behavior.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The low-to-high-density transition in compressed silica glass is investigated using percolation theory. Large-scale molecular dynamics simulations of SiO$_2$ glasses, with system sizes of up to 10$^6$ atoms and pressures ranging from 0 to 35 GPa, were carried out to investigate the emergence of structural motifs and their growth to system-spanning length scales under compression. On this basis, we introduced long-range descriptors that complement conventional local and medium-range structural measures. The results reveal critical percolation transitions of SiO$_Z$-SiO$_Z$ clusters with increasing coordination number $Z$. The critical exponents slightly deviate from the standard (random) correlation, a behavior that seems to be more pronounced for higher coordinated polyhedra than for tetrahedra, suggesting a possible rigidity percolation mechanism. SiSi$_z$-SiSi$_z$ clusters were also analyzed using the non-bonded approach. Bonded and non-bonded approaches complement each other in a particularly illuminating way for describing pressure-induced structural transformations and common mechanisms shared by bonded glasses, such as SiO$_2$, and non-bonded glasses, such as amorphous ice.

</details>

### [Robust Steerability Classification via Key Feature Extraction and Matrix Structure Preservation](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04363v1)

**Authors:** Yutao Xin, Huixian Meng, Zhongyan Li, Pu Wang  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.04363v1>

**Summary.** This work develops robust machine learning methods to classify quantum steerability, a key measure of quantum correlation. By recognizing that standard feature vectorization loses crucial information, the authors introduce CNNs that process the full matrix structure of the quantum state. The resulting model achieves state-of-the-art robustness, allowing for the prediction of measurement requirements for detecting steerability.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides advanced machine learning techniques (CNNs on structured data) to tackle fundamental questions in quantum correlation theory, which is highly relevant to quantum information processing and characterization.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the challenge of creating robust quantum steerability classifiers that can generalize accurately across various challenging quantum states.

**Main result.** The most robust classification performance is achieved by simultaneously extracting key features and preserving the intrinsic matrix structure of the quantum state using Convolutional Neural Networks (CNNs).

**Method.** The authors compare various machine learning models (SVM, MLP, CNN) trained on different feature representations (F1, F2) derived from quantum state density matrices.

**Model / system.** The study analyzes quantum steerability, an asymmetric correlation measure between quantum systems, using density matrices ($ho_{AB}$) for two-qubit states. The classification is tested on specific state families, including Werner, T-diagonal, and All-Versus-Nothing (AVN) states.

**Key observables.** Classification accuracy (%) on random and Werner states, and the prediction accuracy of the minimum number of projective measurements required to detect steerability.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The number of projective measurements ($m$), the feature sets ($F_1, F_2$), and the CNN hyperparameters (e.g., learning rate, kernel size).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The primary assumption challenged is that flattening quantum states into 1D vectors destroys essential matrix structure, necessitating the use of CNNs on matrix-structured features.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare classification accuracies of various models (SVM, CNN) on random and Werner states, and show the successful prediction of steerability boundaries for axially symmetric states.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first establishing the limitations of basic classifiers on challenging states, then introducing key feature extraction, and finally achieving the best results by integrating feature extraction with matrix structure preservation via CNNs, culminating in an application to measurement prediction.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Generalization ability is essential for assessing the robustness of quantum steerability classifiers. In this work, we investigate robust steerability classification from the perspective of key feature extraction and matrix structure preservation. The dataset introduced in Phys. Rev. A 100, 022314 (2019) provides the training basis for the present work. With strictly unsteerable random states, T-diagonal states, and All-Versus-Nothing (AVN) states, we evaluate the generalization performance of support vector machines (SVMs), multilayer perceptrons (MLPs), and deep perceptron control classifiers(DPs) trained on full-information features. None of these classifiers perform consistently on T-diagonal or AVN states. Given that stochastic local operations and classical communication and local unitary transformations preserve steerability, we introduce a key feature that determines steerability. SVMs trained on this feature overcome the instability on T-diagonal states but still fail on AVN states. Moreover, this feature alone is insufficient for training robust neural-network-based steerability classifiers. Recognizing that flattening quantum states into one-dimensional vectors may destroy their intrinsic matrix structure, we introduce matrix versions of both features and train convolutional neural networks on them. The most robust overall performance among the tested classifiers is achieved only when the matrix structure is preserved and key features are extracted simultaneously. Finally, as an application, we employ the most robust classifiers to predict the number of projective measurements required to detect the steerability of axially symmetric states

</details>

### [Anisotropic Rabi Model as a Noise Biased Qubit](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04487v1)

**Authors:** Jia-Wen Yu, Ke-Xiong Yan, Yuan Qiu, Yiming Yu, Yexiong Zeng, Adam Miranowicz, Zhi-Cheng Shi, Ye-Hong Chen, Yan Xia, Franco Nori

<sub>FIG. 4. Influence of external noise on the operational performance of different gate types. **Summary.** This paper treats the anisotropic Rabi model as a resource for building a noise-biased qubit. By tuning the anisotropy parameter $\eta$, researchers can selectively suppress environmental decoherence pathways. This engineering capability leads to logical qubits with enhanced coherence times and robust quantum gates, advancing the feasibility of quantum computation in strongly coupled regimes.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work directly addresses the critical challenge of decoherence in strongly coupled quantum systems by providing a tunable Hamiltonian parameter ($\eta$) to engineer intrinsic noise protection, which is highly relevant for building scalable quantum processors.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates using the quantum anisotropic Rabi model (ARM) to engineer a noise-biased qubit capable of protecting quantum information against environmental decoherence.

**Main result.** Tuning the anisotropy parameter ($\eta$) allows for the selective suppression of dominant decoherence pathways, leading to logical qubits with enhanced coherence times and robust gate operations.

**Method.** The analysis involves deriving noise susceptibilities ($S_r, S_p$) and quantifying noise bias ($\Deltaho$) by comparing the ARM dynamics to the isotropic case, using master equations.

**Model / system.** The system is the quantum anisotropic Rabi model, describing an atom coupled to a bosonic field. The dynamics are analyzed in the ultrastrong and deep-strong coupling regimes, utilizing a master equation approach.

**Key observables.** Coherence times, noise susceptibilities ($S_r, S_p$), noise bias ($\Deltaho$), and gate fidelity ($f$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The anisotropy parameter $\eta$ is the key control knob, which tunes the relative strengths of rotating-wave and counter-rotating-wave interactions.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on defining logical states within the ground and first excited state subspace, and some perturbative treatments are noted to break down in the deepest coupling regimes.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates the energy gap variation in the weak coupling regime. Figure 2 shows how noise sensitivities vary with coupling strength ($g$) for different $\eta$ values, demonstrating $\eta$'s control over coupling imbalance.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from defining the ARM and its Hamiltonian, to analyzing noise susceptibility in various coupling regimes, demonstrating how $\eta$ controls decoherence, and finally constructing and testing universal, noise-robust gate operations within the logical subspace.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We present the quantum anisotropic Rabi model as a potential resource for a noise biased qubit. The system-environment coupling can be biased by tuning the relative strengths of the rotating-wave and counter-rotating-wave interactions, characterized by the anisotropy parameter $η$. This anisotropy selectively suppresses dominant decoherence pathways, thereby enabling the construction of a protected logical qubit in the ultrastrong and deep-strong coupling regimes. The logical states (formed by the ground and first excited states of the anisotropic Rabi model) possess coherence times that are enhanced compared to the isotropic case. Moreover, we construct a set of universal gate operations within the logical-state subspace and demonstrate that the gate operations associated with different values of $η$ exhibit robustness against external noise. These findings are expected to inspire applications and research directions for the anisotropic Rabi model with promising potential impacts.

</details>

<sub>Figure 3. Quasi-energies E± −iΓ±/2 of Helium in a monochro- matic, linearly polarized XUV field, resonant with the 1s2 ↔ 1s3p transition. Panel (a) shows the real part of the quasi- energies, E±, and panel (b) the decay rate Γ±. The black solid lines are exact calculations obtained from Floquet the- ory. We compare the exact quasi-energies to the effective Hamiltonian from RSPT in 10th order (yellow dashed), see Eq. (21), and to the AMP effective Hamiltonian in 10th order (blue dotted), see Eq. (34) (since the field is exactly degen- erate, HAMP = HQD-AMP). We label the dressed states as ∣+⟩ and ∣−⟩. In panel (c), we show the asymmetry of the Hamilto- nian, where the asymmetry is defined in...</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper addresses the limitations of standard effective Hamiltonian methods when modeling light-matter interactions under intense laser fields. The authors introduce Quasi-degenerate Rayleigh-Schrödinger perturbation theory (QD-RSPT) as a superior, systematic approach. QD-RSPT accurately describes complex phenomena like non-orthogonal eigenstates and asymmetric coupling, providing a new state-of-the-art tool for strong-field quantum optics.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant as it pushes the theoretical frontier in quantum optics by providing a systematic, high-intensity framework that correctly handles non-Hermiticity and state coupling asymmetries, which are critical for modern quantum control.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The primary challenge is reconciling effective Hamiltonians used to describe quantum systems undergoing intense light-matter interaction, especially where standard approximations fail.

**Main result.** The authors propose Quasi-degenerate Rayleigh-Schrödinger perturbation theory (QD-RSPT), which provides a robust framework that correctly accounts for non-orthogonal eigenstates and asymmetric coupling strengths at high intensities.

**Method.** The work employs advanced theoretical techniques, including QD-RSPT, quasi-degenerate extensions of adiabatic elimination, and comparison with Floquet calculations to derive the effective Hamiltonian.

**Model / system.** The system involves quantum matter (atoms/molecules) interacting with intense laser fields, modeled by a full light-matter Hamiltonian. The theory uses essential-state models to reduce the system to a manageable, effective Hamiltonian.

**Key observables.** Dressed energies (eigenvalues), population transfer dynamics, and the non-orthogonality of the effective Hamiltonian's eigenstates.

**Important parameters / regimes.** High light intensities (strong-field regime), detuning, and the ratio of photon energy to binding energy.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Standard approximations (like the Markov or pole approximation) break down at high intensities, necessitating the use of QD-RSPT to maintain accuracy.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare the results of various effective Hamiltonians (AMP, QD-RSPT) against exact Floquet calculations, showing agreement in Rabi oscillations and quasienergy structure across different intensities.

**Paper structure.** The paper builds from identifying the breakdown of standard approximations (AMP) to introducing QD-RSPT as the solution. It demonstrates the method's accuracy by comparing results across low, intermediate, and high-intensity regimes, particularly in minimal atomic models and real atoms like Rubidium.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Essential-state models are central for quantum control and technology in broad regimes of light-matter interaction. The canonical effective Hamiltonian is obtained equivalently from adiabatic elimination, the Markov approximation, and the pole approximation. These approximations are known to break down at high intensities, significantly limiting their applicability to moderate light-matter interaction. We show how this limitation can be addressed by applying quasi-degenerate Rayleigh-Schrödinger perturbation theory (QD-RSPT). We reconcile QD-RSPT with adiabatic elimination and propose a quasi-degenerate extension of adiabatic elimination that is robust when the detuning of the essential states is non-negligible. The accuracy of QD-RSPT is demonstrated in both the low- and high-frequency regime, showing excellent agreement with Floquet calculations at high intensities. The crucial corrections to adiabatic elimination make the eigenvectors of the effective Hamiltonian non-orthogonal. Physically, this allows us to account for the asymmetric strength with which different essential states couple to the non-essential states. We expect that our systematic approach to effective Hamiltonians from QD-RSPT will constitute a new state of the art in intense light-matter interaction and quantum optics with novel forms of strong coupling and quantum control phenomena being conceivable.

</details>

**Summary.** This work introduces an analytic perturbative framework to quantify errors in quantum gates implemented using photon-heralded processes between quantum emitters. By extending the ZPG framework, the authors derive closed-form expressions for error weights, allowing for physics-informed optimization of gate designs for fault-tolerant quantum computing.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a rigorous, analytical tool to bridge the gap between complex, physical hardware imperfections in quantum optics and the abstract mathematical tools required for quantum error correction.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper aims to characterize small Markovian errors in probabilistic, photon-heralded quantum operations between non-interacting quantum emitters.

**Main result.** The authors developed an analytic perturbative framework that successfully maps microscopic hardware imperfections (like source noise or phase miscalibrations) to abstract qubit-level noise models (Pauli error weights).

**Method.** The core method involves developing an analytic perturbative expansion built upon the Zero-Photon-Generation (ZPG) framework to derive closed-form solutions for process matrices and error weights.

**Model / system.** The system involves hybrid light-matter quantum systems using non-interacting emitters, single photons, and photon-number-resolving detectors. The dynamics are governed by a Lindblad master equation, analyzed via the ZPG framework.

**Key observables.** Process matrices ($\chi$ matrix), Pauli error weights ($\chi_{P,P}$), state fidelities ($F_{	ext{state}}^m$), and average gate fidelities ($F_{	ext{avg}}$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Error strengths ($\gamma_k$), noise parameters ($\gamma_1, \gamma_2, \gamma_3$), and the low-noise regime ($\epsilon$ expansion).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on perturbative expansions, valid in the low-noise regime, and often assumes ideal detector performance initially.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the conceptual pipeline from physical models to fault-tolerant design, and show quantitative comparisons between analytical perturbative predictions and exact numerical simulations for various noise sources.

**Paper structure.** The paper builds conceptually from the general ZPG framework to derive perturbative solutions for conditional maps, applies these solutions to benchmark specific gates (like CZ), and then analyzes various physical error sources (spin relaxation, dephasing, phase shifts) to calculate resulting Pauli error weights.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We develop an analytic perturbative framework that enables the analysis of small Markovian errors in probabilistic, photon-heralded quantum operations between non-interacting emitters. Building on and extending the Zero-Photon-Generation (ZPG) framework, we derive closed-form perturbative solutions that capture both ideal (zero-order) and noisy (low-order) gate dynamics conditioned on time-integrated photon counting. Our framework provides analytic solutions to process matrices and Pauli error weights up to leading order, bridging the gap between detailed physical imperfections of a system and its corresponding abstract Pauli noise models. Moreover, our framework captures imperfections across the full physical system stack, from photon generation to optical manipulation. We benchmark the resulting perturbative predictions on a repeat-until-success CZ gate against numerical simulations, demonstrating accurate modeling of source-induced noise, and then apply the same framework to analyze coherent phase-shifter miscalibrations as a representative example of optical-manipulation errors. The methods developed in this work enable physics-informed parameter tuning to optimize gate designs and develop tailored quantum error correction protocols toward fault-tolerant quantum computing using hybrid light--matter quantum systems.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2606.04096"></a>
### [Better Pauli Channel Learning with Maximum Likelihood Estimation](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04096v1)

**Authors:** Daniel Belkin, Faisal Alam, Matthew Thibodeau, Alireza Seif, Ewout van den Berg, Bryan K. Clark  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.04096v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5** · `quantum measurements` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.04096_figures/2606.04096_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Schematic illustration of our work. We give an algorithm that allows MLE to be used for channel learning. This gives better learning of noise models than previous approaches. When used for PEC on a quantum circuit, the resulting noise model also gives markedly better error-mitigated outputs.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper addresses the difficulty of characterizing noise in quantum computers by proposing an efficient Maximum Likelihood Estimation (MLE) technique. By mapping the complex likelihood function for 1D Pauli noise channels onto a tractable Bayesian network, the authors achieve significantly better noise parameter estimation than previous methods. This advancement promises more reliable error mitigation protocols for near-term quantum devices.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a concrete, computationally feasible pathway to perform high-precision quantum process tomography, which is crucial for building reliable quantum hardware and understanding decoherence mechanisms in solid-state or superconducting qubits.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The primary challenge is accurately estimating the unknown noise channel in noisy quantum devices, as standard tomography methods suffer from high sample complexity.

**Main result.** The authors show that Maximum Likelihood Estimation (MLE) can be made computationally tractable for 1D-local sparse Pauli-Lindblad channels by mapping the likelihood function to an efficiently-evaluable Bayesian network, leading to substantially improved tomography.

**Method.** The core method involves reformulating the likelihood function for the noise parameters into a Bayesian network structure, allowing for efficient inference using belief propagation, which outperforms empirical estimators like EPF.

**Model / system.** The system is a quantum computation process corrupted by a Pauli-Lindblad noise channel, modeled specifically as a 1D-local sparse Pauli channel. The analysis compares MLE against Empirical Pauli Fidelities (EPF) for parameter estimation.

**Key observables.** Mean squared difference between learned and true noise parameters ($\omega_i$), and the resulting fidelity/magnetization of error-mitigated observables.

**Important parameters / regimes.** System size (number of qubits), circuit depth, and the noise parameters $\{w_i\}$ defining the Pauli-Lindblad channel.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The noise is assumed to be Pauli-Lindblad, 1D-local, and sparse. The computational tractability relies on mapping the likelihood to a Bayesian network structure.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare MLE vs. EPF sample complexity (requiring MLE to use significantly fewer samples) and illustrate the mathematical reduction from quantum tensor networks to classical Bayesian network probability distributions.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first establishing the need for MLE over EPF, then deriving the computational simplification for 1D Pauli channels using Bayesian networks, and finally demonstrating the superior sample complexity and accuracy of the MLE approach through simulations.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Error mitigation in a noisy quantum device requires a very good estimate of the noise channel. The accuracy of probabilistic error cancellation is often limited by the high sample complexity of channel tomography. In principle, optimal sample complexity is attained by maximum likelihood estimation (MLE), but MLE is computationally challenging. We show that MLE can be made computationally tractable in certain cases of interest. For the common case of a 1D-local sparse Pauli-Lindblad channel, the likelihood function reduces to an efficiently-evaluable Bayesian network. We show that the resulting computation leads to substantially improved tomography. In addition, we demonstrate by simulation that this can lead to meaningful improvements to the overhead of error mitigation. We also discuss possible extensions of our algorithm to more general settings, such as non-1D circuits and non-Pauli errors.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.04723"></a>
### [Finite-Basis Duality Estimate for the Surface-Code Threshold under Correlated Bit-Flip Errors](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04723v1)

**Authors:** Masayuki Ohzeki  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.04723v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.04723_figures/2606.04723_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 1. (Color online) (a) Local correlated pair used by Chubb and Flammia. Opposite plaquette edges e, e′ carry quenched vari- ables xe, xe′, while the statistical-mechanical Ising spins sv live on vertices. The pair gives two edge terms and a four-spin term. (b) Smallest toroidal finite basis used in Eq. (9); red and blue arcs de- note non-overlapping correlated pairs, and periodic or antiperiodic seams give the four sectors Zαβ.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper develops a theoretical method using finite-basis duality to estimate the error threshold for the surface code under correlated noise. By systematically improving the basis size on a toroidal lattice, the authors provide a refined estimate for the critical threshold ($p_c = 0.10348$). This work advances the theoretical understanding of fault-tolerance thresholds in the presence of complex error correlations.

**Why it may be interesting.** The rigorous application of duality techniques to estimate critical thresholds in complex, correlated error models provides advanced theoretical tools applicable to quantum error correction codes.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper aims to accurately estimate the critical threshold ($p_c$) for the surface code when subjected to spatially correlated bit-flip errors.

**Main result.** By employing a finite-basis duality analysis on toroidal bases, the authors obtained an improved estimate for the critical point: $p_c = 0.10348$.

**Method.** The core method involves applying finite-basis duality analysis, utilizing periodic and twisted-periodic sectors on toroidal lattices to analyze the random-bond Ising model mapping.

**Model / system.** The system is the surface code under correlated bit-flip noise, which is mapped to a random-bond Ising model containing both two-body edge and four-body face interactions.

**Key observables.** The critical threshold ($p_c$) for error correction.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The error probability parameter ($p$) and the correlation structure parameter ($\eta = p_+/p_-$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on using local Boltzmann weights, quenched averages, and twisted-sector partition functions rather than a full Monte Carlo phase diagram.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates the local correlated pair used by previous work and the smallest toroidal finite basis used in the current analysis. Table I compares threshold estimates using various methods (single pair, $2 	imes 2$ basis, $4 	imes 4$ basis, and MC).

**Paper structure.** The argument progresses by showing that the finite-basis duality method systematically refines the estimate by increasing the size of the basis used in the calculation, moving from single-pair estimates to $2 	imes 2$ and then $4 	imes 4$ finite bases.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We apply finite-basis duality to a statistical-mechanical model introduced by Chubb and Flammia for the surface code under spatially correlated bit-flip noise. Their mapping gives a random-bond Ising model with both two-body edge interactions and four-body face interactions. The single-equation estimate based on the duality analysis is slightly deviated from their Monte Carlo estimate \(p_c=0.1004(6)\). Following the finite-basis and graph-polynomial strategy, we instead use periodic and twisted-periodic sectors on toroidal bases. We have obtained an improved estimate of the critical point, $p_c = 0.10348$.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.04099"></a>
### [Gravitationally Induced Quantum Decoherence of Macroscopic Objects](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04099v1)

**Authors:** Hiroki Takeda, Shogo Tomizuka, Takahiro Tanaka  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.04099v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.04099_figures/2606.04099_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. Contour plot of log10 Γ in the (∆x, M) plane, obtained by numerically evaluating the saturated limit r →∞of Eq. (4.24) for a dilute thermal gas with m = 28 amu ≃4.65×10−26 kg, Tgas = 300 K, Pgas = 1 atm ≃1.0×105 Pa. Dashed lines indicate constant values of log10 Γ, and shaded regions show its magnitude. </details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2606.04099_figures/2606.04099_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. Contour plots of log10(Γ/Γcol) in the (Rs, M) plane for fixed superposition sizes: (a) ∆x = 10−13 m, (b) ∆x = 10−8 m, and (c) ∆x = 10−3 m. In each panel, only the representative experimental regime relevant to the chosen value of ∆x is overlaid schematically.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This theoretical work calculates the decoherence rate for macroscopic quantum superpositions induced by gravitational coupling to an environment. Using the closed-time-path formalism, the authors find that while a logarithmic dependence on distance exists, the gravitational decoherence is generally negligible compared to standard gas collisions. This sets a quantitative limit on observing gravity-induced decoherence in current experimental setups.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a first-principles, quantitative benchmark for estimating decoherence rates from fundamental forces (gravity) in macroscopic quantum systems, which is crucial for testing quantum gravity effects.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates the quantum decoherence of massive objects when subjected to gravitational interactions with an environment.

**Main result.** The calculated gravitational decoherence function is found to be subdominant compared to conventional collisional decoherence under realistic, dilute gas conditions.

**Method.** The authors use the closed-time-path influence functional formalism to derive the reduced system dynamics, integrating out environmental degrees of freedom.

**Model / system.** The model involves a massive system particle prepared in a spatial superposition, coupled to linearized gravity and an environmental scalar field, specialized for a dilute nonrelativistic gas.

**Key observables.** Decoherence exponent/function ($\Gamma$), reduced density matrix ($ho_r$), and the environmental noise kernel.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Mass ($M$), separation distance ($\Delta x$), gas temperature ($T_{	ext{gas}}$), and various momentum/length scales ($k_{	ext{UV}}, k_{\sigma}$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis is performed in the nonrelativistic and quasi-static regime, utilizing approximations like the dipole-like parametrization for the system source.

**Figures summary.** Figure 2 shows the dependence of $\log_{10} \Gamma$ on mass and separation distance, while Figure 3 compares the ratio of gravitational to collisional decoherence.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically derives the influence functional, models the environmental kernel using windowing/convolution for physical regularization, and finally calculates the cumulative decoherence function by separating diagonal and off-diagonal contributions.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We formulate the gravitationally induced quantum decoherence of a massive object prepared in a spatial superposition. Starting from linearized gravity coupled to a massive system particle and an environmental scalar field, we derive a closed-time-path influence functional governing the reduced system dynamics. In the nonrelativistic and quasi-static regime, the decoherence exponent can be written as a bilinear functional of the difference of the system stress-energy tensors and an effective noise kernel obtained by dressing the environmental stress-energy tensor correlator with graviton propagators. We then apply this framework to the Newtonian long-range gravitational interaction and evaluate the resulting decoherence function for a dilute nonrelativistic gas modeled by finite wave packets and coarse-grained in time and space. By performing controlled approximations, we obtain analytic expressions for the cumulative decoherence function and show that the dominant contribution is accumulated logarithmically over a broad range of distances, while remaining subdominant to conventional collisional decoherence under realistic conditions.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.04681"></a>
### [Protecting Quantum States via the Super-Zeno Effect and Anticoherence](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04681v1)

**Summary.** This paper proposes a robust method for protecting quantum states by combining the Super-Zeno Effect with the concept of anticoherence in spin systems. By confining the state to a specially defined anticoherent subspace and applying periodic unitary pulses, the authors demonstrate that quantum evolution can be effectively 'frozen' against environmental noise to arbitrarily high orders in time. This offers a powerful theoretical framework for building more resilient quantum hardware.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a powerful theoretical toolkit for quantum control, showing how combining geometric constraints (anticoherence) with dynamical control (SZE) can achieve superior noise resilience compared to standard error correction methods.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The primary goal is to develop and analyze methods for protecting fragile quantum states from environmental noise and decoherence.

**Main result.** The combination of the Super-Zeno Effect (SZE) and exploiting spin anticoherent subspaces proves highly effective, allowing for the theoretical freezing of state evolution to arbitrarily high orders in time.

**Method.** The authors combine the SZE, which uses periodic unitary pulses, with the concept of anticoherence, which identifies subspaces where the system's evolution is naturally constrained, leading to high-order protection.

**Model / system.** The system modeled is a spin system subjected to noise characterized by an $n$-magnetic interaction Hamiltonian, $H \propto (B \cdot S)^n$. The protection mechanism relies on identifying and confining the state to a $q$-anticoherent subspace $P$.

**Key observables.** Fidelity ($F$) and Freezing Fidelity ($F_P$) are the key observables used to quantify the degree of state protection against noise.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The order of anticoherence ($q$), the degree of the Hamiltonian ($n$), and the number of super-Zeno pulses ($K$) are critical parameters governing the protection level.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes the initial state resides within the protected anticoherent subspace $P$, and the protocol relies on the ability to apply precise, periodic unitary pulses.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare the fidelity ($ar{F}$) achieved using standard Zeno vs. super-Zeno effects, and demonstrate that increasing the order of anticoherence ($q=1, 2, 3$) significantly improves the protection time for a given Hamiltonian.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically introduces the problem of noise protection, defines the mathematical tools (SZE, anticoherence), derives the conditions for protection, and then analyzes the performance using fidelity measures for various orders of protection.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We consider a spin system coupled to a noisy environment via an interaction Hamiltonian $H$ which is a polynomial of degree $n$ in the spin operators $S_i$ ($n$-magnetic Hamiltonian), and show that the use of the quantum super-Zeno effect in conjunction with a spin-anticoherent subspace, is highly effective in protecting the spin from the noise. When $n=1$, as in the case of unwanted rotations induced by stray magnetic fields, our scheme provides a quantum gyroscope, a concept that generalizes naturally to higher $n$ values (a quantum $n$-gyroscope). The gist of the proposed protocol is to use the quantum super-Zeno effect to confine a state to the subspace, and then exploit anticoherence to freeze its evolution within it.

</details>

### [Surface Charge Doping for Ion-Pairing Criticality in Confined Electrolytes](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04791v1)

**Authors:** Na Shen, Yabei Wu, Wenqing Zhang  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.04791v1>

**Summary.** This work analyzes how imposing a weak surface charge on confined electrolytes modifies their ionic stability. Using advanced statistical mechanics theory, the authors show that the surface charge acts as a doping agent, preventing complete ion depletion and suppressing the critical temperature for ion pairing. This reveals a universal scaling law governing the interplay between surface chemistry, confinement, and correlated ionic behavior.

**Why it may be interesting.** The concept of surface charge acting as a tunable 'thermodynamic doping field' to control phase transitions and transport in confined ionic systems is highly relevant to understanding interfacial phenomena in quantum materials and nanoscale devices.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates how weak surface charge doping modifies the ion-pairing criticality in quasi-two-dimensional electrolytes, specifically examining the decoupling between microscopic pairing and macroscopic ionicity.

**Main result.** Surface charge doping suppresses the association-driven thermodynamic instability by maintaining a finite screening floor via mobile counterions, causing the critical temperature to collapse onto a single scaled perturbation variable.

**Method.** The authors employ a generalized Debye-Huckel-Bjerrum theory, supplemented by Brownian-dynamics simulations, to analyze the free energy and stability criteria.

**Model / system.** The system is a monovalent 1:1 electrolyte confined in an angstrom-scale slit with planar walls carrying a uniform surface charge. The theory treats surface charge as a thermodynamic doping field influencing the Coulomb correlations.

**Key observables.** Screening strength ($\kappa_{	ext{eff}}^2$), ionic fractions ($I_{\pm}, I$), critical temperature ($T_c^*$), and in-plane diffusion coefficient ($D_{xy}$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Surface charge density ($\sigma$), slit width ($h$), intrinsic salt density ($ho_0$), and the dimensionless ratio $\chi = ho_0 / \sigma$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis primarily focuses on the weak surface-coupling regime ($h/\lambda_{	ext{GC}} \ll 1$) and assumes the counterions are part of the quasi-2D Coulomb fluid.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the difference between neutral and charged slits regarding ionicity, show the suppression of $T_c^*$ vs. $\sigma$, and demonstrate the collapse of this suppression onto a scaled perturbation variable $X_{\xi}$.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from establishing the physical problem (surface doping effects) to developing the generalized theory, analyzing the resulting decoupling of ion species, and finally validating the theoretical predictions using both scaling laws and explicit Brownian dynamics simulations.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Dielectric confinement strengthens Coulomb correlations in quasi-two-dimensional electrolytes and can promote Bjerrum pairing in charge-neutral slits. Here we use a generalized Debye-Huckel-Bjerrum theory to show that weak surface charge changes this picture by stoichiometrically doping the slit with mobile counterions. These counterions maintain a finite screening floor, decouple microscopic pairing from macroscopic ionicity, and shift association-driven criticality to lower temperatures. The critical-temperature suppression collapses onto a single scaled perturbation variable, revealing how surface charge and dielectric confinement jointly control charged nanofluidic slits. Brownian-dynamics tests further show that the same counterions are not always fully bulk-like diffusive: at low intrinsic salt density, explicit wall charge slows in-plane diffusion, whereas at higher intrinsic density the wall-induced diffusion penalty decreases and the mobile-counterion description becomes dynamically accurate. These results identify surface charge as a thermodynamic doping field that tunes both correlated ionic stability and the diffusion mechanism in nanofluidic confinement.

</details>

### [Coherent State Path Integral Monte Carlo](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.05013v1)

**Authors:** Riccardo Fantoni  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.05013v1>

**Summary.** This paper introduces the Coherent State Path Integral Monte Carlo (CSPIMC) method, a new technique for simulating quantum many-body liquids at finite temperature. It replaces the standard plane-wave basis with coherent states of a harmonic oscillator, offering a tunable parameter that enhances computational efficiency. The method successfully reproduces known thermodynamic limits for kinetic energy estimators.

**Why it may be interesting.** The ability to tune the harmonic oscillator stiffness ($\xi$) to improve computational efficiency by allowing larger imaginary time steps is a significant algorithmic advancement for simulating quantum dynamics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To develop a novel and computationally efficient quantum simulation method for studying many-body quantum liquids at finite temperature.

**Main result.** The proposed Coherent State Path Integral Monte Carlo (CSPIMC) method successfully reconstructs the thermal density matrix, showing convergence to known results while offering a tunable parameter ($\xi$) that allows for larger imaginary time steps.

**Method.** The method expands the high-temperature density matrix using the overcomplete set of single-particle coherent states of a Harmonic Oscillator, contrasting with conventional plane-wave path integral methods.

**Model / system.** The system is a many-body quantum liquid of identical particles (bosons/fermions) governed by a thermal density matrix $\hat{ho} = \exp(-eta \hat{H})$. The method is tested on free particles and extended to systems like liquid Helium.

**Key observables.** Kinetic energy ($\langle\hat{T}angle$), potential energy ($\langle\hat{V}angle$), and the factor $\phi$ used in the kinetic energy estimator.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Inverse temperature ($eta$), imaginary time step ($	au$), and the stiffness parameter ($\xi$) of the harmonic oscillator, which can be tuned.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The method does not solve the Feynman sign problem for fermions, and the kinetic action approximation relies on the properties of coherent states.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare path snapshots between CSPIMC and standard PIMC for free particles, and tables detail the dependence of the factor $\phi$ on simulation parameters.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the CSPIMC formalism by replacing plane waves with coherent states, derives the necessary path integral elements, analyzes the limiting cases (stiff HO, thermodynamic limit), and compares results against standard PIMC methods.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We propose a new quantum simulation method for a many body quantum liquid of identical particles at finite (non-zero) temperature. The new scheme expands the high temperature density matrix on the overcomplete set of single particles coherent states of John Rider Klauder instead of the usual plane waves as in conventional path integral methods. One is free to tune the elastic constant and/or the mass of the harmonic oscillator subtending the coherent states so as to maximize the computational efficiency of the algorithm. We prove that in the limit of an extremely stiff harmonic oscillator the results for the internal energy tends towards the correct expected values. Moreover we suggest that a stiff harmonic oscillator could allow the use of larger (imaginary) timesteps. This additional degree of freedom is the characteristic feature of our new algorithm and is not available in more conventional path integral methods.

</details>

### [High-density, high-mobility ultrathin spin-polarized two-dimensional electron gas at the polar/polar LaVO$_3$/KTaO$_3$ interface: Insights from first-principles calculations](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04252v1)

**Summary.** This theoretical study uses DFT+U calculations to investigate the LaVO3/KTaO3 interface, finding that the metallic 2DEG arises from charge transfer driven by polar discontinuity. The resulting 2DEG is highly confined, spin-polarized, and possesses an exceptionally small effective mass, suggesting potential for high-mobility quantum electronic applications.

**Why it may be interesting.** The study models charge transfer and electronic reconstruction in a strongly correlated oxide system, which is a key area for understanding emergent quantum phenomena in solid-state devices.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To uncover the microscopic origin and characterize the properties of the two-dimensional electron gas (2DEG) formed at the polar/polar LaVO3/KTaO3 interface.

**Main result.** The 2DEG is highly confined, spin-polarized, and originates from an electronic reconstruction driven by charge transfer across the interface, exhibiting an exceptionally small effective mass.

**Method.** First-principles density functional theory (DFT) calculations, incorporating DFT+U methods to treat strong electronic correlations in the Mott insulator component.

**Model / system.** The physical system is the LaVO3/KTaO3 heterostructure, modeled as a slab. The study investigates the electronic reconstruction mechanism that stabilizes the metallic state at the interface.

**Key observables.** Spin-up parabolic band effective mass ($m^*$), interfacial electron density, layer-resolved charge density, and polarization vectors.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Hubbard U and J parameters for V 3d and Ta 5d orbitals; comparison to the LaAlO3/SrTiO3 interface.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The metallic state arises from an electronic reconstruction to avoid the polar catastrophe; the A-AFM magnetic ordering is assumed to be the ground state.

**Figures summary.** Figures show layer-resolved PDOS confirming Ta 5d orbital contribution, band structures illustrating the parabolic 2DEG band, and charge density maps confirming 2D confinement at the interface.

**Paper structure.** The paper first establishes the physical context (polar/polar interface) and computational methods (DFT+U). It then analyzes the magnetic and polarization states, leading to the key finding of electronic reconstruction, which finally characterizes the resulting high-mobility, spin-polarized 2DEG.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The emergence of high-mobility two-dimensional electron gases (2DEGs) at oxide interfaces provides a fertile platform for exploring emergent quantum phenomena and next-generation oxide electronics. Here, using first-principles density functional theory (DFT) calculations, we investigate the microscopic origin of the 2DEG formed at the interface between the band insulator KTaO$_3$ (KTO) and the Mott insulator LaVO$_3$ (LVO). Although both constituents are insulating in bulk, the LVO/KTO heterostructure develops robust metallicity at the interface, consistent with experimental observations. Our calculations show that this metallic state originates from an electronic reconstruction driven by the polar discontinuity across the interface. To avoid the polar catastrophe on both the polar LVO film and the polar KTO substrate, electrons are transferred from the outer surfaces toward the interface, leading to hole accumulation in the surface VO$_2$ layer and electron accumulation in the interfacial TaO$_2$ layer. This charge redistribution stabilizes a highly confined and spin-polarized 2DEG localized at the interface. The electronic states forming the 2DEG are predominantly derived from interfacial Ta $5d_{xy}$ orbitals, confining carrier motion to the interfacial plane. Remarkably, the spin-up parabolic band hosting the 2DEG exhibits an exceptionally small effective mass, substantially lower than that of the prototypical LaAlO$_3$/SrTiO$_3$ interface, indicating the potential for enhanced carrier mobility. Furthermore, the calculated interfacial electron density is nearly an order of magnitude larger than that of LaAlO$_3$/SrTiO$_3$, consistent with experiment. These findings identify the LVO/KTO heterostructure as a promising platform for realizing high-density, high-mobility spin-polarized 2DEGs and for engineering correlated oxide interfaces for quantum electronic applications.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.04940"></a>
### [Convergence rates of Sum-of-Hermitian-Squares Hierarchies for the Pauli algebra](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04940v1)

**Authors:** Ali Almasi, Dávid Bugár, Cambyse Rouzé, Peter Brown  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.04940v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `analog quantum simulation` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.04940_figures/2606.04940_page2.jpg" width="500"><br>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2606.04940_figures/2606.04940_page3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 3</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2606.04940_figures/2606.04940_page4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 4</sub>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2606.04940_figures/2606.04940_page5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 5</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2606.04940_figures/2606.04940_page6.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 6</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper rigorously analyzes the convergence rate of Sum-of-Hermitian-Squares (SOHS) relaxations used to find the ground state energy of $n$-qubit systems. By extending techniques from commutative optimization to the non-commutative Pauli algebra, the authors derive an explicit error bound dependent on Krawtchouk polynomials. This provides a major quantitative advance in the mathematical analysis of quantum many-body problems.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides rigorous mathematical tools to analyze the convergence of quantum many-body approximations, which is vital for developing reliable quantum simulation algorithms and understanding the limits of current optimization techniques for quantum chemistry and materials science.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper develops explicit convergence rates for Sum-of-Hermitian-Squares (SOHS) relaxations applied to noncommutative polynomial optimization problems (NPO). This is crucial for accurately estimating ground state energies in quantum systems.

**Main result.** The authors provide a quantitative upper bound on the error, showing the convergence rate is governed by the smallest roots of Krawtchouk polynomials, representing the first such analysis for this class of problems.

**Method.** The technique extends the polynomial kernel method from commutative SOS hierarchies to the non-commutative setting of the Pauli algebra, utilizing specialized linear maps $\Phi_\eta$ and properties derived from Krawtchouk polynomials.

**Model / system.** The focus is on the ground state energy problem for $n$-qubit systems, formulated as an NPO problem over the Pauli algebra. The analysis uses the SOHS hierarchy, which generates a sequence of lower bounds ($H_r$) to the true minimum energy ($H_{\min}$).

**Key observables.** The primary observable is the error bound, $H_{\min} - H_r$, which is quantified in terms of the smallest root $\zeta_{n,r+1}$ of the Krawtchouk polynomial $K_{n,r+1}^{(4)}(x)$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The parameters include the number of qubits ($n$), the level of the hierarchy ($r$), the maximum Pauli weight ($d$), and the Krawtchouk polynomial roots $\zeta_{n,r+1}$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The derivation relies on constructing a linear map $\Phi_\eta$ satisfying specific algebraic properties (P0)-(P3) within the Pauli algebra structure.

**Paper structure.** The paper builds by first establishing the necessary algebraic framework (Pauli algebra, diagonal maps $A_k, E_k$) using Krawtchouk polynomials, then deriving the required linear map $\Phi_\eta$ that satisfies the necessary properties, and finally applying this structure to derive the explicit convergence rate bound for the SOHS hierarchy.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Moment/Sum-of-Hermitian-Squares relaxations for noncommutative polynomial optimization problems have become an important tool for analyzing problems within quantum theory. Despite their widespread success, little is known about their rate of convergence and, consequently, their accuracy. In this work, we develop explicit convergence rates for relaxations of noncommutative polynomial optimization problems generated from the Pauli algebra -- covering applications to the ground state energy problem for n-qubit systems. In particular, we show that the rate of convergence can be bounded in terms of the smallest roots of a family of orthogonal polynomials known as Krawtchouk polynomials. Our result represents the first quantitative analysis of the rate of convergence for relaxations of noncommutative polynomial optimization problems.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2606.04686"></a>
### [Digital Quantum Reservoir Computing for ATM Time Series Prediction](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04686v1)

**Summary.** This paper explores using digital Quantum Reservoir Computing (QRC) to forecast ATM cash demand time series on near-term quantum hardware. The methodology encodes time data into quantum rotations and uses measurements to feed a classical regression model. Although QRC underperforms classical benchmarks on point-wise errors, it shows promise in capturing temporal structure via the DTW metric.

**Why it may be interesting.** The work provides an empirical assessment of applying quantum machine learning techniques, specifically QRC, to complex, real-world time series data, which is relevant for understanding how quantum circuits can encode and process temporal correlations.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates using a digital quantum reservoir computing (QRC) framework to forecast multi-step time series data, specifically ATM cash demand.

**Main result.** While QRC models did not outperform the classical Prophet benchmark on standard error metrics (MAE, NMSE), they showed more competitive results using the Dynamic Time Warping (DTW) metric, suggesting partial ability to capture temporal structure.

**Method.** The approach involves encoding time series data into quantum rotation angles, processing it through parametrized quantum reservoirs (using ansätze like MERA), and reading out classical features via measurements to train a classical Ridge-regression model.

**Model / system.** The system is a digital QRC framework utilizing parametrized four-qubit reservoirs. The process involves sequential encoding of time steps into rotation angles and measuring single-qubit expectation values and two-qubit correlators to form the feature vector.

**Key observables.** Mean Absolute Error (MAE), Normalized Mean Squared Error (NMSE), and Dynamic Time Warping (DTW) metric.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Circuit ansatz (Baseline vs. MERA), reservoir memory ($m$), measurement observables (single vs. two-point correlators), and execution backend (Simulation vs. IQM Spark QPU).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Training is restricted to a classical Ridge-regression readout, and the study acknowledges error accumulation in multi-step forecasting.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the quantum circuit blocks for the baseline and MERA ansätze, and show performance comparisons (MAE, NMSE, DTW) across different prediction horizons and hardware backends.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically analyzes the impact of various architectural choices (ansätze, memory, observables) on time series forecasting performance, comparing the QRC model against the classical Prophet benchmark across multiple noise models and prediction horizons.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We investigate a digital quantum reservoir computing (QRC) framework for multi-step forecasting of automated teller machine (ATM) cash demand time series on near-term quantum devices. The proposed approach uses parametrized four-qubit reservoirs with a fixed structure exploiting partial measurement and reset, where temporal data is encoded in rotation angles. Training is restricted to a classical Ridge-regression readout. We systematically analyze the impact of the circuit ansatzë, reservoir memory, measurement-derived observables, and the execution backend on the forecasting performance. Experiments are performed with noiseless simulation, noise-aware emulation, and a real IQM Spark quantum processor. Although the QRC models do not outperform the classical Prophet benchmark in terms of Mean Absolute Error and Normalized Mean Squared Error metrics, they achieve more competitive results in Dynamic Time Warping metric, indicating a partial ability to capture temporal structure. These findings provide an empirical assessment of digital QRC for realistic financial forecasting and highlight both its current limitations and its potential on near-term quantum hardware.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2606.04548"></a>
### [Distributed Variational Quantum Optimisation by Entanglement-Selective Transport](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04548v1)

**Authors:** Edric Matwiejew, Pascal Elahi, Ugo Varetto  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.04548v1>

**Summary.** This paper introduces QESTO, a novel variational ansatz designed for distributed quantum optimization on multiple QPUs. It leverages pre-shared Bell pairs to encode and transport constraint information locally, avoiding costly non-local gates. Benchmarking shows QESTO significantly outperforms standard QAOA methods by efficiently concentrating probability amplitude on low-cost solutions.

**Why it may be interesting.** The focus on utilizing persistent entanglement to simulate long-range interactions while minimizing gate overhead is highly relevant for near-term quantum hardware architectures that struggle with high-fidelity, deep non-local gate sequences.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the challenge of performing distributed quantum optimization for graph-based discrete problems when computing the cost function requires non-local gates across multiple quantum processors.

**Main result.** The proposed QESTO ansatz achieves stronger convergence to low-cost solutions than equivalent partitioned QAOA and outperforms monolithic QAOA on benchmark tile-matching problems, suggesting persistent entanglement is key to efficient distributed computation.

**Method.** QESTO introduces a variational ansatz that uses persistent, pre-shared Bell pairs to encode local constraint information and facilitate amplitude transfer across distributed edges, minimizing the need for non-local gates during the optimization layers.

**Model / system.** The model involves quantum computation distributed over multiple Quantum Processing Units (QPUs) solving graph-based discrete optimization problems, such as weighted Wang tile-matching. The core mechanism relies on local operations and shared entanglement (Bell pairs) to simulate long-range interactions.

**Key observables.** Mean normalized optimality gap (g), mean feasible probability of finding low-cost tilings, and the concentration of probability mass near the optimum solution.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Ansatz depth (p), the number of distributed edges ($|E_{dist}|$), and variational parameters $\{\gamma_r, eta_r, 	ext{tr}, \eta_r\}$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The approach assumes the ability to utilize persistent Bell pairs, which reduces the overhead associated with repeatedly executing non-local gates compared to gate-teleported methods.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the QESTO circuit structure for two QPUs sharing edges, and comparative plots show the mean normalized optimality gap and feasible probability versus ansatz depth for QESTO versus reference QAOA methods.

**Paper structure.** The paper first introduces the problem and the QESTO ansatz, detailing its local and entanglement-based layers. It then benchmarks QESTO against subgraph-QAOA and monolithic QAOA on specific tile-matching ensembles, presenting quantitative results on convergence and probability distribution.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Distributed quantum optimisation is challenging because computing the problem cost function across multiple quantum processors requires non-local gates, which can incur overhead in latency and fidelity. Here we introduce QESTO, a distributed variational ansatz for graph-based discrete optimisation that requires only persistent pre-shared Bell pairs for remote operations. Using local operations, it encodes local constraint information in the Bell pairs that is leveraged to produce amplitude transfer towards globally valid distributed solution states. QESTO requires one Bell pair per distributed edge of the problem graph and, after initialisation of the Bell states, uses no non-local gates. On two bounded weighted Wang tile-matching problem ensembles, QESTO achieves stronger convergence to low-cost tilings than equivalently partitioned QAOA with no distributed gates at ansatz depths of two or higher, and exceeds the mean performance of monolithic QAOA at the deepest studied depth in both ensembles. These results suggest that persistent entanglement can support useful variational communication while reducing per-layer non-local gate overhead.

</details>
