</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2606.04786_figures/2606.04786_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4. Comparison of selection-optimization strategies within LiH (4,6), BeH2 (6,7) and H2O</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2606.04786_figures/2606.04786_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 5. Comparison of selection-optimization strategies in stretched LiH (4, 6), BeH2 (6, 7) and</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper improves the resource efficiency of energy-based operator selection in fermionic ADAPT-VQE by introducing an exact Hamiltonian transformation. This technique significantly reduces the measurement overhead required to score potential operators, making the selection process competitive with gradient-based methods. The results demonstrate that this efficient approach helps achieve accurate quantum chemical results for molecules even in highly correlated regimes.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work directly tackles the resource scaling of quantum chemistry simulations, which is crucial for applying VQE to complex, strongly correlated molecular systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The primary challenge addressed is the computational bottleneck associated with reconstructing the one-parameter energy landscape during energy-based operator selection in fermionic ADAPT-VQE.

**Main result.** The authors introduce an exact Hamiltonian transformation that reformulates the energy landscape, reducing the measurement overhead of energy-based selection by about a factor of two, bringing it close to gradient-based methods.

**Method.** The method involves using an exact Hamiltonian transformation to efficiently calculate the energy cost function $E^{(n+1)}_g(	heta_g)$ for operator selection, which is then used within the ADAPT-VQE framework.

**Model / system.** The study is applied to fermionic quantum chemistry calculations for molecules like LiH, BeH2, and H2O, using the electronic Hamiltonian in second quantization.

**Key observables.** Energy convergence relative to the FCI reference, selection cost (measured in circuit evaluations), and overall resource cost.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The comparison spans weakly correlated (equilibrium) and highly correlated (stretched) molecular geometries.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The efficiency gain relies on the mathematical identity derived from the exact Hamiltonian transformation, which simplifies the calculation of the energy cost function.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare the energy convergence and the relative cost (selection vs. optimization) of various ADAPT-VQE strategies (Gradient vs. Energy-based) across different molecular geometries.

**Paper structure.** The paper first establishes the ADAPT-VQE framework and the cost of energy-based selection. It then details the exact Hamiltonian transformation to derive a resource-efficient energy cost function. Finally, it benchmarks this improved method against gradient-based approaches across various molecular systems and optimization strategies.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The energy-based approach to operator selection in ADAPT-VQE relies on reconstructing the one-parameter energy landscape for each operator in the pool. In fermionic implementations, the cost of reconstructing this energy landscape often becomes a bottleneck. We address this issue through an exact Hamiltonian transformation that reformulates the one-parameter energy landscape according to a generator-dependent fragmentation of the transformed Hamiltonian. While our method is mathematically identical to standard fermionic Rotoselect, it effectively reduces its cost by about a factor of two, bringing it close to that of gradient-based ADAPT-VQE. We use this formulation to benchmark the gradient-based and energy-based selection approaches in combination with two ansatz-optimization strategies -- `last', where only the appended operator is optimized, or `full', where the full ansatz is re-optimized -- and with both fixed-orbital and orbital-optimized formulations. The benchmark comprises $\text{LiH}$, $\text{BeH}_2$, and $\text{H}_2\text{O}$ at both equilibrium and stretched geometries. In the weakly correlated regime, the `last' optimization strategy combined with energy-based selection enables the efficient construction of an accurate ansatz, while avoiding any VQE optimization. As correlation increases, full ansatz re-optimization and orbital optimization become the main factors governing convergence and overall resource cost. These results show that exact Hamiltonian transformations provide an effective route to reducing the measurement overhead of fermionic energy-based ADAPT-VQE. Moreover, the benchmark clarifies the relative role of operator scoring approach, re-optimization strategy, and orbital treatment in the performance of ADAPT-VQE.

</details>

**Summary.** This work develops a theoretical framework using random matrix theory to analyze unsupervised Hebbian networks. It shows that the network's ability to generalize is directly encoded in the spectral structure of its interaction matrix. A clear spectral separation between signal and noise directions is predicted to be the necessary condition for successful reconstruction of underlying patterns.

**Why it may be interesting.** The rigorous mathematical framework connecting spectral properties (eigenvalue distribution) to functional performance (generalization) provides a powerful, generalizable diagnostic tool applicable to complex quantum or classical learning systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To derive the asymptotic spectral properties of the interaction matrix in unsupervised Hebbian networks and establish spectral criteria that predict the network's ability to generalize beyond training data.

**Main result.** Generalization capability is predicted by the spectral transition: the existence of two separated spectral bulks (signal/noise separation) indicates successful generalization, while a merged single-bulk phase implies failure.

**Method.** The analysis combines random matrix theory and replica methods to compute the quenched free energy and derive the limiting spectral density of the interaction matrix.

**Model / system.** The model is an unsupervised Hebbian network trained on noisy realizations of hidden ground-truth vectors, extending classical Hopfield models from simple memory retrieval to latent structure extraction.

**Key observables.** The asymptotic spectrum ($	ilde{ho}'(\lambda)$), the spectral gap (separation into two bulks), and the overlap measures (archetype and example overlaps).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Network load ($\alpha$), noise parameter ($r$), regularization parameter ($t$), and the ratio $\alpha M$ which dictates the spectral regime.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on the thermodynamic limit ($N 	o \infty$) and the use of the replica-symmetric ansatz for order parameters.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the theoretical spectral predictions versus numerical simulations, showing the spectral evolution under regularization and comparing spectral analysis results with overlap measurements across different load regimes.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first deriving the spectral density using random matrix theory and replica methods, then analyzing the effect of regularization ($t$) on this spectrum, and finally establishing the spectral gap as the necessary and sufficient condition for successful generalization.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We consider an unsupervised Hebbian network where the pairwise interactions among neurons are built on noisy realizations of hidden ground-truth vectors. Unlike classical Hopfield models, designed as memory devices, this class of networks can be employed to extract latent structure and generalize beyond the "training" set. By combining random matrix theory and replica methods, we derive the asymptotic spectrum of the corresponding interaction matrix and show that the onset of generalization is controlled by a sharp spectral transition. Depending on the quality and the size of the accessible dataset, the spectrum displays either two separated bulks, encoding informative and noisy directions, or a merged single-bulk phase where such distinction is lost. We show that, when coupled with regularization, the emergence of such a spectral split predicts the network's capability to reconstruct the ground-truth vectors from corrupted samples.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.05148"></a>
### [Variational low-energy subspaces for chemically accurate excited states](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.05148v1)

**Authors:** Clemens Giuliani, Rocco Martinazzo, Giuseppe Carleo, Riccardo Rossi  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.05148v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.05148_figures/2606.05148_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1. Automatic optimization of the low-energy subspace. (a-b) EXIDOS(256,32) calculation of LiH spectrum, oscillator strength f and dipole moment µ, compared with FCI. (c-d) All-electron EXIDOS(24, 256) and EXIDOS(33, 256) results for the N2 and CO molecules, respectively, are compared with the benchmark results from Ref. [23, 24]. We note that panel (c-d) includes additional low-lying triplet states absent from the benchmark.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2606.05148_figures/2606.05148_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>

<sub>Figure 3. Excited states with different electronic character. (a) Charge-transfer excitation in HCl. Ωquantifies the doubly excited character of each excited state: Ω→0 indicates a state...</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2606.05148_figures/2606.05148_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 4. (a) Bond torsion of the ethylene molecule starting from the planar geometry. (b) Bond pyramidization of the ethylene molecule starting from the twisted-orthogonal geometry. </details>

**Summary.** This paper introduces EXIDOS, a novel, low-scaling variational method for calculating electronic excited states. It reformulates the complex excited-state optimization into an iterative, ground-state-like problem solvable via generalized eigenvalue equations. The method achieves chemical accuracy across diverse molecular systems, making it a powerful tool for photochemistry and spectroscopy.

**Why it may be interesting.** This provides a highly scalable, fully variational route to excited states that bypasses the need for explicit symmetry assumptions or state-specific knowledge, which is a major bottleneck in non-adiabatic dynamics simulations.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Accurately calculating electronic excited states is crucial for photochemistry and spectroscopy, but high-level methods scale steeply and require prior knowledge of the state's character.

**Main result.** The developed EXIDOS method achieves chemical accuracy for numerous excited states in various molecules by reformulating the optimization as an iterated ground-state-like problem.

**Method.** The method reformulates the variational excited-state optimization as an iterated ground-state problem, solving a generalized eigenvalue problem within a low-energy subspace spanned by non-orthogonal Slater determinants.

**Model / system.** The work applies to general molecular systems, benchmarked on molecules like N2, CO, HCl, C2, and NH3, using the electronic molecular Hamiltonian.

**Key observables.** Excitation energies, oscillator strengths, dipole moments, and the ability to characterize different state types (charge-transfer, Rydberg, double excitations).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The method is controlled only by the number of states ($K$) and the number of determinants per state ($	ext{ND}$); accuracy is benchmarked against FCI and high-level CC methods.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The method assumes the overlap matrix $\mathbf{S}$ is non-singular and uses an unconstrained Slater determinant basis, which is noted to not be strictly size-consistent.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare EXIDOS results against FCI/CCSDTQ for spectra, show potential energy curves for C2, and illustrate the characterization of different excited states (charge-transfer, double excitations, Rydberg) in various molecules.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically develops the theoretical framework by reformulating the excited-state optimization into a generalized eigenvalue problem, detailing the EXIDOS algorithm, and validating its performance across diverse chemical benchmarks.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Accurate electronic excited states are essential for photochemistry, spectroscopy and non-adiabatic molecular dynamics, but high-level calculations often scale steeply and require prior knowledge of the target state's character or symmetry. Here we show that variational excited-state optimization can be reformulated as an iterated ground-state-like problem for a low-energy subspace of the electronic Hamiltonian. Applying this variational principle to non-orthogonal Slater determinants leads to EXIDOS, an automatic method for excited state calculations controlled only by the number of states and determinants per state. EXIDOS optimizes multiple excited states simultaneously, without explicit orthogonality constraints or imposed spin and point-group symmetries. Benchmarks against FCI and state-of-the-art quantum chemistry methods show chemical accuracy for a multitude of states in N$_2$ and CO, charge-transfer states in HCl, Rydberg states in NH$_3$, double excitations and extended potential-energy curves in C$_2$, and avoided crossings and conical intersections in ethylene. These results establish EXIDOS as a low-scaling, fully variational route to chemically accurate excited states.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.04533"></a>
### [Vibrational model of entropy in dense two-dimensional fluids](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04533v1)

**Authors:** Sergey Khrapak  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.04533v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.04533_figures/2606.04533_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. (Color online) Excess entropy of the 2D OCP with Coulomb (∝1/r) interaction versus the coupling parameter Γ. The solid line corresponds to a fit based on MC simula- tion results [44]. The dashed line corresponds to the present theoretical model.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2606.04533_figures/2606.04533_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>

<sub>Figure 4 shows a comparison between the vibrational approximation to excess entropy and the numerical data for the 2D Yukawa fluid with κ = 1, 2, and 3. For the 2D Yukawa fluid, the ratio ωE/ω0 is only weakly dependent on Γ, but drops considerably as κ increases (see Fig. 4 from Ref. [42]). We use the values reported in Ref. [50], corresponding to the vicinity of the fluid-solid phase tran- sition: ωE/ω0 ≃0.5159 (κ = 1), ≃0.3297 (κ = 2), and ≃0.1893 (κ = 3). </details>

**Summary.** This paper develops and applies a vibrational model to calculate the excess entropy of dense two-dimensional fluids, extending a known 3D technique to 2D systems. It successfully tests this generalized model against various 2D plasmas and fluids, showing strong agreement with simulation results. The work provides a powerful theoretical tool for characterizing the thermodynamics of strongly coupled 2D matter.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on classical statistical mechanics, the rigorous application of vibrational models and the comparison of theoretical approximations (Einstein vs. DR-based) to simulation data mirrors the systematic theoretical development seen in many-body quantum systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper generalizes a vibrational model of entropy, previously used for 3D dense fluids, to accurately calculate the excess entropy of two-dimensional (2D) fluids.

**Main result.** The derived vibrational model provides a universal and simple generic form for the excess entropy ($s_{ex}$) that shows good agreement with numerical simulation data for various 2D systems.

**Method.** The excess entropy is calculated using a vibrational paradigm, approximating the partition function and relating $s_{ex}$ to the excess thermal energy and free energy via established thermodynamic relations.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on dense 2D fluids, including one-component plasmas (OCP) with Coulomb and logarithmic interactions, 2D dipole fluids, and 2D Yukawa fluids.

**Key observables.** Excess entropy ($s_{ex}$), excess thermal internal energy ($u_{th}$), and the coupling parameter ($\Gamma$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Coupling parameter ($\Gamma$), characteristic frequency ($\omega_E$), and plasma frequency ($\omega_0$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The model relies on the Einstein approximation for the vibrational density of states, and the derived equations are most accurate in the strongly coupled regime ($\Gamma \gg 1$).

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 and Figure 2 compare the calculated excess entropy of 2D OCPs (Coulomb and logarithmic) against existing Monte Carlo simulation data, demonstrating the model's accuracy.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the generalization of the vibrational model to 2D, derives the universal expression for $s_{ex}$, and then applies this framework to test its accuracy against known results for multiple 2D plasma and fluid models.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

A vibrational paradigm of atomic dynamic in dense fluids is known to provide useful insight on the transport and thermodynamic properties of fluids in three dimensions. In this paper, a vibrational model is generalized to describe the excess entropy of two-dimensional (2D) fluids. A simple practical implementation of this model is demonstrated to deliver accurate results for various systems, such as one-component plasmas with Coulomb and logarithmic interactions, a 2D fluid of dipole particles, and a 2D Yukawa fluid. The applicability limits, relevance to three-dimensional fluids, relations to other 2D phenomena, and potential practical applications are briefly discussed.

</details>

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.04976"></a>
### [Effective and Floquet Hamiltonians for High Frequency Driving and Floquet-induced Heating in Quantum Spin Chains](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04976v1)

**Authors:** Mayukh Bandyopadhyay, Vinod Ashokan  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.04976v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `non-equilibrium dynamics` **4/5** · 🔥 `scars & prethermalization` **4/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **3/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **3/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **3/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5** · `exotic spin models in light-matter` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.04976_figures/2606.04976_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2606.04976_figures/2606.04976_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. Stroboscopic dynamics of the energy density and entanglement entropy density together in a clean periodically driven TFIM in a parallel field. Parameters: g/J = 1.2, h/J = 2.536, Ω/J = 12.458 and L = 12.</sub>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2606.04976_figures/2606.04976_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper analyzes how disorder and high-frequency driving control the non-equilibrium dynamics of quantum spin chains. By studying entanglement entropy and energy absorption, the authors distinguish between thermalization, prethermalization, and a robust Floquet-MBL phase. The findings highlight that disorder is crucial for stabilizing non-equilibrium, localized states against external driving.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work directly addresses non-equilibrium dynamics in disordered quantum systems, which is central to understanding thermalization, localization, and the stability of quantum states under external driving fields, highly relevant to AMO physics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the non-equilibrium dynamics of a disordered, periodically driven quantum spin chain, focusing on the competition between interaction, disorder, and external driving.

**Main result.** The research establishes that strong disorder and high driving frequencies can suppress energy absorption and lead to a stable Floquet-Many-Body Localized (Floquet-MBL) regime, preventing thermalization.

**Method.** The analysis employs Floquet theory, utilizing the van Vleck inverse-frequency expansion to derive effective Hamiltonians, alongside numerical simulations to track energy and entanglement entropy.

**Model / system.** The system is a disordered, periodically driven quantum spin chain (e.g., transverse-field Ising model) governed by a time-periodic Hamiltonian incorporating nearest-neighbor interactions, disorder fields, and a time-dependent drive.

**Key observables.** Entanglement entropy density, energy absorption/energy density, and the comparison between localized/prethermal and thermalized dynamic regimes.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Interaction strength (J), disorder strengths (W), driving frequency ($\Omega$), and the ratio of disorder to interaction scales.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on the high-frequency expansion (van Vleck expansion) to approximate the effective static Hamiltonian, and numerical simulations are limited to small system sizes.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate stroboscopic dynamics showing bounded energy density oscillations and slow, saturating entanglement growth under strong disorder and high driving frequencies, contrasting with thermalization signatures at low frequencies.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically analyzes the system's evolution by comparing different parameter regimes: low frequency (thermalization), high frequency without disorder (prethermalization), and high frequency with strong disorder (Floquet-MBL).

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study the non-equilibrium dynamics of a disordered periodically driven quantum spin chain, with the competition between the interaction, disorder, and Floquet driving being of particular interest. We study dynamics of entanglement entropy, energy absorption to characterize dynamical regimes of the system whether it stays in the Floquet-MBL(many-body localization) region or thermalized region. Starting with a product state in the computational basis, followed by reduced density matrix which in turn gives rise to the entanglement entropy density. With the strength of the interaction, the transverse field, the parallel field, the disorder strength W and the driving frequency, we discover the distinct behaviors of fast delocalization and logarithmic entanglement growth and long-lasting memory of the initial state, indicative of localized or prethermal Floquet regimes. We observe that strong disorder arrests transport and enables slow entanglement dynamics, whereas strong driving frequency arrests energy absorption and creates a long-lived non-equilibrium state. Conversely, weak disorder or low driving frequency leads to delocalization. The outcomes show strong support for non-equilibrium phases in driven many-body systems.

</details>

### [Many body localization in Disordered One-Dimensional Fermi-Hubbard Model](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04948v1)

**Authors:** Harmanjeet Kaur, Vinod Ashokan  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.04948v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `non-equilibrium dynamics` **4/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **3/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **3/5** · `scars & prethermalization` **3/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.04948_figures/2606.04948_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>

The transition from rapid growth (ergodic phase) to slow logarithmic growth (many-body localized phase) is clearly visible.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This work examines the non-equilibrium dynamics of the disordered 1D Fermi-Hubbard model to study Many-Body Localization. By tracking imbalance and entanglement entropy, the authors demonstrate a transition from thermalizing (ergodic) behavior at weak disorder to a non-ergodic state at strong disorder. Crucially, they find evidence of partial decoupling between charge and spin degrees of freedom in this localized regime.

**Why it may be interesting.** The interplay between disorder, interactions, and dimensionality in determining thermalization pathways is central to modern condensed matter theory, making this highly relevant for understanding strongly correlated quantum materials.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the non-equilibrium dynamics and the breakdown of thermalization in the presence of disorder, specifically focusing on the phenomenon of Many-Body Localization (MBL).

**Main result.** The system exhibits a crossover from ergodic to non-ergodic behavior controlled by disorder strength, revealing partial decoupling between charge and spin sectors.

**Method.** The analysis uses time evolution simulations following a quantum quench, employing observables like sublattice imbalance and bipartite entanglement entropy.

**Model / system.** The model is the disordered one-dimensional Fermi-Hubbard model, described by a Hamiltonian including tunneling, on-site interaction (U), and random on-site disorder potential (V_i).

**Key observables.** Sublattice imbalance ($I(t)$), Spin-Charge Imbalance ($I_c(t), I_s(t)$), and Bipartite Entanglement Entropy ($S(t)$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Disorder strength ($W$), Interaction strength ($U$), and the initial state (e.g., Charge-Density-Wave state).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on characterizing the dynamics through specific conserved quantities and entanglement measures, noting that complete MBL requires specific symmetry breaking.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the time evolution of charge and spin imbalances for varying disorder strengths, showing decay in weak disorder and persistence in strong disorder. Entanglement entropy plots show characteristic logarithmic growth in the localized phase.

**Paper structure.** The paper analyzes the system dynamics by first establishing the Hamiltonian and key observables. It then contrasts the weak disorder (ergodic) regime with the strong disorder (non-ergodic) regime using imbalance measurements, followed by an analysis of entanglement entropy to confirm the MBL signatures.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We investigate the non-equilibrium dynamics of the disordered one-dimensional Fermi-Hubbard model with a focus on many-body localization. The system is initialized in a charge-density-wave-state, and its time evolution is analyzed through sublattice imbalance (spin and charge), and bipartite entanglement entropy. A clear crossover from ergodic to non-ergodic behavior is observed with increasing disorder strength. In the weak disorder regime, rapid decay of imbalance and the fast growth of entanglement indicate efficient thermalization. In contrast, a strong disorder leads to persistent imbalance and slow dynamics, signaling the breakdown of ergodicity. The charge and spin sectors exhibit distinct relaxation behavior, providing evidence for partial decoupling between these degrees of freedom. Furthermore, in the interacting regime, the entanglement entropy shows slow logarithmic growth, reflecting the dephasing-driven dynamics characteristic of the many-body localized phase. These results highlight the interplay between disorder and interactions in determining the dynamical properties of the system and establish robust signatures of many-body localization in the Fermi-Hubbard model.

</details>

### [Local-to-global heating crossover in chains of nanomagnets: A two-scale analytical framework](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04724v1)

**Authors:** H. Kachkachi

<sub>Figure 6 shows θ(ξ, τ) at τ = 0.25, 0.5, 1.0, 2.0 for Λ = 42, N = 7, ∆ξ = 7, ˜b = −0.52, β = 0.017 (localized regime, well below ˜bc). **Summary.** This work establishes a comprehensive two-scale analytical framework to model heat transfer in nanomagnet chains under alternating magnetic fields. By rigorously coarse-graining the nanoscale physics, the authors predict that realistic systems exhibit collective heating, with temperature fluctuations at the microkelvin level. The methodology is valuable for understanding energy dissipation in complex, coupled mesoscopic systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** The rigorous development of a multi-scale, non-equilibrium thermal transport model, especially one that quantifies the crossover between local and global energy dissipation, provides advanced tools applicable to open quantum systems where environmental coupling and spatial averaging are crucial.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper develops a two-scale framework to study heat generation and thermal transport in chains of nanomagnets subjected to an alternating magnetic field, focusing on the crossover between localized and global heating regimes.

**Main result.** For realistic magnetic hyperthermia systems, the model predicts the system operates in a collective heating regime, with local temperature variations at the $\sim\mu	ext{K}$ level, which is currently experimentally unresolvable.

**Method.** The authors employ a rigorous two-scale analytical formalism involving spatial and temporal coarse-graining, solving the resulting coupled equations using modal decomposition.

**Model / system.** The system consists of one-dimensional chains of nanomagnets (e.g., magnetite) embedded in a matrix, where heat generation is modeled as a localized, temperature-dependent source term governed by magnetic losses.

**Key observables.** Spatial correlation length, temperature variance, and the crossover condition governed by the competition between heat generation, diffusion, and dipolar coupling.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Nanoscale volumetric loss coefficient ($L_m$), macroscopic loss coefficient ($L_N$), Knudsen number ($Kn$), and the dimensionless coupling strength ($\gamma$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The continuum Fourier description is valid because the Knudsen number is much less than one ($Kn \ll 1$) for the matrices considered.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the dependence of Specific Loss Power (SLP) on frequency and volume, and compare the thermal behavior under Dirichlet (fixed boundary) versus Neumann (insulated boundary) conditions.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from deriving the nanoscale heat equation, through the rigorous process of spatial and temporal coarse-graining to obtain an assembly-scale equation, and finally analyzing the stability and crossover criteria using modal decomposition.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We develop a two-scale analytical formalism to study heat generation and thermal transport in one-dimensional systems of nanomagnets subjected to a uniform alternating magnetic field. At the nanoscale, each nanomagnet acts as a localized, temperature-dependent heat source governed by its magnetic response, dipolar interactions, and interfacial coupling to the matrix, characterized by a nanoscale volumetric loss coefficient $L_m$. After spatial and temporal averaging, we obtain a coarse-grained assembly-scale equation with effective heating terms and a macroscopic loss coefficient $L_N$.   Using modal decomposition, we solve both equations exactly under Dirichlet and Neumann boundary conditions and establish explicit conditions for a local-to-global heating crossover; this is governed by the competition between heat generation, diffusion, dipolar coupling, and hierarchical losses. The crossover is quantified through the spatial correlation length and temperature variance, with stability criteria incorporating both diffusion and nanoscale losses. The coarse-graining procedure is derived rigorously, and its systematic approximation errors are quantified.   For prototypical magnetic hyperthermia systems, such as magnetite nanomagnets in water, our formalism reveals that realistic parameters place these systems firmly in the collective heating regime, with local temperature variations at the $\simμ$K level, which is currently unresolvable experimentally. The continuum Fourier description used here is validated by a Knudsen-number analysis ($\mathrm{Kn} \ll 1$ for amorphous polymer and aqueous matrices).

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2606.04341"></a>
### [Perfect Nonreciprocal Axion-polaritons](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04341v1)

**Authors:** Abhinava Chatterjee, Chao-Xing Liu  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.04341v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `driven-dissipative phase transition` **3/5** · `interference shaping light` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **2/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **1/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `correlated cavity matter` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5** · `spintronics-quantum-optics interface` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.04341_figures/2606.04341_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. (a) The dynamical axion mode interacting with pho- tons. In the presence of both external electric and magnetic field E0, B0 along y and z directions respectively, the photons from the right (ωphoton, −k) and from the left (ωphoton, +k) couple to the axion mode with different coupling strengths g+ ̸= g−(defined in the section perfect nonreciprocity). (b) The frequency of the axion-polariton modes in the presence (E0 ̸= 0 - black) and absence of an external electric field (E0 = 0 - red) as a function of momentum k. The momen- tum axis is in units of cm−1, obtained using the conversion 1 GHz = 0.0333 cm−1. The magnitude of the group veloc- ity |vg| of the (c) upper and (d) lower...</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2606.04341_figures/2606.04341_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. (a) Perfect nonreciprocity at critical electromagnetic fields Ec 0, Bc 0 given by Ec 0 = c′Bc 0. (b) The axion-polariton dispersion at the critical external field. (c) The axion-photon coupling strengths ga(k) as a function of momentum k at critical electromagnetic fields Ec 0, Bc 0. We use E = c′B, B = 1.5 GHz, m = 40 GHz, v = 0.005 and c′ = 0.3.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2606.04341_figures/2606.04341_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. (a) The axion insulator as an optical isolator. (b) The transmissivities |tLR|2 and |tRL|2 as a function of photon frequency ω. We use E = c′B , B = 1.5 GHz, m = 40 GHz, v = 0 and c′ = 0.3.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper theoretically investigates axion-polaritons in a dynamical axion insulator subjected to external electric and magnetic fields. It demonstrates that these systems exhibit intrinsic nonreciprocity, leading to the prediction of an optical isolator. The effect is realized in a 'perfect nonreciprocity' regime where one direction of light propagates freely while the opposite direction is strongly attenuated.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant to quantum optics and open quantum systems as it demonstrates a controllable, engineered mechanism for directional light control (optical isolation) using fundamental material properties (axion coupling).

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper aims to characterize nonreciprocal axion-polaritons, which are hybrid modes exhibiting directional asymmetry in their dispersion relation ($\omega(k) 
eq \omega(-k)$).

**Main result.** They identify a regime of perfect nonreciprocity where one direction's photon decouples from the axion while the counter-propagating photon strongly hybridizes, realizing an optical isolator.

**Method.** The analysis involves solving coupled equations of motion for axion and photon fields, deriving dispersion relations, and calculating transmission/reflection coefficients using boundary value problems.

**Model / system.** The system is a dynamical axion insulator coupled to electromagnetic fields, described by a Lagrangian involving axion fluctuations ($	heta$) and photon fields ($A$). Nonreciprocity requires breaking both inversion and time-reversal symmetry using external $\mathbf{E}_0$ and $\mathbf{B}_0$ fields.

**Key observables.** Dispersion relation $\omega(k)$, group velocity asymmetry $|\mathbf{v}_g(\mathbf{k})|$, and transmission coefficients $|t_{LR}|^2$ vs. $|t_{RL}|^2$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The perfect nonreciprocity limit is achieved when the external fields satisfy $E = c'B$. The analysis also considers the axion damping rate $\Gamma$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Approximations include $v \ll c'$ and the use of a small dissipative term ($\Gamma$) to model finite axion quasiparticle lifetime.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the coupling mechanism showing different coupling strengths for right- and left-moving photons, and show the calculated transmission coefficients $|t_{LR}|^2$ and $|t_{RL}|^2$ for both nonreciprocal ($E=c'B$) and reciprocal ($E=0$) cases.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from establishing the coupled equations and the requirement for nonreciprocity, to calculating the dispersion relation and coupling strengths, and finally solving the boundary value problem for transmission/reflection coefficients to demonstrate the optical isolator effect.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Dynamical axion describes a massive axion quasiparticle that arises from fluctuations of the magnetic order parameter. These fluctuations couple to electromagnetic fields, forming axion-polaritons -- collective modes that are physically magnon-polaritons. We show that under appropriate static external electric and magnetic fields, axion-polaritons acquire a nonreciprocal dispersion, $ω(\textbf{k}) \neq ω(-\textbf{k})$, arising intrinsically from direction-dependent axion-photon coupling. Strikingly, we identify a regime of \textit{perfect nonreciprocity} in which the photon propagating in one direction is completely decoupled from the axion while the counter-propagating photon hybridizes strongly. Furthermore, the nonreciprocal dispersion manifests as an optical isolator -- a device transmitting light preferentially in one direction. Our results establish nonreciprocal axion-polaritons as a powerful probe of axion quasiparticles and suggest experimentally accessible routes for their detection.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2606.04919"></a>
### [Very strong light-matter coupling in patterned GaAs heterostructures](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04919v1)

**Authors:** David de la Fuente Pico, Johannes Bürger, Antonio Gianfrate, Jesper Levinsen, Meera M. Parish, Daniele Sanvitto, Francesca Maria Marchetti, Dario Ballarini  
**Type:** both · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.04919v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

**Summary.** This paper investigates strong light-matter coupling in patterned GaAs quantum well waveguides, observing the hybridization of heavy- and light-hole excitons into polaritons. By combining advanced magneto-optical measurements with a fully microscopic theoretical model, the authors demonstrate that the quantum well width can precisely engineer these coupled states. This work is crucial for understanding and controlling optical nonlinearities in solid-state quantum emitters.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant to AMO physics and quantum optics as it deals with engineered light-matter interactions in solid-state systems, providing quantitative control over exciton-photon hybridization using external fields and material design.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study aims to demonstrate and theoretically describe the physics of very strong light-matter coupling in patterned GaAs heterostructures, specifically focusing on the hybridization between heavy-hole and light-hole excitons.

**Main result.** The authors successfully demonstrated the hybridization of heavy- and light-hole excitons into a single polariton state and developed a microscopic theory that accurately describes this coupling, showing that quantum well width is a key control parameter.

**Method.** The research combines polarization-resolved white-light reflection measurements with the development of a fully microscopic theory that solves the coupled exciton-photon Hamiltonian.

**Model / system.** The system is a patterned GaAs/AlGaAs waveguide heterostructure containing multiple quantum wells. The theoretical framework uses a Luttinger Hamiltonian for the excitons coupled to the TE waveguide mode, analyzed in a 2D effective model.

**Key observables.** Polariton energy spectra (measured via reflectance), Rabi couplings ($\Omega_{hh}, \Omega_{lh}$), and the composition (photon/exciton fractions) of the resulting polariton states.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Quantum well width ($d_z$), external magnetic field ($B$ up to $9 	ext{ T}$), and the degree of light-matter coupling strength.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis often simplifies the full Hamiltonian by using an effective 2D model, neglecting certain off-diagonal Luttinger terms, and assuming TE mode dominance.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate polariton mode anticrossing vs. in-plane wave vector ($\kappa_x$) for varying magnetic fields, and show the exciton/photon composition of the upper polariton states (UP1, UP2) as a function of detuning and magnetic field.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from establishing the physical system and experimental setup to deriving the full Hamiltonian, developing a microscopic 2D model, extracting key parameters (like Rabi couplings) by fitting experimental data, and finally analyzing the energy shifts and state composition in the strong coupling regime.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The very strong light-matter coupling regime enables the non-perturbative modification of matter properties via light. Using a patterned GaAs/AlGaAs waveguide with twelve wide quantum wells, we demonstrate hybridization of heavy- and light-hole excitons within a single polariton state and show that, at finite magnetic field, the presence of the light-hole exciton suppresses coupling to the heavy-hole Rydberg excitons and unbound scattering states. We develop a fully microscopic theory that accounts for the combined effects of the magnetic field and light-matter coupling on the excitons, providing an accurate description of the experimental results beyond a perturbative coupled-oscillator framework. This identifies quantum well width as a key control parameter for engineering the light-induced hybridization of matter wave functions in polaritons, which, in turn, can play a crucial role in the optical non-linearities.

</details>

### [Bulk and surface excitons in the van der Waals magnet CrSBr: Magneto-optical studies to 55 tesla](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.05057v1)

**Summary.** This research uses magneto-optical spectroscopy on few-layer CrSBr to distinguish between bulk and surface excitons. By measuring how two distinct exciton resonances shift under applied magnetic fields, the authors provide strong evidence that surface excitons are physically and electronically different from their bulk counterparts. This work advances the understanding of exciton physics in low-dimensional magnetic semiconductors.

**Why it may be interesting.** The work provides a clear experimental demonstration of how dimensionality and local environment (dielectric screening) can lead to distinct, measurable quantum excitations (excitons) in 2D magnetic materials, which is highly relevant for designing novel quantum optical devices.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To investigate and validate the existence and properties of distinguishable bulk and surface excitons in the van der Waals magnet CrSBr using magneto-optical measurements.

**Main result.** The study confirms two distinct exciton resonances—a fundamental bulk exciton and a lower-energy surface exciton—whose differing responses to magnetic fields (redshift and diamagnetic shift) provide strong evidence for their distinct physical origins.

**Method.** Polarized optical absorption spectroscopy was performed on few-layer CrSBr samples under varying magnetic fields, up to 55 Tesla.

**Model / system.** The system is few-layer CrSBr, a 2D magnetic semiconductor. The model posits that weak inter-layer coupling confines excitons, leading to surface excitons experiencing a different local dielectric environment than the bulk-like excitons.

**Key observables.** Exciton resonance energies ($	ext{X}_{	ext{bulk}}$ vs. $	ext{X}_{	ext{surf}}$), redshift magnitude in low fields, and diamagnetic shift ($\Delta E_{	ext{dia}}$) in high fields.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Magnetic field strength (up to 55 T), layer thickness (2L to 18L), and the difference in local dielectric environment between surface and bulk layers.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The spectral differences are attributed to the distinct local dielectric environments experienced by surface versus bulk excitons.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate optical absorption spectra showing two resonances ($	ext{X}_{	ext{bulk}}$ and $	ext{X}_{	ext{surf}}$) and demonstrate how their respective energies shift differently under applied magnetic fields (low-field redshift and high-field diamagnetic shift) as a function of layer number.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically analyzes optical absorption spectra across varying layer thicknesses and magnetic fields. It first establishes the existence of two resonances, then uses low-field magneto-optics to compare redshift magnitudes, and finally uses high-field measurements to compare diamagnetic shifts, confirming the surface/bulk distinction.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

In thin layers of the 2D magnetic semiconductor CrSBr, very recent studies identified two distinct band-edge optical resonances, believed to arise from distinguishable bulk and surface excitons. This behavior reportedly originates from the highly anisotropic nature of CrSBr -- particularly in its antiferromagnetic state -- where excitons are effectively confined within individual monolayers, such that excitons in the two surface layers "see" a different local dielectric environment and have a lower resonance energy. To explore this scenario, here we investigate optical absorption properties of few-layer CrSBr in magnetic fields. In addition to the fundamental exciton resonance at ~1.36eV, we observe an absorption resonance ~20 meV lower in energy. Compared to the fundamental transition, this resonance redshifts only half as much in small magnetic fields that induce ferromagnetic order, while in high fields to 55T it exhibits a smaller diamagnetic shift. Both behaviors point to distinguishable populations of bulk and surface excitons in CrSBr.

</details>

**Summary.** This theoretical study investigates how ferroelectric substrates induce novel excitonic states in 2D TMDs. Using advanced first-principles calculations, the authors predict that the electric field gradient at domain walls splits valley-degenerate excitons by several meV. This finding establishes a pathway for engineering robust, addressable two-level quantum systems for quantum information applications.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides concrete, theoretically derived design rules for engineering quantum emitters (two-level systems) in solid-state platforms by leveraging built-in electric fields from ferroelectric substrates, which is highly relevant to quantum optics and solid-state quantum information.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study addresses fundamental challenges in controlling and addressing valley-polarized excitons in 2D TMDs, particularly concerning decoherence and energy splitting.

**Main result.** The authors predict that the combination of spatial confinement at ferroelectric domain walls and intervalley exchange interactions can split valley-degenerate excitons by $\sim 3.1$ meV, creating addressable two-level systems.

**Method.** The research employs a combination of first-principles electrostatic embedding approaches and large-scale GW plus Bethe-Salpeter equation (GW-BSE) calculations.

**Model / system.** The physical system is a monolayer TMD (e.g., MoS2) heterostructured with a ferroelectric twisted bilayer hBN substrate. The model incorporates an effective polarization potential ($V_p$) derived from the substrate's ferroelectric domains (AB/BA).

**Key observables.** Exciton types (Wannier vs. Charge-Transfer), exciton energy splitting ($\Delta E_{L-T}$), linear optical dichroism, and the spatial variation of the electrostatic potential ($V_p$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The splitting magnitude ($\sim 3.1$ meV), the width of the domain wall ($\sim 2$ nm), and the twist angle ($	heta \lesssim 1^\circ$) are critical parameters.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The calculations rely on an *ab initio* electrostatic embedding approach to model the substrate potential, and the quasi-1D approximation is used to simplify the analysis along domain walls.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the device arrangement, the spatial potential map ($V_p$) showing AB/BA domains and domain walls, and the resulting exciton density distributions for different polarizations.

**Paper structure.** The paper first establishes the physical system and the need to address exciton decoherence. It then details the computational methodology (electrostatic embedding, GW-BSE) to model the ferroelectric substrate interaction. Finally, it presents the key results regarding exciton splitting and the resulting design rules for quantum applications.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Valley-polarized excitons in two-dimensional transition metal dichalcogenides (TMDs) offer a promising platform for quantum applications, yet the addressability and decoherence of these states remain fundamental challenges. Here, by developing a first-principles electrostatic embedding approach and performing large-scale GW plus Bethe-Salpeter equation calculations, we reveal novel excitons that emerge in TMD monolayers when supported by a ferroelectric twisted bilayer hBN substrate. We predict two competing low-energy excitons whose ordering depends on the dielectric environment: optically dark, charge-transfer excitons, and quasi-one-dimensional Wannier excitons with linear optical dichroism. The spatial localization of Wannier excitons, together with intervalley exchange interactions in monolayer TMDs, splits valley-degenerate excitons by about 3~meV without external magnetic fields. Our ab initio calculations clarify the role of the interfacial twist angle and the spatial localization of fringe fields, establishing design rules for engineering long-lived two-level systems in TMD monolayers supported by ferroelectric substrates.

</details>

**Summary.** This work demonstrates that creating moiré patterns in $	ext{MoSe}_2/	ext{WS}_2$ heterobilayers dramatically extends the spin relaxation time of electrons, a crucial property for quantum technologies. By combining optical measurements with detailed theoretical modeling, the authors show that moiré confinement suppresses spin mixing, enabling long-lived spin coherence at low power levels.

**Why it may be interesting.** The ability to engineer spin coherence times in solid-state systems using moiré potentials is highly relevant to quantum information, as it offers a platform for realizing long-lived spin qubits or quantum memories.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The research aims to understand and significantly extend the spin relaxation times (SRTs) of Bloch electrons in solid-state systems by engineering moiré patterns in van der Waals heterobilayers.

**Main result.** Moiré engineering in $	ext{MoSe}_2/	ext{WS}_2$ heterobilayers suppresses spin relaxation by orders of magnitude compared to monolayers, enabling long spin coherence times necessary for quantum applications.

**Method.** The study combines advanced optical spectroscopy (TRLE, RHC) to measure spin dynamics with theoretical modeling ($\mathbf{k}\cdot\mathbf{p}$ and DFT) to explain the underlying physics.

**Model / system.** The physical system is the $	ext{MoSe}_2/	ext{WS}_2$ heterobilayer, which forms moiré supercells. The electronic structure is analyzed using $\mathbf{k}\cdot\mathbf{p}$ theory, incorporating moiré potentials derived from DFT calculations.

**Key observables.** Spin relaxation time ($	au$), valley polarization, reflection helicity contrast (RHC) spectra, and time-resolved light ellipticity modulation.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Moiré periodicity, carrier density ($n$), temperature, and optical power levels.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The long-lived TRLE signal is attributed to valley polarization/spin pumping, and the spin relaxation mechanism in the HBL is linked to the Elliott-Yafet mechanism mediated by impurity scattering.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the moiré lattice structure, doping-dependent optical spectra showing valley polarization, and time-resolved measurements demonstrating the vastly extended spin coherence time in the heterobilayer compared to the monolayer.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first characterizing the electronic structure and valley polarization in the HBL vs. ML using spectroscopy, then quantifying the spin relaxation time using TRLE, and finally providing theoretical explanations based on moiré band structure calculations.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The spin degree of freedom is a fundamental quantum mechanical attribute with implications spanning from magnetism to quantum computing. Consequently, the relaxation of spin states for extended, Bloch electrons in solids has been studied for decades as it defines many of their properties and applications. We show that moiré patterns in layered materials can extend spin relaxation times by two orders of magnitude to 1 millisecond and beyond. This is achieved by suppressing spin mixing for electrons in 2D semiconductor heterostructures, particularly in the MoSe2/WS2 system, as we elucidate both experimentally and theoretically. The extended longitudinal lifetime facilitates spin alignment over 50% using only nanowatt levels of optical power. Our findings highlight the potential of moiré engineering for future quantum sensing and information processing.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.04841"></a>
### [Chiral Transport in Metric-Affine Geometries](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04841v1)

</details>

**Summary.** This theoretical paper analyzes how anomalous chiral transport occurs in fermionic fluids when the background spacetime geometry possesses nonmetricity. Using advanced differential geometry techniques, the authors derive a constitutive relation for the axial-vector current, showing that nonmetricity induces separation effects dependent on fluid vorticity and background magnetic fields. This work bridges concepts from general relativity (metric-affine geometry) and condensed matter physics (chiral anomalies).

**Why it may be interesting.** While highly specialized in gravity/QFT, the study of anomalous transport and chiral effects in structured media (like Weyl semimetals) has analogues in condensed matter physics, making the mathematical framework relevant for understanding topological responses.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates anomalous transport phenomena in fermionic fluids that are chirally coupled to background nonmetricity within the framework of metric-affine geometry.

**Main result.** It demonstrates the existence of nonmetricity-mediated chiral separation effects, which are driven by the fluid's vorticity and the background Weyl magnetic field.

**Method.** The analysis employs formal descent techniques, transgression formulas, and the calculation of invariant four-forms ($I_Q^4$) to derive the constitutive relation for the axial-vector current.

**Model / system.** The system involves relativistic fermionic fluids coupled to a background characterized by metric-affine geometry, incorporating nonmetricity ($Q_{ab}$) and curvature ($R_{ab}$).

**Key observables.** The covariant axial-vector current ($\langle \star J_5 angle_{	ext{cov}}$), the anomaly polynomial dependence on nonmetricity, and the nonmetricity invariant $I_Q^4$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The nonmetricity tensor ($Q_{ab}$), the fluid's vorticity ($\omega$), and the Weyl magnetic field ($\mathcal{B}_Q$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The derivation often requires simplifying assumptions, such as setting the shear nonmetricity component to zero ($Q_{ab}^{ightarrow} = 0$) or analyzing specific limits of the coupling terms.

**Paper structure.** The paper proceeds by reviewing the coupling of fermions to nonmetricity, constructing a relevant invariant ($I_Q^4$), performing a descent analysis, and finally deriving the constitutive relation for the chiral current.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Anomalous transport in equilibrium fermionic fluids chirally coupled to background Weyl-type nonmetricity is studied. A formal descent analysis is carried out in which the dependence of the anomaly polynomial on the nonmetricity tensor is encoded in a Weyl invariant four-form. The constitutive relation of the axial-vector current is evaluated from the equilibrium partition function obtained using transgression techniques, showing the existence of nonmetricity-mediated chiral separation effects driven by the fluid's vorticity and the Weyl magnetic field. A second nonminimal coupling of fermionic matter to metric-affine geometries proposed in the literature is also discussed.

</details>

### [Electron-Phonon Coupling and Charge Density Wave Instabilities in W2N and Halogen-Functionalized W2N Monolayers](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04953v1)

**Summary.** This theoretical study explores how CDW instabilities and superconductivity compete in W2N monolayers. Using first-principles calculations, the authors show that both phenomena stem from the same soft-phonon-driven Electron-Phonon Coupling (EPC). Tuning the material via halogenation, strain, or doping allows researchers to tune the system between these competing quantum ground states.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on solid-state physics, the concept of competing ground states (CDW vs. SC) driven by lattice fluctuations and electron interactions is a core theme in many-body physics, relevant to understanding emergent quantum phases.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates the fundamental competition and interplay between Charge-Density-Wave (CDW) instabilities and conventional superconductivity in monolayer transition metal nitrides.

**Main result.** The low-energy physics across the W2N family is governed by soft-phonon-driven Electron-Phonon Coupling (EPC), establishing CDW order and superconductivity as competing manifestations of the same mechanism.

**Method.** The study employs first-principles Density Functional Theory (DFT) calculations, utilizing Density Functional Perturbation Theory (DFPT) to analyze phonon spectra, EPC constants, and estimate superconducting critical temperatures via the Allen-Dynes modified McMillan equation.

**Model / system.** The primary systems are monolayer W2N and its halogen-functionalized derivatives (W2NF2 and W2NCl2). The analysis compares pristine, van der Waals corrected, and strained/doped versions of these 2D materials.

**Key observables.** Phonon instabilities (softening near M/K points), phonon linewidths ($\gamma_{q
u}$), EPC constant ($\lambda$), and superconducting critical temperature ($T_c$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Compressive strain (e.g., -3%), electron doping level, and the strength of van der Waals interactions.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on DFT approximations (GGA+PBE) and the Allen-Dynes formalism for superconductivity.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate crystal structures, electronic band structures, phonon dispersions, and the evolution of soft-mode frequencies under external tuning like strain or doping.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically analyzes the structural and electronic properties of the W2N family, diagnosing CDW tendencies via phonon instabilities, quantifying EPC, and calculating $T_c$ for various doping and strain regimes to establish a unified physical picture.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The interplay between charge-density-wave (CDW) order and superconductivity is a central problem in condensed-matter physics because both phenomena often originate from the same electron-phonon coupling (EPC) mechanism. Here, we investigate the structural, electronic, vibrational, and superconducting properties of monolayer W2N and halogen-functionalized W2N (W2NF2 and W2NCl2) using first-principles calculations. Pristine W2N exhibits pronounced phonon instabilities near the M and K points driven by exceptionally strong EPC associated with softened low-frequency phonons. The coincidence between phonon softening and enhanced phonon linewidths identifies the instability as EPC-driven and indicative of a CDW tendency. Inclusion of van der Waals interactions stabilizes the lattice and yields strong-coupling superconductivity with λ = 1.00 and Tc = 13.2 K, while fluorination further weakens the soft-phonon anomaly, resulting in a moderate-coupling superconductor with λ = 0.67 and Tc = 5.3 K. In contrast, W2NCl2 exhibits a re-emergence of CDW-related phonon softening that can be continuously suppressed by compressive strain or electron doping. Under -3% compressive strain, the EPC constant decreases from λ = 1.35 to λ = 0.71, giving rise to superconductivity with Tc = 5.8 K. Across the entire W2N family, the low-energy physics is governed by softened ZA phonons near the M point, establishing a unified framework in which CDW order and superconductivity emerge as competing manifestations of the same soft-phonon-driven EPC mechanism.

</details>

### [Coulomb-mediated interactions of charge-transfer excitons in TMD lateral heterostructures](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04865v1)

**Authors:** Kabyashree Sonowal, Daniel Erkensten, Ermin Malic, Roberto Rosati

**Summary.** This work provides a microscopic theory for Coulomb interactions in CT excitons within TMD lateral heterostructures. It predicts a density-dependent energy blueshift, uniquely showing a quadratic dependence on the dipole moment. The study highlights that temperature and band offset are key external knobs for controlling this excitonic response.

**Why it may be interesting.** The detailed microscopic treatment of dipolar interactions and the identification of tunable parameters like temperature and band offset make this highly relevant for understanding excitonic dynamics in engineered quantum materials.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the mutual Coulomb-mediated interactions and resulting density-dependent energy renormalization of charge-transfer (CT) excitons.

**Main result.** The energy renormalization is predicted to be a net blueshift, which exhibits a quadratic dependence on the dipole moment for small dipoles, contrasting with linear scaling in vertical heterostructures.

**Method.** The authors employed a microscopic theoretical framework, using an equation-of-motion approach and modeling the interaction terms (dipole-dipole, bosonic, fermionic exchange) via Gaussian wavefunctions.

**Model / system.** The system is based on lateral heterostructures (LHs) of TMDs, hosting spatially separated CT excitons with large in-plane dipoles and center-of-mass quantization. The model accounts for various interaction terms derived from the Coulomb interaction.

**Key observables.** Density-dependent energy shift ($\Delta E$), which is predicted to be a net blueshift.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Dipole moment ($d$), band offset ($\Delta E_v$), temperature ($T$), and exciton density ($n$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on decoupling the center-of-mass and relative motion ($\Psi(Qx, q) = \psi(Qx)ar{\phi}(q)$) and assumes an equilibrium, unscreened environment.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the schematic interactions (d-d, bos-ex, fer-ex), the dependence of energy shifts on band offset and dipole moment, and the non-monotonic temperature dependence of the energy shift.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from establishing the physical system and interaction terms to calculating the density dependence, then analyzing the role of the band offset, followed by a detailed investigation of temperature effects on the various interaction contributions.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Lateral heterostructures of transition-metal dichalcogenides (TMDs) host spatially separated charge-transfer (CT) excitons. While analogous to interlayer excitons in vertical TMD heterostructures, these interfacial excitons possess much larger in-plane dipoles of several nanometers and an additional center-of-mass quantization. Here, we study the mutual interactions between these highly dipolar CT excitons on a microscopic footing. Accounting for the dipolar and quantum exchange interactions, we evaluate the experimentally accessible density-dependent energy renormalization and predict a net energy blueshift of a few meV for bound CT excitons. Interestingly, for small dipole moments, the energy renormalization displays a quadratic dependence with respect to the dipole moment, in contrast to the linear dependence found in vertical TMD heterostructures. We show that spatial energy offset and temperature are the key tuning knobs for controlling the density-dependent excitonic response. Overall, our results contribute to a better microscopic understanding of CT excitons and their interactions in lateral TMD heterostructures.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.04614"></a>
### [Curvature-Controlled Infrared Regularization of Crystalline Membranes](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04614v1)

**Authors:** Pablo A. Morales, Pavel Castro-Villarreal  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.04614v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **2/5** · `Frenkel-Kontorova` **1/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.04614_figures/2606.04614_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. The mean-square normal displacement W 2 ∞(L) is shown for hyperbolic curvature radii RK = 50, 100, 200 nm with graphene-inspired elastic parameters. Flat anomalous growth at small L (blue region) is cut off at the curvature scale ℓc, after which W 2 ∞(L) saturates to a finite value W 2 ∞ (red region). The dotted and dashed lines mark the satura- tion plateaus and ℓc values, respectively. The inset shows the normalized saturation deficit, the dashed line indicates the universal large-scale approach (L/ℓc)−2.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This theoretical work shows that the intrinsic negative Gaussian curvature of crystalline membranes prevents them from undergoing anomalous elastic collapse (crumpling). By analyzing the effective energy derived from integrating out in-plane phonon modes, the authors demonstrate that the membrane roughness saturates to a finite value determined by the local curvature, resolving a long-standing theoretical paradox.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on condensed matter geometry, the mathematical techniques involving effective Hamiltonians derived from integrating out high-frequency modes (phonons) and the resulting regularization mechanisms are highly relevant to understanding effective theories in quantum field theory and many-body physics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the anomalous infrared behavior of crystalline membranes, where standard elasticity theory predicts divergences and a crumpling phase when assuming flat reference geometries.

**Main result.** Negative Gaussian curvature regularizes the infrared sector, leading to a finite, curvature-controlled saturation of membrane roughness and precluding the anomalous crumpling phase.

**Method.** The analysis uses a covariant formulation of embedded elasticity, integrating out in-plane phonon modes to derive an effective quadratic Hamiltonian for the normal fluctuations.

**Model / system.** The system is crystalline membranes modeled by continuum elasticity theory, incorporating both stretching and bending energies (Helfrich-Canham type). The analysis is performed using Riemann normal coordinates.

**Key observables.** Static structure factor S(q) and the mean-squared normal displacement W2(L), which are shown to saturate to finite values.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Negative Gaussian curvature (K < 0), the crossover scale (l_c), and the local elastic moduli.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The regularization derived is a strict lower bound based on truncating the local geometric expansion, and the analysis relies on specific boundary conditions for integration by parts.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates the saturation of the mean-squared normal displacement W2(L) for hyperbolic curvature radii, showing a transition from anomalous flat growth to a finite plateau at the curvature scale l_c.

**Paper structure.** The paper develops the elastic energy functional, analyzes the contribution of tangential vs. normal deformations to the phonon integral, and finally derives the effective Hamiltonian by integrating out in-plane phonons to determine the long-wavelength response.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We show that negative Gaussian curvature regularizes the infrared sector of crystalline membranes. In a covariant formulation of embedded elasticity, the Green strain contains a symmetry-required linear coupling between the normal phonon and extrinsic curvature. Integrating out the in-plane phonons converts this coupling into a finite quadratic contribution to the inverse flexural response. Anomalous roughening is thereby replaced by curvature-controlled saturation, and the mechanism survives on minimal hyperbolic patches. Hyperbolic geometry preempts anomalous elasticity before the flat infrared regime is reached, implying the absence of a crumpling phase. The same Gaussian-order coupling admits sound propagation in the infrared.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.04481"></a>
### [When Screening Current Controls Ferroelectric Switching: From Field-Limited to Current-Limited Regimes under an SPM Tip](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04481v1)

**Authors:** Denis Alikin, Anastasya Meng, Michael Kosobokov, Semen Melnikov, Violetta Safina  
**Type:** both · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.04481v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<sub>Figure 3. Dynamics of the charge injection into (a)-(c) SiO2 and (d)-(i) LiNbO3 with (d)-(f) downward and (g)-(i) upward direction of the polarization. (b), (e), (h) Dependences of the injected charge on the pulse duration (at 100 V amplitude) and (c), (f), (i) pulse amplitude (at 10 s duration).</sub>

<img src="2606.04481_figures/2606.04481_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 4. Local switching at the domain wall and far from the wall: (a) the domain structure of the sample: the red point is the point of application of an electric field for local switching on the domain wall, the cyan point is that for local switching on the domain wall; formed domains: (b) at the domain wall, (c) far from the domain wall; mechanisms of local switching: (d) voltage-limited regime, (e) current-limited regime; voltage dependencies of (f)-(h) domain radius and (i-k) domain area for pulse durations of: (f, i) 100 ms, (g, j) 1 s, (h, k) 10 s.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2606.04481_figures/2606.04481_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 5. (a) Schematic illustration of used methodology addition of an external resistance; (b) Dependence of domain area from RC circuit resistance.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This work argues that ferroelectric switching under an SPM tip is not purely field-driven but is instead limited by the rate at which screening charge can be supplied via the tip-sample junction. By modeling the system using charge transport equations, the authors establish that the process operates in a current-limited regime, providing a new, practical handle for controlling nanoscale domain engineering.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on ferroelectrics, the core concept of identifying a hidden, rate-limiting transport mechanism (current control) that overrides a seemingly intrinsic material property (field threshold) is highly relevant to understanding non-equilibrium dynamics in complex condensed matter systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper reinterprets ferroelectric switching under an SPM tip, arguing that the process is fundamentally controlled by the rate of screening charge supply rather than solely by domain-wall kinetics.

**Main result.** Switching is governed by a screening-current-limited mechanism, where the domain size and switching dynamics are dictated by Space-Charge-Limited Current (SCLC) transport into the ferroelectric material.

**Method.** The authors combine local switching experiments (PFM/SSPFM) with detailed finite-element simulations solving coupled time-domain equations for charge transport and domain evolution.

**Model / system.** The system is a ferroelectric material (e.g., LiNbO3) probed by an SPM tip. The model incorporates electric field balance, domain wall motion kinetics, and charge transport governed by drift-diffusion equations under external current injection.

**Key observables.** Domain radius and area as functions of voltage pulse amplitude and duration; apparent coercive voltage; injected charge density mapped via KPFM.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Screening current, space-charge-limited current (SCLC) exponents (e.g., $m=2$ or $m=3/2$), and the external resistance ($R_{ex}$) used to control injection current.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis contrasts field-limited (domain-wall kinetics) vs. current-limited (charge injection) regimes, noting that the latter dominates the observed scaling laws.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate domain radius/area scaling with voltage/time, surface potential maps, and simulation results showing the time evolution of domain size under different charge injection assumptions.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first establishing the limitations of purely field-driven models, then introducing the screening current concept, developing coupled simulation frameworks (drift-diffusion + domain evolution), and finally demonstrating that SCLC transport dictates the observed scaling laws.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Tip-induced switching in ferroelectrics is commonly framed as a field-driven process controlled by domain-wall kinetics. Here we argue for a more universal viewpoint: under a scanning probe, polarization reversal is fundamentally constrained by how fast screening charge can be supplied and redistributed. By combining local switching experiments with finite-element simulations, we identify a screening-current-limited mechanism in which charge injection and its space-charge-limited relaxation set the pace of switching and the scaling of domain growth. This framework naturally explains regime changes and the apparent breakdown of intrinsic domain-wall laws often inferred in the nanoscale piezoelectric hysteresis measurements. Beyond a reinterpretation of tip switching, the results position screening currents as a hidden control parameter of ferroelectric reversal across materials, turning current regulation into a practical handle for deterministic nanoscale domain engineering.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.04097"></a>
### [Probing pairing symmetries through quasiparticle interference in chiral Bloch bands](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04097v1)

Impurity-induced change in δASC αβ(r, ω) in real space and their corresponding QPI patterns (insets) for achiral SC in (i) top-top and (ii) bottom-bottom combination; chiral SC in (iii) top-top and (iv) bottom-bottom combination at q ̸= 0. Panel (v) shows the momentum-space density map of the allowed scattering processes, determined by the Fermi surface geometry (inset), which sets the accessible scattering wavevectors visible in the QPI patterns of panels (i)–(iv).</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2606.04097_figures/2606.04097_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4. Separating normal and anomalous contribu- tions. Normal and anomalous contributions to δA(r, ω), de- noted as I and II, respectively, and corresponding to the first and second term in Eq. (17). Panel (i) depicts the contribu- tions for the tt configuration corresponding to both achiral and chiral types of pairing. Panel (ii) depicts the same, but for the bb configuration. The corresponding QPI patterns are shown in the insets. While both contributions are of the same order in the tt case (allowing for a distinction between the two pairings), the anomalous one is much smaller than the normal one in the bb configuration.</sub>

</details>

<img src="2606.04097_figures/2606.04097_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 5. Normal state with trigonal warping.
